## Page 8

Foreword
By Jacco van der K ooij, Founder, W inning by D esign
In every era of business, there comes a moment when the playbook must be
rewritten. Today is that moment. For the past decade, RevOps has been the
quiet engine behind G T M  success—aligning revenue teams, streamlining
processes, and turning data into action. It has optimized eﬃciency, improved
conversion rates, and integrated systems. But eﬃciency alone is no longer
enough. W hat got us here won’t get us through the next decade. AI is
reshaping the way we sell, market, and grow. But the question isn’t what AI
will change. It’s what will stay the same. W ill RevOps drive this transformation
or be left reacting to it?
RevOps Is The Backbone of AI-powered GTM
Historically, G T M  has been measured by volume with how many emails, calls,
and meetings were set, leading to how much revenue was acquired, regardless
of the cost. But volume alone is no longer a strategy. Nor is cost reduction.
And yet, this is where AI today is largely focused—doing more, doing it faster,
and doing it cheaper.
But the real opportunity for AI isn’t just about scaling activity or cutting
costs. It’s about creating recurring revenue. And recurring revenue is the direct
result of delivering recurring impact. Achieving recurring impact isn’t just
about acquiring the right deals, delivering initial value, or driving expansion in
isolation. It requires orchestration across the entire customer journey, ensuring
that every stage, from acquisition to retention, feeds into the next.

---

## Page 9

is is where AI can play a transformational role. Historically, building a
closed-loop system—where happy customers drive sustainable growth—has
been diﬃcult. G T M  motions have remained fragmented, making true
alignment nearly impossible. AI has the power to change this. e role of AI
isn’t just to automate tasks. It’s to orchestrate the entire system seamlessly,
predictably, and at scale.
is is why RevOps is in a deﬁning position. U nlike siloed teams chasing
individual KP Is, RevOps has a holistic, end-to-end view. It spans Sales,
M arketing, Customer Success, Finance, and beyond. It understands how the
business truly operates.
at perspective is what separates AI that accelerates bad processes from AI
that optimizes GT M  for durable, scalable growth.
The Next Era of RevOps
AI will change many things in G T M , but the future isn’t about what AI will
change. It’s about what it won’t. e real power of AI lies in understanding
what will stay the same. is isn’t a new concept. Jeﬀ Bezos built Amazon
around what he believed would never change—lower prices, faster delivery,
and broader selection. e same principle applies to AI-driven G T M , and they
present us with three constants:
•H um an behavior rem ains constant: People will always want to meet in
person, have a coﬀee, and build trust. Humans are inherently lazy. AI
won’t just assist. It will take over the heavy lifting, automating tasks so
people don’t have to.
Humans are sloppy and forgetful. AI will ensure accuracy, catch
mistakes, double-check details, and manage execution. Humans are
drawn to creativity. AI can enhance, but creativity will likely remain a
human edge.
•Process will always com e before System s: AI is a powerful enabler. It
reﬁnes, accelerates, and scales execution. But it follows a process; it

---

## Page 10

doesn’t create one. e most successful companies will ﬁrst build
structured, scalable GT M  processes—designed through human creativity
and strategic thinking.
AI then operates within this framework, driving ﬂawless execution and
continuously recalculating the best route to hypergrowth, just like a
navigation system constantly adjusts to ﬁnd the fastest way home.
•Custom er success will rem ain the foundation of durable growth: AI-
powered RevOps must be designed to optimize customer outcomes or
impact, not just pipeline eﬃciency. Too many companies still treat
revenue as the goal rather than the natural result of their customers
achieving success, leading them to deploy AI with the wrong focus.
But what if we used AI to drive customer success instead? W hen AI is
deployed to enhance impact rather than just eﬃciency, it transforms
growth into something sustainable, scalable, and customer-driven.
Because in the end, true, lasting growth happens when customer impact
drives revenue. Not the other way around.
RevOps: Deﬁning the AI-Powered Future
ese constants aren’t just ideas. ey’re real, unshakable, and built for action.
ey don’t just help RevOps adapt to AI. ey put RevOps in the driver’s seat,
shaping how AI will redeﬁne GT M  for the next decade.
e companies that master RevOps as the operating system for AI-powered
G T M  won’t just survive this transformation; they’ll deﬁne it. If you’re reading
this, you’re either driven by RevOps or leading a G T M  team. Either way, we’re
all on the same mission: charting the future of revenue growth. at makes
you a pioneer. And as a pioneer, your greatest achievements aren’t in the
rearview. ey’re on the road ahead.
e future of GT M  is being written. And you’re the one holding the pen.
Jacco van der Kooij
Founder, W inning by Design

---

## Page 11

Preface
RevOps is often misunderstood. Not just in what it does, but even in how
well-known it is. In fact, most people have never even heard of it.
Take a look at this chart from G oogle Trends. It shows you how relatively
popular a search term is compared to another. 0 means relatively not popular,
and 100 is very popular.
is chart compares D eveloper Operations (D evOps) with RevOps. G uess
which line is RevOps? M y wishful thinking would say the top line, but no. It’s
the bottom line. e gap is massive.
Let’s take a look at another one. W hat do you think is the bottom line?

---

## Page 12

It’s D evOps, and the top line is Nike. Nike is a commercial brand that
probably most people on the planet have heard about. D evOps is insigniﬁcant
compared to that. It’s very niche.
is shows how tiny RevOps truly is.
You might have heard of Sales Operations (SalesOps) which is a support
function for Sales. Or M arketing Operations (M arkOps) which is a support
function for M arketing.
RevOps is special compared to those similar-sounding functions. Namely,
it has a holistic focus on revenue-generating teams.
So, why am I telling you all this? Because I am passionate about RevOps,
but sadly, it’s still misunderstood. Often, people ask me what they can read to
better understand what RevOps is and I struggle to point them to a good
resource. At the same time, I’m a problem solver. So, partly out of frustration,
and partly out of the need to show the power of RevOps, I started to work on
this book in August 2023.
And it’s indeed powerful. RevOps is a framework that helps businesses
reach unprecedented levels of operational eﬃciency. Companies that deploy
RevOps see:
•10 to 20% increase in sales productivity.1
•30% less GT M  expenses.2
•53% of companies see an improved net dollar retention.3
•Aligned companies have 36% more revenue and are 28% more
proﬁtable.4
•S&P 500 companies with RevOps achieve 176% higher EBIT D A
compared to their peers.5
is is what RevOps can help you achieve, no matter what industry you
are in.
For businesses with a recurring revenue model, RevOps is especially
important. And there is no other industry that leverages the recurring revenue

---

## Page 13

model like Software-as-a-Service (SaaS). e recurring revenue model promises
huge proﬁts. It’s why RevOps is especially beneﬁcial for these companies.
In this book, you’ll see that some chapters speciﬁcally focus on SaaS. D oes
this mean you should skip a chapter if you’re not in SaaS? You can, but there’s
still enough value in learning about those principles. If you apply RevOps
principles for all other teams that impact revenue, then you’ll have an
incredible impact on your bottom line.
e majority of the book is written to be applicable across industries like
healthcare, manufacturing, and retail. ere’s no excuse to ignore RevOps; it
gives you a competitive advantage. is book will help you understand it on a
fundamental level. At the same time, it’s full of examples and actionable
instructions with the same frameworks and methods that the world’s fastest-
growing businesses use. To make this journey easier for you, I’ve included
templates, four research papers (conducted speciﬁcally for this book), and
additional resources in the digital resource section. You can ﬁnd them at this
link:
https://revenuewizards.com/revops-book.
W hile I was writing this book, the force of a lifetime erupted. You already
know from Jacco’s foreword what it is: AI. M ost of my knowledge of RevOps
was formed before AI reached mainstream appeal. e thing with AI is that it
completely disrupts how businesses operate. Everything I knew, and wanted to
share in this book, was now challenged by AI. ere is no way I could write
this book without highlighting the impact of AI on RevOps and the wider
ﬁeld of Go-To-M arket (G T M ) function.
e good news is that you still need a strong foundation to successfully use
AI. RevOps is all about building that foundation. AI provides RevOps more
momentum. Not only does it make RevOps more eﬃcient, but RevOps is also
a great facilitator for embedding AI tools in the business because no other
function understands the complete G T M  function as well as RevOps.

---

## Page 14

erefore, all chapters of the books are AI-proof, with the last chapter fully
dedicated to AI.
Introducing the RevOps Pendulum
Figure 0.1 A Newton’s Pendulum
Have you ever seen this mesmerizing and almost magical object in an
oﬃce? It’s called Newton’s Pendulum. is book uses it as a symbol to explain
the four main pillars of RevOps and how alignment, balance, and momentum
are all essential for business success.
Each ball represents each of the main pillars of RevOps: Enablement, D ata
and Insights, Systems, and Process. W hen they are perfectly aligned, energy
ﬂows smoothly, just like in a well-run business. But if one ball is oﬀ, energy is

---

## Page 15

lost. Just like how misaligned teams can slow down progress and drain
resources.
RevOps is the force that keeps everything in sync. It ensures energy
moves eﬃciently toward shared goals, helping businesses grow.
Figure 0.2: RevOps Aligns the Pillars
Now, AI is adding even more power to this motion. It reduces friction and
speeds up progress, pushing businesses to new heights.
Figure 0.3: AI Accelerates M om entum
One of the pendulum’s most interesting qualities is its adaptability. It can
still function with fewer balls as long as at least two remain. In business, this
represents the ability to adjust to challenges and stay on track.
ink of the movement of the balls as your revenue and customer
experience. In theory, the motion could go on forever, generating continuous

---

## Page 16

success. But in reality, friction such as competition, market changes, and
ineﬃciencies gradually slows it down. If there were no competition,
momentum would never stop. But because businesses must compete, staying
ahead requires constant innovation, smart decisions, and an occasional push to
keep moving forward.
is book explores how RevOps keeps the pendulum in motion. Part 1
introduces its foundations, Part 2 dives into its four pillars, while Part 3
examines its relationship with ﬁnance and the transformative power of AI.
By the end, you will have a fundamental understanding of RevOps as a
business growth driver. If you’re new to RevOps, you will have a path on how
to deploy it in a business. If you already have a RevOps function, then this
book can help you to strengthen existing principles and maybe consider a new
angle to an existing problem.
Above all, this book should contribute to creating a common
understanding of what RevOps is and how it can help your business. Should at
least a few of you ﬁnd the words between these pages helpful, then I have done
my job.
W ith warm regards from Amsterdam,
H aris Odobasic

## Page 21

PART 1
Establishing the Swing
Part 1 sets the swing in motion and lays the foundation of RevOps through
four key chapters: a clear deﬁnition of what RevOps is, its organizational
structure, the path from strategy to execution, and the role of eﬀective change
management. ese elements work together to build momentum—setting the
stage for aligned, scalable growth.

---

## Page 22

CHAPTER 1
SETTING THE PENDU LU M IN
MOTION
“Once you stop learning, you start dying.”
Albert Einstein
You picked up this book to learn more about RevOps. To understand what it
truly means, let’s rewind to 2017.
Back then, companies had various revenue-generating teams: Sales,
M arketing, and Customer Success. As businesses grew, these departments
became supported by their own dedicated operations teams. W hile they all
worked toward the same goal of generating revenue, they became increasingly
siloed. Of course, some coordination happened between them, but it was often
minimal.
D id it work?
Yes! is setup created billions, if not trillions, of dollars in revenue
globally. In fact, it continues to work well to this day.
But the question is: will it continue to work in the future?
If a business has a lot of cash available to invest, it can. e traditional
approach for many companies, especially in tech, is to put more manpower
into a problem to solve it. M ore money for more people equals more growth.
In addition, money is easily spent on tooling to facilitate growth. is is fueled
by the expectation that future returns will more than cover these costs.

---

## Page 23

is has led to high valuations and the rise of unicorn startups (a privately
held company with a market evaluation of a billion or more). However, when
companies focus heavily on growth, they become increasingly ineﬃcient, with
siloed teams, weak alignment between departments, and overspending on
isolated technology platforms.
en came the “Big Correction” in 2020.
It started with the COVID -19 pandemic and impacted not only the
technology sector but the whole world. Supply chain disruption, inﬂation, and
spikes in interest rates made cheap capital a thing of the past. Investors became
reluctant to fund cash-burning businesses with longer payback periods; they
simply couldn’t tolerate the risk.
M eanwhile, most businesses were missing their targets. In the tech
industry, the bubble burst as growth projections failed to align with economic
reality. e result was massive layoﬀs across tech and other sectors.
e impact was swift. Layoﬀs were not the only cost-cutting measure.
M any companies introduced hiring freezes and more stringent budget controls.
Every new investment was carefully evaluated, and existing expenses were cut,
when appropriate. Companies used to buy every tool possible (whether needed
or not) as long as it promised a Return on Investment (ROI). ose tools had
to go during the Big Correction. In SaaS, that led to a big wave of revenue
churn, when customers stop using a recurring product.
At the time of writing in 2025, the Big Correction is still unfolding.
But that’s not the only challenge today. ere’s more competition than ever
in every domain, and it has never been easier to start a business. D ue to low-
code, no-code, more eﬃcient D evOps, and especially AI, the barrier to entry is
very low. e time to build and market a product has shrunk signiﬁcantly.
Companies no longer need a massive team; a small team of 10 can build the
same product that required 50 people a few years ago.
Businesses have harder access to investments, forcing them to rethink their
business model. All of this puts more pressure on companies to be more
eﬃcient.
is is where RevOps comes in.

---

## Page 24

RevOps oﬀers a way to increase eﬃciency and reduce headcount by
aligning all revenue-generating teams. Just as D evOps emerged in the late
2000s to bridge the gap between Software and IT  teams, RevOps addresses the
misalignment between Sales, Marketing, and Customer Success.
RevOps is still a young discipline. Its earliest mention traces back to 2017.
By contrast, SalesOps has been around since the 1970s, pioneered by Xerox to
handle behind-the-scenes tasks like sales planning, compensation, and
forecasting. Patrick K elly described the job of SalesOps as: “All the nasty
number of things that you don’t w ant to do but need to do to make a great sales
force.”6
Today in 2025, SalesOps remain relevant, but it’s no longer enough. It’s a
race for eﬃciencies that lead to more revenue, less G T M  expense, and more
proﬁtability.
RevOps is best prepared for the new challenges of our AI-driven
generation.
Today, companies need RevOps to compete in an environment where
eﬃciency, proﬁtability, and alignment are paramount.
So, how is RevOps changing the game? Let’s ﬁnd out.
RevOps Gains Global Mom entum
G lobally, there are around 17,000 RevOps professionals on LinkedIn.*

around 3,000 RevOps professionals, 1,000 of whom are in the UK.
It’s no surprise that the majority are working for IT  companies, most
prominently SaaS companies. It’s fascinating to see that RevOps is getting
embraced by the healthcare industry, but it’s not too surprising since the
healthcare industry is deﬁned by innovation.

e above might indicate that RevOps is more common in smaller
companies, but this is relative, as we can see in the table below.
RevOps is more common in larger enterprises. Among the biggest of them
—the S&P 500—26.84% have adopted RevOps.7 is reinforces its
popularity in enterprise companies, a trend we’ll explore further in this book.
However, this doesn’t mean RevOps isn’t widely used by Small and M edium-
sized Enterprises (SM Es). ere are simply far more SM Es than enterprises,
which aﬀects the overall numbers. In certain industries, like SaaS, RevOps is
especially prevalent.

might not have RevOps in their title. For example, an enablement specialist or
Customer Relationship M anagement (CRM ) admin would not be counted
directly.
W hat we can say so far is that RevOps is still in its infancy compared to
other operation functions. ere are 32 times more other operations
professionals than those in RevOps! W hile it’s a huge diﬀerence, let’s not forget
that it’s still relatively young.

D evOps did for tech, with hundreds of thousands of people working in the
ﬁeld.
And we need more people in RevOps to break down the silos.
Breaking Down the Silos
Almost every problem in business can be traced back to communication and
silos:
•W e did not hear the customer feedback…
•How could [insert any department] not know this?
•is is the process; why are they not following it?
•W hy are they doing this? at is not helping with our goals.
•at new [feature, campaign, program] is not aligned with what we hear
from [data, clients, market].

---

## Page 29

•W e did not see the data for that…
•W e don’t have access to that…
e reality is that we’re quite bad at communication. M ost teams are siloed
and struggle with it because they each have their own goals.
According to an article from the Harvard Business Review, 90% of
M arketing and Sales professionals report misalignment in terms of strategy,
process, culture, and content.8 According to ID C M arket Research,
misalignment in data accounts for 20 to 30% loss in revenue.9
is is a lot, but I’m not surprised. e default organizational design is to
create silos. W e can’t be surprised if they are ineﬀective.
e primary goal of RevOps is to create alignment, integration, and
collaboration between the various departments. is makes the whole
organization more eﬃcient. It increases revenue, reduces costs, and increases
proﬁtability.
A RevOps Soup with Stones
By Dr. Lukas M ohr, Revenue Operations Lead at M ARKT -PILOT
I like to describe RevOps using the Stone Soup story. It’s a folk tale about
resourcefulness and collaboration.
Hungry travelers arrive in a village, claiming they can make soup from just a
stone, intriguing skeptical villagers. As the travelers begin boiling water with
a stone, they spark the interest of the local villagers. Leveraging this interest,
they suggest that the soup would improve with ingredients, prompting
villagers to contribute vegetables, meat, and spices. In the end, everyone
shares a delicious meal, realizing the power of collaboration. RevOps is like
making soup from stones: RevOps itself is meaningless, but it provides the
foundation and aligns all revenue-contributing departments —essentially
the “ingredients”—so that a GT M  organization can function as a whole.

---

## Page 30

RevOps U nraveled: W hat’s the Pivot?
e ﬁrst time I heard about RevOps was in 2018.
I understood it as a function that collaborates closely with Sales,
M arketing, and Customer Success—or the teams focused on generating
revenue. Since they share a uniﬁed support function, calling it RevOps makes
perfect sense.
At that time, I was working in SalesOps for a sustainability software
company. M y position was part of SalesOps, but I was actually working with
other G T M  teams: coordinating with M arketing, building out the billing
process with Finance, working with Legal on contract management, and
improving the retention process with Customer Success. at sounds quite
RevOps-eque to me.
I explained to my boss what RevOps is and suggested making the shift
from SalesOps to RevOps. He was supportive of the transition and we adopted
RevOps for the company.
However, I quickly noticed that most people didn’t know what RevOps
was. If they knew the term, they normally had diﬀerent interpretations of its
deﬁnition. It was a problem I noticed outside of the company itself.
e lack of a shared deﬁnition creates confusion in the best case and can
kill a movement in the worst. A deﬁnition is important for the adoption and
success of a concept. It’s not helpful when people have a diﬀerent
understanding of something.
RevOps suﬀers from this.
ere are diﬀerent interpretations because there is no dictionary deﬁnition
of it. People end up googling what RevOps is and comparing various
deﬁnitions. en, they adopt the one that resonates most with them or the one
that seems easiest to deploy. e consequence is that businesses adopt it with
various degrees of success.
is is NOT RevOps is is RevOps
W orks only for Sales W ork with all G T M  teams and other
business functions

---

## Page 31

Focuses only on pipeline management Optimizes the entire revenue funnel
(M arketing, Sales, Customer Success)
Is only doing CRM  admin D oes strategic work and some admin
Focus onsupporting each department Focus on aligning departments
Only reacts to problems Proactively drives eﬃciency and growth
M easures success by operational tasks
completed
M easures success by revenue impact and
eﬃciency improvements
W orks independently from leadership Partners with leadership to align strategy
In some businesses, RevOps is perceived as a system admin and ends up just
managing tools. In other businesses, RevOps works with Sales and M arketing
on operational tasks. en, there are businesses where RevOps partners with all
revenue leaders on strategic and operational projects.
If that’s the case, how do we deﬁne it?
An Objective Deﬁnition
In January 2024, an academic deﬁnition of RevOps was published by Tariq
Hassan Ahmad for his PhD  dissertation:
“Revenue Operations (RevOps) is an integrative device that drives strategic and
operational alignment, integration, and collaboration across go-to-market (G T M )
functions using data & insights, processes, systems, and enablement to deliver the
desired business impact and customer experience.”10
I don’t know about you, but this sounds a bit too academic. On one hand,
the objective of RevOps is to create business impact. is can mean more
revenue, less G T M  costs, and more proﬁts. On the other hand, it’s about
improving the customer experience by having more satisﬁed customers. I like
to summarize it as:
RevOps is a strategic business partner for G T M  T eams.
But to better understand it, let’s return to Tarik’s deﬁnition and deﬁne each
element on its own.

---

## Page 32

Integrative Device
In the 1960s, Harvard professors Lawrence and Lorsch’s research focused on
how functions that needed to be highly diﬀerentiated could be highly
integrated.11 ey found, from their research, the existence of integrative
devices in the form of teams or departments that could assist in integrating
highly diﬀerentiated functions. is is exactly what RevOps is trying to do:
integrate the highly diﬀerentiated functions across the revenue function like
Sales, Marketing, and Customer Success.
Strategic and Operational
RevOps encompasses both strategic and operational tasks. Each plays a distinct
role in driving revenue growth.
Strategic: Strategic RevOps deﬁnes the “what” and “why.” It sets the vision
and direction. It focuses on the big picture and designs a complete customer
journey from initial intent to renewal. It involves analyzing the entire revenue
funnel and using data-driven insights to make decisions that improve customer
experiences and overall business outcomes. is is where long-term planning
and alignment with organizational goals come into play.
Operational: Operational RevOps focuses on the “how.” It’s all about
translating strategy into action. is involves implementing processes, enabling
revenue teams, and continuously improving workﬂows. It also includes
managing the day-to-day tasks such as CRM  administration, user support,
reporting, and other systems that keep the revenue engine running eﬃciently.
Together, they create a cohesive approach to driving revenue.
Alignm ent, Integration, and Collaboration
Alignment is about ﬁnding a common language for all G T M  functions. is
includes common goals, objectives, and targets.
Integration involves connecting the G T M  function both in processes and
technology. RevOps looks for opportunities to unify the business and drive

---

## Page 33

eﬃciencies.
Collaboration means creating an environment where various G T M
functions collaborate in a safe and productive manner. It’s about removing
barriers that stand between teams with the goal to unlock new revenue
opportunities.
Across GTM Functions
e core G T M  functions include M arketing, Sales and Customer Support, but
it can ﬁlter down into any function that impacts revenue. is can include
Product, Legal, Operations, or Finance.
Data &  Insights, Processes, System s, and
Enablem ent
ese are the four pillars of RevOps and form the foundation of its success.
e majority of our day-to-day work in RevOps is structured around them.
ey work in a continuum together and complement each other.
Let’s break them down:
D ata and Insights It is concerned with having reliable data within the G T M
function. e data is then used to generate insights that
support strategic and operational decision-making for the
business
Processes It is about how your business executes its G T M  strategy and
the day-to-day operations of each G T M  function. Processes
should support the core philosophy of alignment, integration,
and collaboration whenever possible.
Systems It is about using technology in an eﬃcient and eﬀective way to
support the execution of the strategy and its processes.

---

## Page 34

Enablement It is about empowering the G T M  function to succeed in its
role. It involves onboarding, continuous training, and
providing them with consistent content.
At the end of each chapter, you’ll notice a section called “Socratic
Reﬂections.” It’s inspired by the ancient G reek philosopher Socrates and his
famous “Socratic M ethod.” Socrates was known for exploring ideas through
thought-provoking questions rather than providing direct answers to
encourage critical thinking and self-discovery. ese reﬂections are designed to
help you pause, consider the key themes from each chapter, challenge your
assumptions, and uncover your own insights.
ey’re not about prescribing what to do but rather about encouraging you
to deeply consider how the insights from each chapter apply to your speciﬁc
business context.
Let’s get started.
Socratic Reﬂections
•D oes everyone in your company have the same understanding of
RevOps?
•RevOps focuses on building alignment, collaboration, and integration.
Of these three, is there one area you might want to prioritize?
•W hich department is most siloed in your business? W hy is that the case?
Can you think of one initiative that could make the department more
integrated?

---

## Page 35

CHAPTER 2
THE PIVOT POINT –
STRU CTU RING YOU R
REVOPS TEAM
“e best structure will not guarantee results and performance. But the wrong
structure is a guarantee of failure.”
Peter Drucker, Austrian-A merican M anagement Consultant
RevOps sounds like a great idea, right? But how do you actually make it a
reality?
To make it work, your board and leadership must have the same
understanding of RevOps. e RevOps team needs to be empowered. If that’s
not the case, then you’ll fall into the trap of being a “fake” RevOps function
that’s not living up to its potential.
It’s a waste of resources and talent.
You can quickly spot a fake RevOps function with these signals:
•Has a bias towards a speciﬁc department (e.g., Sales or M arketing).
•Is perceived as the admin.
•Is excluded from strategic decisions.
•e stakeholders don’t understand what RevOps does.
•Has limited access to some data.

---

## Page 36

•Has no “seat” at the table (a.k.a., can’t make real, impactful decisions).
W hen this happens you’re:
•U nable to prove impact on revenue, cost reduction, or proﬁts.
•Lacking productivity gains.
•Perceived as a cost center that doesn’t add value.
To prevent a fake RevOps function, I recommend the leadership team
complete a foundational RevOps workshop before implementation. is will
help the entire organization have a common understanding of RevOps and
how it ﬁts into the business strategy.
A t Revenue W izards, my G T M  & RevOps consulting company, we
regularly perform these stakeholder alignment workshops. It usually requires 2
to 3 hours for all functional leaders impacted through RevOps (e.g., CM O,
CEO, CFO, and CRO) to be together in one room. In the workshop, we build
on leadership’s existing knowledge of RevOps and guide them to uncover its
meaning on their own. I believe people learn best by discovering the truth
themselves rather than being told. is time commitment can save hundreds of
thousands of sunk costs down the line.
W hether you do this internally or have someone else facilitate it, the
importance of this meeting shouldn’t be overlooked. If a leadership team has a
common understanding of RevOps, then it dramatically increases its success.
W ith that in mind, let’s explore how to start a RevOps department.
Starting a RevOps Departm ent From  Scratch
Tarik breaks down RevOps into three main deployment models: the Service
Provider, the Enforcer, and the Partner.
Your pick depends on how mature your business is and where you’re in
your growth journey. Let’s take a closer look at these models to help you decide
which approach ﬁts best.
First up: the Service Provider.

---

## Page 37

Figure 2.4: e three RevOps D eployment M odels
The Service Provider
In the Service Provider deployment model, your M arketing or Sales operation
teams take the lead in developing RevOps. ese teams are typically very
focused on operations due to their origin. Tarik points out that these teams
frequently ﬁnd themselves “ﬁreﬁghting,” or reacting to issues as they arise
rather than proactively strategizing.
Hence, RevOps acts as a service provider: various departments assign tasks
to RevOps, but RevOps doesn’t provide strategic insights in return. ey
execute work given by others but may not inﬂuence the overall strategy or oﬀer
insights that could drive the business forward.
is is often a rebrand of SalesOps or M arkOps as RevOps. However, this
approach tends to lack a strategic focus. e operational bias makes it
challenging to make RevOps strategic. ough, it’s not impossible.
A  key factor in this dynamic is that leadership often doesn’t fully
understand the role and potential of RevOps. W ithout a clear understanding
of how RevOps can strategically beneﬁt the business, leadership may limit the
team to operational tasks. is lack of insight can keep RevOps in a reactive
role, preventing it from driving proactive growth and eﬃciency.

---

## Page 38

The Enforcer
e Enforcer model is mainly seen in larger organizations, typically those with
500 or more employees. RevOps is enforced from the top as a corporate
function and often reports to C-Level executives.
is approach is chosen when individual departments (such as M arkOps,
SalesOps, and others) are already well-established and deeply embedded within
the company’s processes. Implementing drastic changes in such an
environment could negatively aﬀect the business due to the complexity and
interdependence of existing systems. erefore, any modiﬁcations must be
carefully considered and orchestrated from a high level.
A s the Enforcer, RevOps holds a unique position of seniority and
authority, allowing it to guide and direct other operational units eﬀectively.
Because RevOps is the only function that understands the full revenue
operations cycle across the organization, it can enforce alignment and drive
strategic projects that span multiple departments. is top-down approach
ensures consistency and cohesion in executing revenue-generating strategies
without disrupting established processes.
is structure is best suited for large companies where building RevOps
from scratch is not feasible.
The Partner
In the Partner deployment model, RevOps operates at a peer level with other
departments such as Sales, M arketing, and Customer Success. is structure
empowers RevOps to collaborate closely with all functions and have an equal
seat at the decision-making table. By being free from departmental biases,
RevOps serves as an objective partner that bridges gaps between teams.
In this model, RevOps often reports directly to an objective leader like the
CEO, CRO, or CFO. is reporting structure ensures that RevOps maintains
a holistic view of the organization’s revenue-generating processes and can
inﬂuence strategic decisions eﬀectively.

---

## Page 39

e Partner model is considered the most successful deployment model of
RevOps and is eﬀective in both small and large organizations. It allows RevOps
to function as a true strategic business partner to all G T M  teams, enhancing
their capabilities and ensuring that strategies are aligned with overall business
objectives.
Personally, I relate most to the Partner model. It exempliﬁes how RevOps
can elevate its role beyond operational tasks to become integral to strategic
planning and execution.
How to Choose W hich One to Im plem ent
e right deployment model for your business will depend on the stage of your
company, along with other important factors such as your goals, budgets, or
experience.
If possible, I recommend avoiding the Service Provider model. It takes time
for RevOps to become really strategic when you have to continuously ﬁght
against the perception of being an admin who is only ﬁreﬁghting. But if you’re
stuck in the Service Provider model, here are a few tips to become more
strategic:
•A lways link the outcomes of operational activities to strategic objectives
(more in Chapter 3).
•Have an executive sponsor who supports RevOps (more in Chapter 4).
•Balance longer projects (i.e., implementation of a new tool) with quick
wins (more in Chapter 3).
Determining Reporting Lines
One important decision you’ll need to make when creating this department is
your reporting lines. is will directly impact how eﬀective your RevOps team
will be in your organization.
Reporting lines (or who reports to whom) can strengthen or weaken how
well RevOps is executed.

---

## Page 40

First, let’s start where RevOps should never report to.
W ho RevOps Shouldn’t Report To
RevOps should never report to M arketing, Sales, or Customer Success.
Reporting to those is a logical contradiction to its deﬁnition.
In the cases where RevOps reports directly to one functional leader, it
creates a bias by design to that revenue leader. For instance, if RevOps reports
to the Head of M arketing, then RevOps would have a strong incentive to focus
on marketing. In addition, the Head of M arketing would not really have an
incentive to drive initiatives for Customer Success. is makes the whole
RevOps department far from optimal.
Now that we know who it should not report to, we can discuss viable
reporting options.
1. Chief Revenue Ofﬁcer
RevOps can report to the Chief Revenue Oﬃcer (CRO). e Head of RevOps
is often seen as the right hand of the CRO.
is model is common when your organization has a CRO to begin with.
e CRO needs to have a handle on all revenue departments: M arketing,
Sales, and Customer Success.
Some organizations rebrand their VP of Sales as a CRO, whose focus is
mainly on Sales with little oversight of M arketing and Customer Success. M ore
often, the leader does not possess the necessary skills to support these teams,
and as a result, they are neglected.
In this case, RevOps is simply rebranded from SalesOps. Organizations
that follow this path will not see the beneﬁts of RevOps.
2. The Chief Financial Ofﬁcer

---

## Page 41

e Chief Financial Oﬃcer (CFO) is focused on revenue and cost savings.
is is a nice match with RevOps because operational excellence is important
for both roles. is is best suited for organizations that don’t have a CRO role
but have division leaders like VPs of Sales, Marketing, Support, etc.
In such cases, you don’t want RevOps to report to one of these VPs. It’ll
create a bias and make RevOps less objective. However, there are even more
practical reasons why RevOps should report to the CFO.
First, RevOps has a direct impact on ﬁnancial performance indicators such
as bookings, annual recurring revenue, or net revenue retention. W orking
closely with ﬁnance on these topics can be beneﬁcial for the business as a
whole. In addition, being under the CFO provides a level of authority that
makes some initiatives easier. Hence, it’s best suited for Enforcer deployment.
e role of the CFO is to control spending, but in RevOps, you do need to
take ﬁnancial risks at times. A  too-rigid CFO can limit the growth potential of
RevOps.
3. The Chief Operating Ofﬁcer
Reporting to the Chief Operating Oﬃcer (COO) is a wild card. is is one of
the most diverse roles in an organization: some are close to sales, some are in
between, and some are literally managing the operations of warehouses.
Sometimes, there isn’t a CRO in the business, and the CFO doesn’t have
the right proﬁle for the job. In this case, the COO is often close enough to the
business and might even have a revenue background (this isn’t too uncommon
in SaaS companies). is can be even better than the CFO. e reason is that
RevOps might not only be limited to Success, Sales and M arketing but also
Legal, Finance and Product. is can help solve certain problems early on.
A s with the CFO, if the objectives and background of the COO are not
revenue-focused enough, then the risk is that RevOps will spend time on
activities that don’t drive revenue. It’s easy to keep people busy, but it takes skill
to identify key revenue drivers.

---

## Page 42

4. The Chief Executive Ofﬁcer
RevOps can unleash its full potential when it reports directly to the Chief
Executive Oﬃcer (CEO). U se this approach whenever possible. It signals that
the CEO is fully embracing the RevOps culture. If the board and CEO fully
support RevOps and recognize its potential, there’s no reason this shouldn’t be
the reporting structure.
For early-stage companies, the best way to start with RevOps is with the
Partner M odel. Larger companies should consider making the switch so that
RevOps is strategic. Once a business hits 1,000 employees or $100 million in
annual revenue, the CEO’s responsibilities shift and RevOps begins to report
to the CFO or COO.
“e question of who RevOps should report to isn’t just about hierarchy—it’s
about inﬂuence. If RevOps sits under Sales, it risks being too pipeline-centric.
U nder Finance, it can become overly focused on cost controls. e best approach?
RevOps should report to the CRO, COO, CBO, or CEO, ensuring a balanced
inﬂuence across G T M  teams–Sales, M arketing, and Customer Success. At Blink
Ops, we’ve structured RevOps to drive alignment across G T M  teams while
maintaining independence in strategic decision-making.”
— Leore Spira, D irector of RevOps, Blink Ops
Visualizing Your Organizational Chart
e next step is to design your RevOps organizational chart.
e organizational design will depend on your size, the composition of the
departments within your business, and the existing reporting lines. In the case
where RevOps reports to the CEO, then the organizational chart would look
like the following:

---

## Page 43

Figure 2.5: RevOps Reporting D irectly to CEO
is clearly signals that RevOps is at the same level as the other department
leaders.
A s we discussed earlier, RevOps is a strategic business partner for G T M
teams. e hierarchy levels of an organizational chart can support this view,
but it’s not necessary. To be a strategic business partner, the RevOps leader
does not necessarily need to be on the same management level as the CRO,
COO, or CFO. RevOps can accomplish the same when reporting to one of
those leaders.
Figure 2.6: RevOps reporting to a functional leader
Next, you need to consider who will report to your RevOps leader. It’s
mostly structured around the four main pillars of RevOps: Enablement,
Process, Systems, and D ata and Insights. ese four pillars are both strategic
and operational in their work.
If you combine them all together, then it could look like the following:

---

## Page 44

Figure 2.7: RevOps reporting to a functional leader with full org chart
Since these four pillars set the foundation for a successful RevOps
department, it’s important to understand the potential roles that exist under
each one.
Enablem ent
Enablement is about providing your G T M  teams with the information,
content, and tools they need to increase their performance.
A  complete Enablement team could have the following functions:

---

## Page 45

You’ll notice marketing content falls within RevOps. Having all content
under one pillar internally assures a high level of consistency. Your customers
do not care how your department is structured. ey perceive you as one
brand from the moment they ﬁrst hear about you until they purchase.
Process
Processes are about how your business executes its G T M  strategy and the day-
to-day operations. W hile they can be speciﬁc to a department, they often range
across the GT M  functions.
A  complete Process team has the following roles covered:

---

## Page 46

One team I’d like to highlight here is Cross-Functional Ops. Ideally, you
hire this team to be responsible to support M arketing, Sales, and Customer
Success, which increases alignment. A s the business complexity grows with
size, you can add speciﬁc support ops like M arkOps specialists, SalesOp
specialists, or Customer Success specialists.
Systems
Systems enable RevOps to function as a unifying device. is is when you dip
into selecting the right tools, how to combine them, as well as administration
tasks.
A  complete Systems team would have the following roles:

---

## Page 47

You’ll see that System A dmins can become a dedicated team. For example,
companies that use Salesforce and have a team above 1,000 employees will
easily have a whole team dedicated to Salesforce. at may include Salesforce
A dmins, Developers, Architects, or Project M anagers.
Data &  Insights
D ata & Insights are the last of the four pillars.
is pillar focuses on data governance, data hygiene, reporting, and
dashboarding. RevOps has a unique view of data insights because they also
understand the business context. at’s in contrast to your average data analyst
who excels in data analytics but lacks the business context.

---

## Page 48

M any businesses have a dedicated Business Intelligence (BI) team next to
the RevOps team. In these cases, the RevOps team works together with the BI
team to drive insights. e key is that RevOps is still deeply involved in the
analytics process. I like to think of this as “seeing the data with the right set of
eyes.” It brings a perspective that understands the business context behind the
data.
In the long run, it might make sense to move them all under RevOps. In
such a setup, RevOps would have an organizational-wide focus. However, it’s
most likely that RevOps has some dedicated data folks who collaborate with
the larger data team.
Synchronizing Functions: Integrating Your
RevOps Elem ents
Once you combine all of the roles together, you’ll have an organizational chart
that looks something like this:

---

## Page 49

Figure 2.8: RevOps full Org. Chart
For a business with over 1,000 employees making $500 million in annual
revenue, this would be an ideal scenario. But the reality is there are many
diﬀerent variations of it. Furthermore, with more A I technology being
deployed in businesses, the team size of RevOps (and any team) will decrease,
but the function will stay the same.
Let’s walk through a few other scenarios for diﬀerent maturity levels.
Early Startup
Characteristics: Less than 30 full-time employees, >$1 million annual
revenue, and product-market ﬁt (PM F) has been found.
Once PM F is conﬁrmed, it’s time to scale. Cost is still an issue, and in
most cases, it doesn’t justify hiring a full-time experienced RevOps, be it a
RevOps manager or a Head of RevOps.

---

## Page 50

For companies in this stage, we do recommend a RevOps consulting
service provider. e primary advantage is experience. Having a RevOps
specialist with a proven track record of successfully scaling early-stage
companies on your side can help you avoid common mistakes, such as:
•Poorly deﬁned strategy.
•Poorly designed CRM  with huge costs to clean it later.
•Processes that don’t scale.
•Costly trial and error before you ﬁnd what works.
•Premature automation.
Hiring an experienced RevOps consultant allows for a quicker hiring
process, faster onboarding, and earlier results. ey’ll also need less time to
ﬁgure things out internally. A s a result, this can be more aﬀordable than hiring
an in-house employee and is deﬁnitely more productive than your Sales team
managing the CRM  themselves.
Growth Startup
Characteristics: Less than 100 full-time employees and >$10 million annual
revenue.
is is a good starting point for RevOps because scale is not yet that big,
and facilitating change is still simple. You can start building your RevOps
department with the ultimate goal of it becoming a strategic partner.
A t this point, you can start with a Head of RevOps who has a basic
understanding of the four pillars. Look for a generalist. Ideally, with a business
background and growth mindset. is person should be more strategic than
analytical. She/he will be setting the foundation for your RevOps team.
A n alternative approach is to hire someone mid-level and leverage a
RevOps consulting provider to mentor, guide, and collaborate with the person.
However, if your Head of RevOps is your only hire, they should focus on
strategy and the big picture, leaving less time for operational tasks. External
RevOps support can help by freeing up resources for your team.

---

## Page 51

A s you grow with RevOps, you’ll also want to extend the team with a
specialist. e type of specialist you hire will depend on the unique situation of
your business, but here are a few considerations:
Deal Desk
If your focus is on enterprise clients, it makes sense to invest in a D eal D esk
early on. e sales cycle for enterprise clients is longer, and Requests for
Proposals (RFPs) and security questionnaires are almost mandatory with every
corporation, especially in the ﬁnance and healthcare sectors.
System  Adm in
System A dmin(s) could be a good addition to manage your tech stack. e
focus is on basic integrations and customizations. A  badly managed tech stack
can lead to costly data clean-up projects. In that regard, a good system admin
pays for itself. A  CRM  like Salesforce would require you to invest early on in
talented System Admin(s).
Data analyst
A  D ata A nalyst comes in handy if you have a product-led growth (PLG )
strategy or are a high-volume business. However, if you are selling enterprise
deals above $100,000 per deal, then you probably have around 100 clients.
e analytics at this scale are simple. ere is no need for a dedicated person,
especially now, since AI tools make it easier to assess your data.
Here’s what an organizational chart could look like.

---

## Page 52

Figure 2.9: RevOps partial Org. Chart for G rowth Startup
From here, you would build it out as you see ﬁt, slowly integrating more
roles into RevOps.
Scale U p
Characteristics: ~250 full-time employees, ~$50 million in annual revenue,
and scattered ops.
A t this level of maturity, it’s required to make a signiﬁcant change in
management to get started with RevOps. You will probably have a siloed
approach with various leaders on top of each. Here, the board and leadership
team need to align about what they want out of RevOps. Once that’s
conﬁrmed, the change can start.
e best starting point is with the Process pillar. Start adding cross-
functional project operational specialists. eir goal at the start should be to
work with speciﬁc projects and align them.
It’s best to start by aligning the M arketing and Sales departments. From
there, Sales and Customer Success. Be warned, aligning Sales, M arketing, and
Support at this stage will take plenty of time. A  project manager will also be
crucial. ey bring a lot of value here since most of RevOps’ work will involve
managing the change that comes with introducing a RevOps department.

---

## Page 53

One thing to avoid is aligning systems. Often, companies at this stage have
a dedicated IT  department that manages tech. e same goes for the analytics
or BI department. Instead of integrating those teams, it makes more sense to
collaborate with them. en, in the future, you can consider how to integrate
them if needed.

Figure 2.10: RevOps partial Org. Chart for Scale up
Enterprise
Characteristics: ~1,000 full-time employees, ~$150 million in annual
revenue, and scattered ops.
Implementing RevOps in an established enterprise will be more complex.
It requires a lot of internal change and eﬀort to deploy RevOps into the
business. ere are existing structures already in place, and too much change
can disrupt day-to-day operations.
But if you’re committed to deploying it, I recommend a phased
deployment. is can start with an Enforcer model where RevOps has a clear

---

## Page 54

revenue goal. e focus of the RevOps team is to align teams by Processes and
Systems and also by using D ata & Insightsto inform those decisions.
Enablement stays with the existing operations team of M arketing, Sales, or
Customer Success. is option is complementary to the existing roles.
Here’s what an organizational chart could look like:
Figure 2.11: RevOps partial Org. Chart for Enterprise
Once this structure is adopted and shows its ﬁrst success, then it’s possible
to move to the Partner D eployment M odel and slowly integrate speciﬁc roles
into the above chart to reach the ﬁnal version.
is is just one of many options for an enterprise organizational chart. A
company may have multiple divisions working alongside each other, operate in
multiple geographies with diﬀerent maturities, or may have other specialized
departments. In short, every enterprise has a unique composition of teams. It’s
important to consider the uniqueness of your business before making any
decision on how to best start a RevOps department.
W hile it’s great to understand how to organize your business around
RevOps, it’s also critical to understand what each individual role looks like for
the department.

---

## Page 55

RevOps Career Paths and Growth
RevOps is a young function. A t the same time, it’s also one of the fastest-
growing jobs. In 2023, Head of RevOps was listed as the fastest-growing job in
A merica on LinkedIn’s “Jobs on the Rise” list.12
D emand is high for excellent talent. e majority of people in RevOps
have been in their roles for less than a few years. ere aren’t that many
experienced RevOps professionals, and that puts additional pressure on great
talent.
is just ampliﬁes the importance of having a clear career path and
progression possibilities for great talent. From the start, it’s important to have a
transparent career path for your RevOps team (frankly, you want to have it for
your whole company).
Career paths are important as they:
•Improve employee retention.
•Improve productivity (an employee with a direction knows what they are
working towards).
•Support succession planning.
Regarding the last point, you don’t want to end up in a situation where you
can’t promote a “star” employee because there is no one to replace them! You
run the risk of losing that employee as he/she might go shop somewhere else,
and you end up with no one.
Here are some things to keep at the forefront of your mind as you think
about the career paths for your team.
The Two Career Paths For RevOps
A  RevOps career path has two routes: one for individual contributors and one
for managers.
A n individual contributor is an employee without management
responsibilities. You aim to become a subject matter expert in the domain you

---

## Page 56

are interested in, be that Enablement, Process, Systems, or Data and Insights.
In the management path, your ambition is to lead people to drive business
impact. In corporate settings, they typically become the VP of RevOps, a
CRO, or even a CEO.
Both routes should be ﬁnancially attractive and mentally stimulating. Not
everyone wants to stay an individual contributor, nor does everyone want to be
a manager. Talk to your HR team for advice on this topic.
Talent Developm ent: The RevOps
Leadership Imperative
“Building a career path for your RevOps team is essential for maintaining
engagement and retention. A s RevOps teams scale, leaders must transition
into eﬀective people managers, dedicating signiﬁcant time to developing
career frameworks and growth opportunities. is strategic investment in
talent development becomes a critical responsibility that directly impacts
the long-term success and stability of the revenue operations function.”
- Andreas Drakos, Senior Director of Revenue Operations at Superside
How to Hire Your First RevOps Position
Your ﬁrst RevOps hire should never be a junior. e main concern is that soft
skills like stakeholder management, communication, and change management
aren’t up to par. RevOps has to work with the whole organization to drive
change. Change is hard. You need some life experience for this. It is also
important that your ﬁrst RevOps hire is a generalist. You should know by now
that RevOps covers a wide range of topics. Your ﬁrst RevOps hire should have
a good understanding of every element. You face the risk of missing out on the
full RevOps potential if you hire a specialist.
A  complementary approach is to hire a mid-level RevOps professional and
use a RevOps consulting partner to mentor the individual. is gives more

---

## Page 57

progression options to the individual and can help with tighter budgets. is is
what I recommend for smaller companies.
In recent years, professional training programs have become available on
the market. ese programs are great for exposing new people to the ﬁeld and
providing training for more advanced professionals.
However, your ﬁrst RevOps hire will also depend on your deployment
model.
In the Support model, the person can be a mid-level hire as the focus is on
short-term initiatives. I don’t recommend this, as you will be setting up
RevOps for a bad start. RevOps will be perceived as an operational support
function, and from there, it will be much harder to become a strategic
function.
In the Enforcer or Partner deployment models, the ﬁrst hire has to be in a
senior role, such as a Head of RevOps. is person has to collaborate and drive
initiatives with various G T M  leaders who have their own agenda. e person
will be driving the strategy for the growth of the RevOps department and
setting it up for success. is person will deeply understand the revenue-
generating aspect of the business from both an operational and strategic level.
Nobody will understand the business that well. I often see this proﬁle make it
to a VP of RevOps, CRO, and even CEOs.
M ostly though, you want this ﬁrst hire to run your RevOps department.
So make sure they meet the criteria for a leadership position. ey don’t
necessarily need to have managed anyone before, but the right characteristics
should be present.

---

## Page 58

Figure 2.12: RevOps Key Traits for Leadership
I would call this person an “analytical people wrangler.” A  big mistake is
hiring someone who is strong in hard analytical skills but weak in people skills
(that help you work well with others). I recommend working with an
experienced RevOps recruiter to ﬁnd your ﬁrst RevOps hire.
Hiring Specialists Over Managers
Once you have a RevOps leader, and that person reaches their limit in terms of
skills or capacity, then it’s time to hire a RevOps specialist. ese specialists
may be experts in enablement, data wizards, or analytics for marketing data.
ey will become seniors, team leads, and eventually, managers. D on’t make
the mistake of hiring a manager that has no management responsibility. A
manager is, by deﬁnition, someone who manages people. Stick to the meaning
of the word. It will avoid misunderstandings and frustration and reduce
employee turnover.
Vertical vs. Horizontal Prom otion
Not everyone wants to move vertically up the career ladder. M any people are
also happy to move horizontally within the organization. Someone who is great
at sales enablement might eventually want to work as a solution architect.

---

## Page 59

A mazing! Provide this option to your team. W hat’s better than a motivated
team member who wants to invest in his career and learn new skills?
All Roads Lead to RevOps: The RevOps
Career of Lorena
“I was one of the RevOps ‘originals’ about 8 years ago, working with the
actual methodology before it even had a name in the SaaS world. It was an
exciting time, helping to create this new category alongside partners like
G 2.
ere’s this idea ﬂoating around that to lead in RevOps, you must have
climbed up from operations roles—either Sales Ops or M arketing Ops. But
my story breaks that mold completely. W hile I’ve always been pretty
dangerous with operational skills, that wasn’t my background at all.
I actually came from what people call “traditional” marketing—spending
years in branding, paid advertising, and partnerships. Yet that didn’t stop
me from becoming a RevOps leader at a Fortune 500 company, building a
global team that truly excels at what we do.
I want people to know that you don’t need to follow someone else’s path.
W hat matters most is your attitude and your ability to learn and unlearn
things quickly—faster than most people can manage. RevOps is still
deﬁning itself, which means there’s plenty of room for people from diﬀerent
backgrounds who share one essential quality: a deep obsession with the
customer experience.
e journey to RevOps success isn’t about where you started. It’s about how
quickly you can adapt, how well you can connect the dots across
departments, and how deeply you care about creating seamless experiences
for customers.

---

## Page 60

W hat makes RevOps special is that it welcomes diﬀerent perspectives. Your
unique background might actually be your superpower, giving you insights
that others miss. e ﬁeld keeps evolving, but that customer focus remains
at the heart of everything.
So if you’re thinking about a career in RevOps, don’t get caught up in
whether you have the “right” background. W hat matters is your willingness
to learn, adapt, and keep the customer at the center of everything you do.”
- Lorena M orales, Director of Global Digital M arketing RevOps at JLL
Compensation Strategies for High-Im pact
Talent
is section focuses on compensation and bonus structures for a RevOps team.
A  common misconception is that only managers should be compensated
well. But what matters most is their impact on revenue.
Imagine you have a great sales enablement professional who is an
individual contributor. e coaching skills of this professional have increased
revenue by 5%. How should they be compensated? Probably more than his
manager because that’s a signiﬁcant impact on revenue.
I have worked with excellent sales coaches and have seen the impact they
have on the sales side. ese individuals are very aware of their own skills.
at’s why you see the best ones becoming independent consultants or
working for high-growth companies. ose high-growth companies
understand their impact and pay them a premium.
M ake sure to compensate your high-impact individual contributors well so
they stay with your business. e loss of this talent leaves a noticeable gap in
your business.
Determining Compensation For Your Team
Memb ers

---

## Page 61

Seniority is closely correlated with the impact someone has on revenue. It often
serves as a proxy for responsibility and typically aligns with greater inﬂuence
on business outcomes. However, certain roles inherently have a higher impact
on revenue. For example, someone working on enablement will have a higher
impact than someone working as a System A dmin. ese are important
elements when considering variable pay.
How to Think About Variable Pay
RevOps’ outcomes are to increase revenue, reduce costs, and increase
proﬁtability. at’s why variable pay for RevOps is important. ey need to
have skin in the game. e share of variable pay diﬀers on the level of seniority
and the impact the role has on revenue.
Here’s what it might look like:
To determine the seniority is rather easy but determining the impact on
revenue might be a bit trickier.
Building a High-Impact RevOps Foundation
Remember, the best structure won’t guarantee results, but the wrong one
almost always leads to failure.

---

## Page 62

Choosing the right deployment model, whether it’s the Service Provider,
Enforcer, or Partner, depends on your company’s maturity and goals. But no
matter the model, leadership and board alignment are non-negotiable.
W ithout it, you risk ending up with a “fake” RevOps function that can’t live
up to its potential, which is a waste of resources and talent.
A s your organization grows, so should your RevOps team. U nderstanding
where you stand on the RevOps maturity continuum helps you identify areas
for improvement, moving from reactive and operational to proactive and
strategic. Building transparent career paths not only attracts top talent but also
keeps your team motivated and aligned with your objectives.
The Evolution of RevOps
A  RevOps department not only grows in size and skills of employees but also
matures in its capabilities.
You might be starting out with RevOps. Or perhaps you already have an
established team. Either way, your RevOps department won’t be perfect the
ﬁrst time; we all have diﬀerent starting points and corresponding strengths.
ere is always something new to learn and an area to improve. e drive for
continuous learning is part of the RevOps DNA .
The RevOps Maturity Continuum
Tariq Hassan A hmad created a RevOps maturity framework. I took his
framework and adopted it slightly.
He identiﬁes four stages of RevOps maturity: beginner, intermediate,
advanced, and mature. A n organization progresses through these stages based
on its position along the maturity continuum.

---

## Page 63

Figure 2.1 - RevOps M aturity Pyramid
A  continuum is a great metaphor because it illustrates how RevOps
matures in a business. Sometimes, it’s okay to have only an operational focus,
but you also need to be strategic at times. It all depends on the circumstances
of the business.
On the low end of the continuum, you could see a RevOps team that’s
constantly ﬁreﬁghting and ﬁxing low-value tickets. is team is not involved in
decision-making and hears about strategic decisions only after they’ve been
made. ey are perceived as admins, and hardly anyone in the business
understands what the team does (or has even heard of them).
Contrast that with a rockstar RevOps team that’s known across the whole
business as the key driver for strategic initiatives. e team is proactive in
anticipating challenges and is able to involve other stakeholders to come up

---

## Page 64

with innovative solutions that drive revenue and growth across the business.
ey have a tangible business impact.
Your business might be somewhere between the two extremes. Hopefully,
you’re moving more toward the high end of the continuum.
Figure 2.2 RevOps M aturity Continuum

---

## Page 65

e table provides a quick overview of each stage, along with the average
score. A s part of this book, we at Revenue W izards conducted research
surveying over 180 RevOps professionals globally.13 One aspect of the survey
measured RevOps maturity on a scale from 1 to 5, where 1 represents low

---

## Page 66

maturity and 5 represents high maturity. Overall, the results suggest that
RevOps maturity is moderately mature.
Now, let’s explore each stage in detail to help you better understand where
you might fall.
Reactive ↔  Proactive
In a reactive state, the RevOps team has tasks assigned and only has limited
control over them. ey tend to have a backlog and have some choice in
prioritizing activities.
In a proactive state, the RevOps team has autonomy to impact strategy and
it supports those with relevant projects they’ve crafted.
Operational Focus ↔  Strategic Focus
In some organizations, the RevOps team is perceived as system administrators.
In these businesses, the focus is solely on operational activities. is includes
report creation, adding ﬁelds to a CRM , training a user, ﬁxing bugs, setting up
an integration, scraping data, writing documentation, or responding to any
immediate support inquiries. ese activities are all important and need to be
done to operate a business. But those activities alone won’t create new revenue
streams, reduce operational costs, or increase proﬁt margins.
Contrast that with businesses where RevOps inﬂuences decisions such as
which markets to enter, sales capacity planning, analyzing data to improve the
customer journey, developing new commission plans to increase revenue,
building smart A I agents that complement the work of every employee,
creating operational infrastructure that naturally enables employees to succeed
in their work, or collaborating with department leaders to further align G T M .
is is strategic RevOps.
A s highlighted earlier, this does not mean that RevOps is not operational.
It is a continuum. RevOps has to do both. In a mature organization, RevOps
impacts the strategy and executes it.

---

## Page 67

Low Empowerm ent ↔  High Em powerm ent
A  RevOps professional once told me a story about a large Japanese industrial
consulting ﬁrm with over 1,500 employees. ey tasked its RevOps team with
a comprehensive review of data as the company prepared for an IPO.
e team uncovered some troubling trends: many salespeople were
underperforming, spending signiﬁcant time without selling. Proﬁt margins on
existing clients were lower than expected. W hen the RevOps team reported
these ﬁndings, some leaders became uncomfortable. M any of these
underperforming employees were hired due to nepotism, and the exposure of
these insights threatened the personal reputation of these individuals. In
response, leadership attempted to disband the team and eventually ﬁred the
entire RevOps department.
Just a few months later, more than 70% of the company’s workforce was
laid oﬀ, as it became impossible to ignore the ﬁnancial reality.
is illustrates an extreme case of low empowerment. Here, the RevOps
team had the responsibility to identify critical data insights but lacked the
support to act on them. M iddle management failed to accept the
consequences.
is is why I emphasize the need for empowerment from the highest levels,
speciﬁcally from the board and C-suite. RevOps teams are often positioned to
surface uncomfortable truths that require bold decisions. W ithout true
empowerment, RevOps becomes “fake RevOps,” where the function exists in
name only without the authority to drive change.
If you ﬁnd yourself in a fake RevOps environment, it may be best to look
for a new role; it’s not your job to ﬁx that foundational misalignment. e
magic happens when leadership and the board are behind RevOps. is gives
the team the support they need to drive organizational-wide projects. A s you’ll
see later on, this is where RevOps adds the most value. High-growth
companies understand this and are fully behind their RevOps teams.
Low Inﬂuence ↔  High Inﬂuence

---

## Page 68

RevOps with low inﬂuence understand what needs to be done to reach a goal,
but they don’t manage to get buy-in from stakeholders. ey may present an
idea in a rational way, but are unable to inﬂuence how other stakeholders
perceive their work.
Inﬂuence requires persuasion.
W hen you combine low inﬂuence with low empowerment, it makes
everything worse. However, it can’t all be explained through low
empowerment. Inﬂuence is a soft skill that can be learned. But learning takes
time and requires both work and life experience. In Chapter 4, we’ll look at a
few techniques on how to improve inﬂuence when it comes to stakeholder
management.
RevOps with high inﬂuence understands what needs to be done and what
they need to do to get buy-in from stakeholders. RevOps is all about
alignment, integration, and collaboration, which requires excellent stakeholder
management skills. e larger an organization becomes, the more political it
gets. ey understand that the right solution without political power is
meaningless.
Even businesses with the ﬂattest hierarchies and the best culture are
political in some form. at is just the social game of humans. It is important
to learn to have inﬂuence in RevOps and life.
Weak tooling ↔  Strong Tooling
W eak tooling means that technology is not properly administered. e
platforms are isolated and don’t interact with each other. It might even mean
that you have multiple tools that do the same functions. W eak tooling often
results from not having a technology strategy or not having RevOps oversee it.
Businesses with strong tooling make sure that each tool is ﬁt for purpose.
In RevOps, this means that it facilitates alignment, collaboration, and
integration.

---

## Page 69

GTM Inﬂuence ↔  Organization-W ide
Inﬂuence
ere are businesses where RevOps only supports Sales, or perhaps there is a
strong bias towards M arketing. is is the fake RevOps we talked about earlier.
In mature organizations, RevOps has an organizational-wide inﬂuence. ose
additional stakeholders include Legal, Finance, and Product.
Figure 2.3 RevOps Organizational-W ide Inﬂuence
is makes a lot of sense as those departments all impact revenue. Your
revenue process does not stop with customer success. Legal reviews impact the
customer experience, the invoicing process impacts your cash ﬂow, and the
product impacts whether clients will renew.
W ith a solid RevOps foundation in place, it’s time to turn our attention to
the next critical piece of the puzzle: your strategy and its execution.
