CHAPTER 3
FROM VISION TO SW ING –
MOVING FROM STRATEGY TO
EXECU TION
”H appiness comes from what we do.
Fulﬁllment comes from why we do it.”
Simon Sinek
Imagine embarking on a journey without a clear destination. Sure, the ride
might be thrilling for a while. But eventually, you’ll start to wonder: where am I
headed? W hy did I start this journey in the ﬁrst place?
is is the crossroads many companies ﬁnd themselves in at the start of
their RevOps journey.
RevOps isn’t just about ﬁne-tuning the operations of Sales, M arketing, and
Customer Success. It’s about orchestrating these elements to work in harmony
toward a strategic vision. It’s about ensuring every cog in the wheel is not only
functioning eﬃciently but also propelling the organization towards its ultimate
goals.
But here’s the challenge: the term “Revenue Operations” often misleads. It
emphasizes “operations,” overshadowing its strategic signiﬁcance. It’s like calling
a master chef merely a cook. W hile it’s technically correct, it misses the artistry
and vision behind the title. Some might argue for a name like “Revenue

---

## Page 72

Strategy Operations,” but the essence lies not in the label but in understanding
and implementing the core principles that drive RevOps.
In this chapter, we’ll explore why a mission, vision, and values are important
for your RevOps function. You’ll understand why these matter, how they can
transform your organizational culture, and ultimately, how they can drive your
business forward.
Once you have a strategic understanding, you can move toward the
execution of those elements down to the practical day-to-day management.
Deﬁning Mission, Vision, and Values
How do you infuse strategy into operations?
It starts with a clear vision, mission, and values. ese are not just corporate
buzzwords; they are the guiding stars that provide direction and purpose. A
M cKinsey study highlights that employees are more motivated when their work
has meaning.14 is isn’t surprising—who doesn’t want to feel that their eﬀorts
contribute to something greater?
Your mission, vision, and values help set the right culture and direction. A
brilliant statement even helps to increase the strength of your brand. One brand
that excels at this is Nike, so let’s look at them in more detail.
Mission
Your mission is the reason why your business exists. It’s the main driving force
behind your business and why it’s important.
In your statement, highlight how you will achieve your mission.
Nike’s M ission Statement
Our mission is to bring inspiration and innovation to every athlete* in the
world. [*If you have a body, you are an athlete.] is mission drives us to do
everything possible to expand human potential. W e do that by creating
groundbreaking sports innovations, by making our products more

---

## Page 73

sustainable, by building a creative and diverse global team, and by making
a positive impact in communities where we live and work.
Vision
Your vision is the future you want to create as a business. is is your dream
world. It should be ambitious. M ost people should say that it’s impossible, but
not everyone. e ones that share your vision are the ones who should join your
company.
Nike Vision Statement
W e see a world where everybody is an athlete—u nited in the joy of
movement. D riven by our passion for sport and our instinct for innovation,
we aim to bring inspiration to every athlete in the w orld and to make sport
a daily habit.
A mission and vision are powerful tools to create purpose and a moral
compass for a company. e problem they may have is that they are not
tangible enough in day-to-day work. A solution to help combat this is to create
department speciﬁc visions and mission statements.
How to Do This For RevOps
Your mission and vision is crafted on an organizational level, which is the task
of the leadership team. But RevOps is a cross-functional department that is
characterized by many competing activities, so crafting a speciﬁc mission and
vision statement for RevOps will empower the department. It deﬁnes their
purpose and provides a guiding framework. is becomes especially important
as the team and company grows in size.
Here’s an example of what this could look like:
Our mission is to empower revenue teams to deliver the best customer
experience and create a sustainable business impact. W e achieve this by
leveraging state-of-the-art AI technologies, enhancing our colleagues’

---

## Page 74

capabilities through optimized processes, and fostering collaboration across
all G T M  teams.
e goal of the mission statement is that it represents the values of your
organization. e above examples have universal parts of the RevOps deﬁnition
(i.e., collaboration) but also speciﬁc elements that are relevant for that company
(i.e., prioritization of AI technologies).
Here’s what a vision statement could look like for a RevOps team:
W e see a world in which G T M  teams are empowered by AI agents to deliver
the best customer experience. W e are driven by our passion for high-quality
data that serves as a prerequisite for an excellent process.
An ambitious mission and vision are hard to reach, and they’re not easy. At
times, you have to make hard decisions. is is where values come into play.
Values
Values serve as a moral compass, directing your actions toward achieving your
vision and mission.
Nike’s Values
•D o the right thing.
•Be on the oﬀense always.
•Serve athletes.
•Create the future of sport.
•W in as a team.
Having a vision, mission, and values is great, as it shows you a path for the
future.
en, it’s time to carve out the path.

---

## Page 75

ere is no better way to do that than to create a Target Operating M odel
(TOM ).
Crafting Your Target Operating Model
For a business to grow, it needs a balance between:
•e “why”: Its purpose and vision for the future.
•e “how”: e way it operates daily and how it evolves.
•e “what”: e actual steps taken to achieve success at scale.
e why and the how establish the greatness of a company. is is where the
real innovation happens. ese are the companies that changed the world, like
Nvidia, Apple, and OpenAI.
W hat refers to the actual steps the company takes to make this greatness a
reality… and scale it.
at’s where a Target Operating M odel (TOM ) comes in.
A TOM  is a plan for how a business should operate in the future. It outlines
the changes needed to achieve long-term success. M eanwhile, the Current
Operating M odel (COM ) represents how the business works today to meet its
present goals. You can think of the TOM  as a roadmap that helps a company
move from where it is now to where it wants to be.

---

## Page 76

A TOM  is a detailed plan that includes key processes across all areas of a
business. It’s meant to be granular and breaks down sub-processes to identify
what needs to change. Some processes may stay the same, while others might
need to be adjusted or completely redesigned.
Here’s an example. e TOM  column on the right shows the ideal
outcome. You don’t need to have all the details ﬁgured out right away. It’s meant
to help guide decisions and keep the business on track for long-term success.

---

## Page 77

For this exercise, you want to be realistic but still ambitious. For example,
the goal might be to become the leading SaaS solution for G T M  operating
platforms. e TOM  could consider a point in the future with ﬁve times the
amount of clients. Or you could get more speciﬁc, such as entering three new
countries or having four times more resellers. All of this adds color to it. It
requires you to think about all of the diﬀerent scenarios for your business. In
the resource section of this book, you will ﬁnd a template to build your own
TOM .
Finally, you don’t need to add an end date here. is exercise is rather about
the robustness of your processes to ensure they can handle future requirements.
e question then becomes: what does success look like for the RevOps
department?
at’s where Key Performance Indicators (KPIs) come in.
Choosing the Right KPIs
In every business, you measure success with KPIs. e idea is to review one
number and understand if you are crushing it or losing it. For example,

---

## Page 78

M arketing can look at the number of inbound leads, while Sales looks at new
business deals closed, and Customer Success monitors net revenue retention.
But what KPIs matter for RevOps?
I haven’t met anyone who has solved this issue, and I ﬁnd that fascinating.
In RevOps, we pride ourselves on being data-driven, but as a department, we
don’t have our own KPIs. Of course, you will hear people saying they have KPIs
for their RevOps department. But such statements raise at least a few eyebrows
because nobody really believes it. Anyone who has a decent understanding of
RevOps will say they’re not really RevOps KPIs.
ese are not RevOps KPIs:
•Customer Acquisition Cost (CAC)
•Customer Lifetime Value (CLT V)
•Customer Churn Rate
•Conversion Rates
•W in/loss ratios
•W ebsite traﬃc
•Revenue
I would love to agree that RevOps can own any of those KPIs. Especially
the “Revenue” KPI, since we have revenue in the name. But that would be a lie.
I have never seen someone in RevOps ensure revenue is generated. Probably for
the better, as the folks in Sales or Success are better at that.
D oes that mean that KPIs such as CAC, CLT V, or revenue are not
important for RevOps? Of course not. W e live and breathe these K PIs. ey are
part of our identity, but we don’t own them. W e just piggyback on them. e
actual owners are the speciﬁc departments, and RevOps has limited inﬂuence
over them. RevOps does not pick up the phone and make calls. RevOps doesn’t
close deals. RevOps doesn’t write content for the website. RevOps doesn’t have
quarterly performance review calls with clients. ese actions directly impact
the KPIs.
is limited inﬂuence actually holds the key to identifying our KPIs because
we do inﬂuence them. Remember, RevOps is a support function for all revenue

---

## Page 79

teams, designed to drive integration, alignment, and collaboration across these
functions. e ultimate goal is to enhance both business performance and
customer impact. G iven this role, it’s no surprise that RevOps projects aﬀect
nearly every KPI in the organization. at’s the core purpose of RevOps: to
serve as a powerful integrative force.
So, while RevOps impacts most revenue KPIs, it can’t own any because the
team has no direct impact on them. To measure the performance of RevOps,
you need to compare two similar companies. One that has a RevOps
department and one without. en, compare the performance of KPIs over
time.
If the company with RevOps is performing better than the one without, we
have our answer. It’s hard to make this comparison because it’s hard to ﬁnd two
similar companies and get this type of information about them. However, we
have the next best thing: publicly listed companies. W e did this with my team
at Revenue W izards and analyzed the stock performance of S&P 500
companies: those with RevOps and those without.15 Companies with RevOps
see:
•225.52% more market capitalization.
•14.14% higher stock performance.
•176% more EBIT D A.
You can ﬁnd the full report in the digital resource section of this book.
(https://revenuewizards.com/revops-book).
W hat we also can do is to compare diﬀerent points in time within the same
company. For example, imagine you have a project that improves the hand-oﬀ
process between Sales and Customer Success. e goal of the project is to
improve onboarding satisfaction and ultimately reduce churn. You can measure
this by comparing the onboarding satisfaction and churn rate before and after a
project. is will indicate how successful the project was. is, again, is a proxy
KPI for RevOps; RevOps contributed to the project, but so did Sales and
Customer Success.
Here we are again in the limited inﬂuence space. Shall we just embrace the
limited inﬂuence and deal with it? Not really, we can instead look at Objective

---

## Page 80

and Key Results (OKR s).
The Next Best Thing to KPIs
OKR s are a framework that helps a business deﬁne its goals and measure them.
ey ﬁt perfectly into RevOps as they focus on alignment across departments.
ey are used by individuals, teams, and organizations to deﬁne measurable
goals and track their progress toward achieving them.
Here’s a breakdown of the components:
Objective: is is a clearly deﬁned goal set on a company, team, or
individual level. Objectives are typically ambitious, qualitative, and
inspirational.
K ey Results: ese are measurable outcomes needed to achieve the
objective. ey are used to gauge progress and determine how close you are to
achieving your objective. Typically, each objective has around 2 to 5 K ey
Results.
Figure 2.2: Objective and K ey Results (OK R) in RevOps

---

## Page 81

For example, the Objective could be to reduce churn by 5% within the
current ﬁscal year. In that scenario, your Key Results could be:
1.Implement an AI support chatbot to reduce manual ticket processing by
15% by the end of Q1.
2.Implement a Customer Success platform to reduce churn by 3% by the
end of Q2.
3.Improve the Net Promoter Score (NPS) by 0.3 by the end of Q3.
Of course, you’ll run into the same problem as KPIs in terms of limited
inﬂuence. But with OKR s, it’s less of a problem as they can share responsibility.
RevOps doesn’t need to own a speciﬁc one. e creation of them is usually a
joint exercise between company leadership, who links it to the strategic aspect,
and the diﬀerent departments, who link it to the operational aspect. A business
that created a TOM  can use it as input for the OKR s.
OKR s also can further help RevOps to prioritize incoming projects. Some
requests will be tied to OKR s and can be added to the roadmap. Others won’t
ﬁt, but they’ll still add value and can be included. ere will be requests that
have a low value-add and don’t ﬁt the OKR s. In that scenario, OK Rs can act as
a shield against distractions and protect the RevOps roadmap.
It’s also much easier to take ownership of OKR s because you’re working
with projects that are related to a speciﬁc objective. at’s why OKR s are well
suited for RevOps. ey provide some form of accountability and measure of
success (it’s important that you have a deadline for them).
I wish there would be a simple KPI to measure the success of RevOps, but I
haven’t found one that’s satisfactory. For me, OKR s are the next best alternative.
It’s time to embrace them.
Setting the Right Goals: OKRs in RevOps
An interview with Hugo Pereira

---

## Page 82

In an Amsterdam brown bar, I sat down with Hugo Pereira, a growth
leadership practitioner and strategy design expert, to discuss how RevOps
teams can eﬀectively implement goal-setting frameworks. As someone who’s
helped numerous companies transform their approach to goals, Hugo shared
his key insights.
H aris: Hugo, how do most companies handle their goal-setting process?
H ugo: Let’s put it this way: when done well, goal setting creates clarity,
aligns teams, and gets everyone pushing in the same direction. W hen done
poorly... well, imagine a marching band where everyone’s playing diﬀerent
songs while walking backward. at’s what bad goal-setting looks like. And
if there’s one function that can’t aﬀord that kind of chaos, it’s RevOps.
H aris: W hy is RevOps particularly sensitive to this?
H ugo: RevOps sits at the intersection of Sales, M arketing, and Customer
Success, which means RevOps rarely owns business outcomes outright. But
you guys in RevOps inﬂuence nearly everything. Traditional KPIs like
Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR), conversion rates, or dials made don’t
work well for RevOps because these metrics measure results, but they don’t
measure the systems and processes that shape those results.
H aris: So, how should RevOps teams approach OKR s?
H ugo: e best OKR s for RevOps are outcome-driven and focused on levers
the team can actually move. Instead of an OKR  like “Increase ARR by
20%,” which RevOps doesn’t own, focus on something like “Reduce lead
response time from 4 hours to 30 minutes” or “Improve forecast accuracy
from 60% to 80%.”
is is where the NCT  framework helps: Narrative, Commitments, and
Tasks. e narrative explains why this goal matters, commitments deﬁne
what must happen, and tasks outline how you’ll get there. W hen embedded
with OKR s, this approach keeps teams focused on outcomes, not just
numbers.

---

## Page 83

H aris: How does RevOps handle goals that overlap with other departments?
H ugo: Take churn, for example. Customer Success might own it, but
RevOps inﬂuences it by optimizing renewal workﬂows, improving customer
health scoring, or reﬁning expansion playbooks. e solution lies in shared
data, joint debriefs, and clear accountability. Teams need to know who owns
what, who inﬂuences what, and who executes what.
H aris: Any ﬁnal thoughts on how RevOps teams should think about OK Rs?
H ugo: RevOps thrives when it sets OKR s that are inﬂuential rather than
absolute. e goal isn’t to measure everything. Rather, it’s to move what
matters. W hen teams understand this distinction, they can create meaningful
objectives that drive real business impact.
How to Think Through Prioritization
In RevOps, you work with all revenue teams to support their projects and cross-
functional projects. e workload can easily become immense, and the question
becomes: how do you best prioritize this workload?
e ﬁrst step is to explore various project management methods.
MoSCoW
D ai Clegg invented M oSCoW  in the 90s during his time at Oracle while
working as a software developer.16 Since then, it has become a popular
prioritization method that is used in management, project management, or
software development.
To use the M oSCoW  method, you need to list your projects. en, you
categorize based on your requirements. e categories are:
•M ust have: ese are critical and must-have.
•Should have: ese are important but not critical.

---

## Page 84

•Could have: ese are nice to have.
•W on’t have: ese are not a priority.
Figure 3.3: M oSCoW  for Project management
e method is simple to use and therefore widespread. It has some
problems, as it does not consider time (and we all know how important time is
in business).
Value/Effort Matrix to Prioritize Projects
e Value/Eﬀort M atrix has two axes. e ﬁrst axis rates from low to high on
the value it will generate. e other axis looks at the eﬀort to get there. e
combination of these criteria will place the speciﬁc project in a speciﬁc
quadrant.

---

## Page 85

Figure 3.4: Value / Eﬀort M atrix to Prioritize Projects
For example, an incremental project may be to educate your Revenue teams
about Customer Relationship M anagement (CRM ) hygiene. It’s low eﬀort and
while the value is low in the short term, it’s beneﬁcial in the long run.
Or perhaps a new regulatory requirement was introduced that requires your
business to have a deletion process in place for all customer data. at can
become a complex and timely project with little to no value. ere might be
value in it as you avoid a potential ﬁne, but from a revenue perspective, it’s low
value.
You always want to have a few easy wins in your backlog. ese are the
projects that are easy to implement and will have a big impact—for example,
adopting a call recording and transcript solution. Your customer-facing
colleagues will save a lot of time around notetaking and can then focus on client
activities that add value.

---

## Page 86

Big bets are strategic projects such as deploying a company-wide AI-ﬁrst
strategy. ese are the projects that require a lot of resources but have the
promise of a big impact. U sually, you’ll work on a few of those projects over the
course of a year.
In RevOps, we don’t live in our own bubble. W e are part of a bigger
organization and
at’s why it’s essential to align our projects with the business’ broader
goals.
The RevOps Roadm ap
A RevOps roadmap is a visual representation of the projects RevOps is working
on, according to priority.
Figure 3.5: Illustrations of a Roadmap
e goal of a RevOps roadmap is to provide strategic orientation for your
RevOps department and stakeholders from other departments. It’s dynamic, as
priorities can (and will) change. is is especially true for startups, as the
business tends to pivot more. It includes projects that are part of the TOM  and
the OKR s, as well as ad-hoc projects that can arise.

---

## Page 87

e degree of how dynamic and accurate your RevOps roadmap is will
depend on your organization. In startups, the roadmap might be roughly one
year, but isn’t overly speciﬁc. In an enterprise, the time span will be multiple
years as the organization is more complex. It will also be less ﬂexible, and the
dates will be more exact.
If you’re a startup, you might want to be more precise. But from my
experience, the changes are just too frequent, and the future is too uncertain to
have a roadmap for multiple years.
In the Scale-U p phase (when product-market ﬁt is reached), long-term
planning becomes crucial. In this stage, there are many competing activities
happening at the same time—prioritization matters. A roadmap is another tool
to help you with it. Visual representations will help you get further support for
the various projects within your business, but it will also help you to say no to
projects that don’t ﬁt within your company’s priorities.
is visual representation is helpful when having G T M  meetings between
all department leaders to move, add, or remove projects. Remember, the
priorities of the business will certainly change. It’s a living, breathing document.
RevOps at Adyen: The “Behind the Scenes”
Growth Driver for Enterprise GTM
By Evelina Petrova-op den K elder, VP of RevOps at Adyen
Adyen’s enterprise-focused strategy revolves around providing a uniﬁed, end-
to-end payment platform that enables large enterprises to manage global
payments seamlessly. e company has a presence in 28 locations worldwide

---

## Page 88

and leverages strategic partnerships with major platforms, such as Salesforce,
Oracle, and Shopify. is strategy introduces several complexities into our
sales cycle and GT M  execution:
1.Longer and more consultative sales cycle: Enterprise deals involve
multiple stakeholders, whilst uniﬁed payments infrastructure requires
deep technical evaluation and integrations.
2.M ulti-market and multi-channel decision making: Selling to large
enterprises often involves multiple regional buying centers, requiring sales
to manage cross-border approvals.
3.Land and Expand M odel with ongoing commercial expansion: Many
enterprise deals start with a single region or payment type (e.g., online
transactions) before expanding to omnichannel payments, embedded
ﬁnancial products, and new geographies.
is is where RevOps comes into the G T M  picture. e old operating
model was that individual Ops teams reported to the individual functions:
M arketing Ops to M arketing, Sales Ops to Sales, and Support Ops to
Support. Our tooling, processes, and data insights were siloed and did not
support our W ays of W orking between functions in our markets in an
eﬀective manner. RevOps was therefore established with the following
vision: “Facilitate the alignment between M arketing, Sales, Partnerships, and
Customer Success across the customer lifecycle in order to drive growth through
operational eﬃciency and keep all teams accountable to revenue.”
As a team, our ambition was two-fold:
1.D eﬁne a GT M  execution blueprint and operationalize that across all
regions.
2.Build scalable processes and tracking that enable revenue functions to
analyze the success of our strategy across markets and customer segments.
e expectation was that by delivering on the promise of our ambition, our
G T M  motion would experience improvements across many areas, which

---

## Page 89

would also aﬀect the bottom line. e areas include:
•Identiﬁcation and capture of essential data metrics at every step of the
merchant’s journey.
•Capitalizing on the most strategic opportunities and engaging our target
accounts.
•Enabling teams to handle more opportunities without sacriﬁcing quality
or eﬀectiveness.
•Capitalize on global synergies and exchange of best practices from
markets.
•Tracking the eﬀectiveness of tactics and making informed decisions about
eﬀort investments.
•Improved merchant experience.
•Validation and iteration of our GT M  strategy through consolidated
insights.
e key to a successful roadmap execution is clear prioritization and project
scoping. At Adyen, our plans follow ﬁrst principles thinking and a culture of
“launch fast and iterate.” By using the Value/Eﬀort matrix and RACI,
RevOps was able to stack rank initiatives in a way that secured buy-in early
on from end users and leadership because we prioritized “easy wins” over big
bets and incremental ﬁxes. e buy-in opened the door for more
involvement by the business, adoption of new processes, and willingness to
collaborate. After a year of re-building, automating, optimization, and
training, we closed 2024 with two key achievements: our new pipeline
generation went up by 30%, and we doubled our average deal size compared
to 2023.

---

## Page 90

Figure 3.6: H ow Adyen drives SaaS eﬃciency
Project Managem ent for Balanced Execution
W hen you log in to LinkedIn as a RevOps professional, chances are you’ll see a
meme of someone in RevOps ﬁghting ﬁres. ese memes typically reference
RevOps’ overwhelming day-to-day operational tasks with no time for strategic
projects that are part of the roadmap.

---

## Page 91

Figure 3.7: M eme of a RevOps’ Typical D ay in RevOps
17
To avoid ﬁre ﬁghting, start with project management.
Project management is simply the act of planning, organizing, and
managing a project to achieve a predeﬁned goal. at can be something small,
such as organizing a new sales training, or something as big as implementing a
new CRM . M ore likely, it will be both—and ten other projects. Since RevOps
works cross-functionally on dozens of projects with various stakeholders,
keeping track of every project and subtask is close to impossible without good
project management. e risk of forgetting something is too big.
e process of project management forces you also to plan as you literally
have to create a project plan. e beneﬁts of the planning phase are multifold:
they help you identify dependencies on projects. D ependencies impact resource
allocation, and they allow you to distribute resources eﬀectively and identify
risks before they occur.
e great beneﬁts of this process are that it creates transparency. You’ll be
better able to understand the timeline of the project and it creates visibility.
G ood project management helps your stakeholders understand the scope better.

---

## Page 92

It helps prioritize your projects and, most importantly, helps you to say no. is
reduces the amount of ﬁres you need to put out.
Begin With the Right Tools
e best starting point is to use project management software (PM S). If you
already have a PM S in your organization, use that. If you don’t have one, I
recommend ClickUp.
No matter what you choose, create a habit of adding all of the projects
you’re working on to it. Be sure to add a due date, the priority, and add an
assignee. ese few steps already help you plan accordingly. Next, create
dependencies between tasks and indicate if they wait for each other or even
block one another. For example, a data migration can’t start before the
integration is set up.
Transforming Project Managem ent
Principles into Revenue Operations
Excellence
By Richard de Veer, D irector of Revenue Operations - Corporate Solutions @ Just
Eat T akeaway
“Project management was the foundation or starting point of our RevOps
journey. As we built the B2B department, the ﬁrst Project M anagers led key
initiatives such as CRM  system implementation, website development, and
invoicing workﬂows. Over time, this evolved into a robust RevOps function,
yet the same project management principles remain essential to balance
strategy with execution.
To keep operations smooth and strategic goals on track, here are a few
Project Management Principles we still use today:

---

## Page 93

1.D eﬁne and agree on a clear scope and ensure all stakeholders are bought
in. Formal kickoﬀs create alignment on the why and the what and
prevent scope creep.
2.W ork Breakdown Structures (W BS) help map out the diﬀerent tasks
required and deﬁne milestones to create clarity for the execution of what
needs to happen and when. is ensures we look at initiatives holistically
and uncover blind spots.
3.Change management and communication, including tracking progress,
keeping stakeholders engaged, and ensuring buy-in, help to keep
momentum and remove blockers along the way.
To align execution with strategy, we adopted an OKR -driven approach. By
breaking our mission into objectives with measurable key results, we make
sure the programs (projects and initiatives) we deploy contribute to the
desired outcomes. rough quarterly OKR  reviews, we adjust initiatives,
ensuring RevOps stays aligned with business goals or changing priorities and
provide insights around areas for improvement in our G T M  that help reﬁne
our strategy.”
W hen to Hire a Project Manager for RevOps
As your business grows, you will work on more strategic projects, and the
tactical demand will continue to increase. Over time, the need for project
management will appear, and it’ll be time to hire dedicated project managers for
your RevOps department. is becomes a good idea when you reach around
200 employees.
e objective of a project manager is to ensure eﬀective communication and
organization throughout the lifecycle of projects. ey excel in both verbal and
written communication, facilitate clarity among team members, and promptly
escalate issues to key stakeholders when necessary. By meticulously organizing
details, they create project scope, objectives, and key milestones. ey identify
crucial dependencies and establish realistic yet ambitious timelines.

---

## Page 94

As the complexity of projects grow, it’s not just about having the right
people in place, but also about deﬁning clear roles and responsibilities to keep
things moving eﬃciently. is is where the RACI framework becomes
invaluable.
The Power of the RACI Fram ework
In RevOps, most projects are cross-functional with a wide range of
stakeholders. Everyone will have an opinion, and you should want to include a
lot of people to drive the adoption of projects. At the same time, there is a risk
that projects will stop moving forward with too many people involved.
D ecision paralysis with too many stakeholders is real.
W hat can you do to manage this better?
e RACI framework can help.
e Responsible, Accountable, Consulted, and Informed (RACI)
framework is a project management tool used to clarify roles and responsibilities
within a project. It helps to avoid confusion, streamline communication, and
ensure accountability within a project.
To apply the RACI framework, identify all of the tasks and milestones.
en, identify everyone who should be involved.
Input this information into a RACI table. In each row, you write down the
deliverables. In the columns, add the roles. For each task, assign the appropriate
letter (R, A, C, I) to each role. RACI stands for:
•Responsible: e person who is responsible for completing the task.
•Accountable: e person who is accountable for the outcome of the task.
•Consulted: e person who is consulted for their input on the task.
•Informed: e person who is informed about the status of the task.
Here’s what it could look like:

---

## Page 95

A few best practices include:
•Have only one Accountable (A) person per task to avoid multiple levels of
oversight.
•Ensure the Accountable (A) assignees have the authority to ensure the
task is completed.
•Avoid having too many Consulted (C) roles, as they can slow down the
process.
•Focus the RACI matrix on major tasks, milestones, and decisions. It’s not
meant to include every minor detail.
Once done, the key is to get stakeholder input for your RACI matrix and
have it conﬁrmed by them.
Everyone should agree on it before the project starts, as that will avoid
awkward discussions later on. Clearly, you can modify the matrix as the project
goes on and add stakeholders. However, don’t change it too much, as it defeats
the purpose. A good reason to change it is that a new stakeholder has joined the
company. A bad reason is a manager just wants to be consulted. Hence, it’s
important to get conﬁrmation from the start from key stakeholders and stick to
it.
W hile a framework can help streamline projects, you’re still going to have an
inﬂux of requests from various departments come in. at requires a diﬀerent
level of organization, which is where ticketing and backlog management comes
into play.

---

## Page 96

A Smarter Approach to Request Managem ent
W hen you work in RevOps, everyone wants a piece of you. M arketing is
waiting for a report, Sales is waiting for new territory, Customer Success is not
happy with the Health Score calculation, and the CEO needs a report that
should be done by tomorrow for the board. at’s the downside if you are a
support function for all GT M  teams!
To process all incoming requests, you need a ticketing system.
Building an Organized Workﬂow with
Ticketing Systems
A ticketing system is a structured and organized approach to managing and
tracking incoming requests, issues, or tasks. It provides a centralized platform
for logging, processing, and resolving various types of inquiries, whether they
come from external customers or internal stakeholders.
W ithout a ticketing system, there’s a higher risk of forgotten tasks,
misprioritization, or lack of accountability. For these reasons and more,
ticketing systems are commonly used in RevOps. Implementing a ticketing
system is highly recommended for teams of any size to ensure clear organization
and responsibility.
e set-up of a ticketing system does not need to be complex. e key
requirements are that your stakeholders can create requests without friction and
that your RevOps team can easily access them. For your stakeholders, I
recommend the team submit the tickets in whatever communication tool they
use (such as Slack or M icrosoft Teams).
Pro Tip: T ry to stick to one communication channel. If not, make sure to inform
your stakeholders about any requests sent per direct message (or from anyw here
else), or those tickets might not be processed.

---

## Page 97

For your RevOps team, the tickets should arrive in your project
management software. e most common project management tools oﬀer this
integration out of the box. Once set up, your team will receive a constant inﬂow
of support tickets. W hat could possibly go wrong?
e dreaded backlog.
Conquering the Pile-U p
A backlog in ticketing is a situation where there are more incoming requests
than the Support team can handle within a reasonable timeframe.
A backlog is not necessarily a bad thing; it depends on the reasons why. It
can accumulate due to various factors, such as high demand, insuﬃcient
support resources, or ineﬃciencies in the ticketing process. e key is to
manage your backlog, and that starts with information capture.
Each item is an internal client request. ese should be written in a
standardized format to ensure clarity and consistency. Each ticket includes a
description, acceptance criteria, and any relevant details or attachments that
help clarify what needs to be done. en, the items in the backlog are
prioritized based on various factors such as business value, urgency, complexity,
and dependencies.
Lastly, each item in the backlog should have a time estimate for completion.
In its simplest form, here’s what it can look like:

---

## Page 98

You want to give your team some guidance on how they should submit
requests, and it should be included in the task description. Train your team on
it and explain that incomplete requests will be returned. is is important to
avoid processing delays. Similarly, everyone should have the same
understanding of the priority system. W hat one person sees as a high priority
may actually be a medium priority. e team should also be aware that RevOps
is able to re-adjust the priority.
However, make sure to inform your stakeholders about such changes to
keep them happy.
D on’t forget to add a time estimate to your backlog. Estimates are crucial in
allocating resources to a task and for prioritization. In the example above, you
wouldn’t necessarily prioritize tasks 01 and 02. Rather, you would get task 03
out of the way because the time commitment is the lowest. e team should be
able to make it a priority, and in doubt, the owner of the backlog has to decide.
Your backlog should have one owner who is responsible for managing the
backlog, ideally a team lead or manager of the RevOps team. eir role is
multifaceted, and the person will handle disputes around the backlog. W hen
you deal with multiple stakeholders, it’s certain that one stakeholder may
complain about why their requests are taking longer than others. For the team,
the owner should give guidance on how to prioritize tasks and monitor the
health of the backlog. e health can include anything from completing tickets,

---

## Page 99

checking the workload against the capacity, or reviewing stale and/or outdated
items.
e companies I engage with that have the smoothest running operations
all have ticketing systems. It’s not just beneﬁcial for RevOps, but for any team
that’s processing requests. You can also use the Value Eﬀort M atrix that we saw
earlier to prioritize incoming tickets!
e beneﬁts of prioritization, resource allocation, and eﬃciency speak for
themselves. M ost importantly, a well-managed ticketing system and backlog
will save the mental health of your team.
Socratic Reﬂections
e following reﬂections help you to further turn your vision into action:
•W hat is your vision for your RevOps department? How conﬁdent are you
that you can get there?
•W hat needs to change in your business to stay competitive in two years?
•D o you have an AI strategy for GT M  and RevOps?
•How could you measure success in your business?
•W hen was the last time you were overwhelmed at work with too many
projects? W hat could have made the situation better?
•W hat concrete steps could you take to improve project management?
