## Page 100

CHAPTER 4
ADJUSTING THE ARC –
CHANGE MANAGEMENT &
COMMU NICATION
“e most important thing in communication is to hear what isn’t being said.”
Peter Drucker
How often have you found yourself in a position when the path forward was
clear to you but not for others?
Chances are, when you are in RevOps, you’ll likely have a deeper
understanding of the entire business than anyone else in the company. M ore
speciﬁcally, your knowledge of revenue will surpass even that of the CEO. is
makes sense because, in RevOps, you look at all business processes related to
creating better integration, alignment, and collaboration. Nobody else in the
business does this.
is knowledge is a superpower.
But with great power comes great responsibility.
It’s a classical case of information asymmetry, where one party has more
information than the other. In this case, the RevOps team has more
information than other G T M  stakeholders—be that M arketing, Sales, or
Customer Success.

---

## Page 101

Figure 4.1: Asymmetric Information in RevOps
In business, you want to avoid the case with asymmetric information as it
creates undesired outcomes. Asymmetric information becomes a huge problem
when an external party is dealing with an internal party. For example, when
you purchase a house and the seller has more information about the house
than you (think rusty pipes). e seller has a clear advantage in the
negotiation, and you face the risk of purchasing a house with a fault. To reduce
the asymmetric information, the buyer has the option of hiring a third-party
expert to examine the house.
In RevOps, what often happens is that other G T M  stakeholders can’t ﬁnd
agreement when approaching a problem. e solution may be clear for
someone in RevOps, but the rest of the G T M  stakeholders don’t have the same
information. at means you have to communicate better with your G T M
stakeholders.
at’s what this chapter is all about. W e will look at diﬀerent stakeholders,
how to become a better communicator, and how to drive change within an
organization.
Emb racing Change in RevOps

---

## Page 102

e concept of change management most likely started in ancient G reece
when Heraclitus proclaimed: “Change is the only constant in life.” e ideas of
the great ancient philosopher changed how humanity saw the world forever.
But those ideas were not always welcome. From the beginning, they faced
resistance, as people did not like change. ey were ridiculed, exiled, or even
executed for their progressive views. W hy was there such resistance to it?
No one is immune to this resistance. It can be as simple as changing the
side of the bed we sleep on, the route we use to commute to work, or which
Spotify playlist we listen to. Resisting change is part of human nature.
For us in RevOps, this can become a problem because we are
changemakers in a business. To make it harder, we have to drive change
without direct authority over the parts of the business. W e can not just dictate
the change we want to see in the business; we have to work with a wide set of
tools to drive it.
e ﬁrst step is to understand where the resistance is coming from.
Figure 4.2 Psychological, Social, and Organizational Factors Impact on Change

---

## Page 103

Psychological Factors
Change leads to the unknown, and people fear what they don’t understand.
Stepping into the unknown requires people to leave their comfort zone. People
often work years to build habits and routines. From their perspective, change
takes that safe space away from them. e consequence is a perceived loss of
control over the environment they feel comfortable in. is can turn into a real
loss of job changes, their status, or job security.
A large portion of our society sees their job as a safe space. It’s a strong part
of their identity. at’s no surprise, as we spend around 35% of our waking
hours at work.18 Having this strong link to our own identity triggers strong
emotional responses. e fear of not being able to adapt to new changes or
failing in a new environment can be a signiﬁcant deterrent. People would
rather attach themselves to the way things have always been. Change takes
safety away.
In RevOps, you have to be aware that you’re also dealing with strong
psychological factors when new changes are proposed. Challenging someone’s
identity is something people don’t like. It’s scary. Being aware of this helps
mitigate it.
Social Factors
I’m sure you’ve heard this statement before: “is is always how we have done it
here.”
You might ﬁnd that some inﬂuential individuals might be against a change.
is tends to impact the behavior of others. Rarely do people want to go
against their tribe. ere needs to be a high degree of trust in the people who
are driving the change inside the organization. A lack of trust will inhibit the
acceptance of change.
Organizational Factors

---

## Page 104

Change can also fail due to practical factors. ere can be a lack of resources to
make the change a reality. It can be money, but it can also be time and eﬀort.
It might be ampliﬁed by any previous negative experiences the team has
had. If they were poorly managed or led to negative outcomes, resistance to
new ones might be higher. Failed change processes are often marked by
insuﬃcient participation. If individuals feel they are not part of the change
process or their input is not valued, they are more likely to resist.
Lastly, poorly planned and executed change processes lead to resistance.
Eﬀective change management involves clear communication, support, and
involvement of those aﬀected. Let’s not make those mistakes.
Applying Lewin’s Three-Stage Model to
Modern Change Managem ent
In our current era, changemakers did not face such extreme consequences like
the ancient G reek philosophers, at least not in a corporate setting. However,
resistance to change slows down innovation, and that’s why it has become a
subject of modern academia. e G erman-American psychologist K urt Lewin
is seen as the creator of change management. In the 1940s, he developed a
three-stage model of change.19
e model states that change goes through a three-step process. To explain
the model, Lewin uses the analogy of unfreezing a block of ice, pouring it into
a mold, and refreezing it into a new shape.
Figure 4.3 Lewin’s ree-Stage Change M odel: U nfreeze, Change, Refreeze

---

## Page 105

In the U nfreezing stage, everyone involved needs to accept that change is
necessary. It requires breaking down the status quo by addressing concerns and
the needs of stakeholders, and by encouraging open communication and
feedback. In the Change stage, the actual transformation takes place. Here, the
involvement of stakeholders and leadership in the change process to gain their
input is key. is reduces resistance and gets buy-in.
In the Refreezing stage, the goal is to solidify the new state after the change
occurs. is is done by reinforcing new processes, behaviors, and cultural
norms through modeling, rewards, and embedding them in the organization’s
practices and policies.
e change has to be fully integrated into the organization and it has to be
monitored.
For example, when we decided to move from the BANT  to M ED D IC sales
qualiﬁcation framework, I knew it required more than a simple switch. e
Sales team used BANT  for years, but it failed. I used Lewin’s Change M odel to
make the switch.
In the U nfreezing stage, I focused on creating awareness. I shared data
showing how BANT  was limiting us. D eals were stalling because it missed
critical elements like understanding the economic buyer or true pain points.
rough workshops and discussions, I listened to the team’s frustrations and
concerns. is open dialogue helped everyone see why the change wasn’t just
necessary but beneﬁcial.
e Change stage was where we began the actual transformation. Training
was key. I worked to design sessions that not only explained M ED D IC but
showed its practical application to our deals. W e also piloted M ED D IC with a
small group, which allowed us to reﬁne the process and gather early wins to
share with the team. At the same time, we updated our CRM  to integrate
M ED D IC ﬁelds, making it easier for the team to adopt the new framework.
Regular feedback loops ensured we could address challenges quickly and keep
momentum.
In the Refreezing stage, we focused on embedding M ED D IC into our
culture. Celebrating early successes was crucial because it built trust in the
framework. I ensured M ED D IC was integrated into onboarding, playbooks,

---

## Page 106

and coaching, so it became second nature. By reinforcing the change and
demonstrating its impact, we didn’t just implement M ED D IC; we made it the
foundation of how we qualify leads and close deals.
is process reminded me that change isn’t just about tools or frameworks;
it’s about people. By involving the team at every step, we turned a necessary
shift into a shared success. Lewin’s Change M odel presented it beautifully in a
practical framework.
But there is one aspect that doesn’t receive enough attention in Lewin’s
Change M odel: emotions.
Applying the Kübler-Ross Change Curve
W hile Lewin’s Change M odel provides a structural approach to implementing
change, it doesn’t fully address individuals’ emotional journey during this
process. is is where the Kübler-Ross Change Curve becomes invaluable.
e K übler-Ross Change Curve outlines how individuals typically respond
to signiﬁcant changes over time. U nderstanding these stages helps leaders
manage the emotional and psychological resistance to change, which is critical
for successful implementation. Originally conceptualized by Elisabeth K übler-
Ross in her work on grief,20 this model has been adapted to explain the
emotional responses to change in a business context.
It’s seen multiple adaptations over the years and can have anywhere from
ﬁve to eight stages (also called morals in her work). Below is a short description
of each of the eight stages.
1.Shock: is is the initial reaction to change. People are often taken
aback and may experience disbelief, feeling unprepared for the sudden
shift. It highlights the need to communicate upcoming changes to
minimize the intensity of this reaction.
2.D enial: Following shock, individuals may enter a state of denial. ey
might refuse to acknowledge the change, cling to the past, and hope
things will revert to how they once were. To move past this stage, open

---

## Page 107

communication and clear, factual information about the necessity and
beneﬁts of the change are crucial.
3.Anger: As denial fades, anger can surface. Individuals might direct their
frustration towards those leading the change, feeling that their routine
and stability are being disrupted. Acknowledging these feelings and
oﬀering support can help manage this stage, preventing it from
derailing the change process.
4.Bargaining: In this stage, individuals attempt to avoid the change by
negotiating a compromise. ey may propose alternative solutions or
seek to delay the change. Leaders should listen to these concerns but
also reinforce the reasons for the change, ensuring the organization
remains on course.
5.D epression: Realizing that bargaining won’t alter the course of change,
individuals may experience feelings of sadness, helplessness, or
depression. is stage is characterized by low morale and productivity.
Providing emotional support, counseling, and fostering a supportive
environment can help individuals navigate this diﬃcult period.
6.Exploration: In this stage, individuals actively seek to understand the
change better. ey explore new roles, processes, and possibilities that
change brings. is period of exploration helps them see the potential
beneﬁts and opportunities, fostering a more open and positive attitude
toward the change.
7.Acceptance: Eventually, individuals begin to accept the change. ey
start to understand its implications and begin to let go of the past.
Acceptance marks the beginning of a more positive outlook, where
individuals start to see potential beneﬁts and opportunities brought by
the change.

---

## Page 108

8.Integration: Finally, the new reality becomes the norm. Individuals
integrate the change into their daily routines and organizational culture.
ey embrace new ways of working, and it’s fully embedded in the
organization. Continuous reinforcement and recognition of the new
behaviors and processes are essential to sustain this integration.
You don’t need to become an expert in all eight stages. Anastasiia Binns,
Head of RevOps at Semble, introduced a simpliﬁcation to the model to make
it easier. She categorizes people experiencing negative emotional states as
detractors and those in positive emotional states as promoters. Your projects
have a higher chance to succeed if you approach these two groups diﬀerently.
As Anastasiia puts it: “e binary split between promoters and detractors helps
make K übler-Ross’ model immediately actionable. Your promoters will race through
the change curve. Pay attention to what makes adoption easy for them, as these
insights are gold when working with detractors. Spot your detractors early, and you
can get ahead of their objections before they become blockers.”

---

## Page 109

Figure 4.4 D etractors and Promoters in the K übler-Ross Change Curve
e key takeaway is the recognition of the emotional journey during a
change process. As you can see, there are ups and downs. An eﬀective RevOps
department must prioritize empathy and support as key elements of successful
change management.
U nderstanding the emotional journey of change lays a foundation for
eﬀective communication and support. But to truly drive successful change, you
need a clear strategy for engaging the right individuals. is is where
stakeholder analysis comes in.
Stakeholder Analysis for Change
Let’s say you’ve decided to roll out a new tool to streamline your product
feedback process. Since you operate in Europe and serve many clients in the
ﬁnance industry, data privacy is a top priority.

---

## Page 110

You go ahead and shortlist vendors, spend weeks evaluating them, and
ﬁnally get conﬁrmation from the ones you like. Feeling good, you send the
proposal over to the Head of Security, only for him to drop a bombshell: all
data must stay within Europe. It’s a security requirement you hadn’t
considered. At this point, you’ve just wasted nearly a month of work.
at happened to me once. No one from the business side knew about this
strict requirement, and even though the vendors were based in Europe, some
had sub-processors in the U S, which was a dealbreaker. It was a huge waste of
time and resources.
And it could have been easily avoided with stakeholder analysis.
Stakeholder analysis is a systematic process for identifying and prioritizing
individuals and groups aﬀected by a proposed change. U nderstanding their
interests, inﬂuence, and impact helps you create strategies to engage them,
resolve concerns, and gain their support. is increases the likelihood of
successful change implementation.
Stakeholder analysis might feel natural in a smaller business with 50
employees, where you know everyone by name (or even their nickname). But
in a company with over 500 people, you probably don’t know everyone well
enough, and that’s when stakeholder analysis really matters.
Remember, everyone’s got their own needs and concerns, such as data
security. How you engage them will vary, too, whether through one-on-one
conversations, workshops, focus groups, or advisory panels. Plus, building
relationships with potential champions within those groups can make a big
diﬀerence; they can inﬂuence others and help move the change forward.
e ﬁrst step in stakeholder analysis boils down to identifying all of them.
If we circle back to the example above (where you’ve decided to roll out a
new product feedback process), possible stakeholders might be:
Stakeholders W hy
COO Project owner
Head of Customer Success Her responsibility
Head of Product Touches product

---

## Page 111

Product M anager 1 Speciﬁc product area 1
Product M anager 2 Speciﬁc product area 2
Customer Success M anager 1 Client interaction experience
Customer Success M anager 2 Client interaction experience
Head of IT As a potential tool
Head of Security As new data capture is required
CRM  admin U nderstand current capabilities
RevOps M anager Project execution
Test Customer 1 For testing
In this example, there are 12 stakeholders involved at various touchpoints.
In reality, there may be even more! To ensure the success of the project, it’s
crucial to understand each stakeholder’s role, interests, and inﬂuence on the
outcome.
Stakeholders can include individuals, groups, departments, and external
entities. It’s essential to categorize them into internal stakeholders, such as
employees, managers, and executives, as well as external stakeholders, like
customers, suppliers, and partners.
Figure 4.5 Example of Internal vs External Stakeholders
W ith a clear list of stakeholders in place, it’s time to understand their needs
and the power they hold within the change process.
Assess Stakeholder Interests and Inﬂuence

---

## Page 112

is process involves understanding what each stakeholder stands to gain or
lose from the change. It is a deep dive into their motivations, concerns, and
expectations. Simultaneously, it’s crucial to determine the level of inﬂuence
each stakeholder holds over the change process. is inﬂuence can be based on
their position, expertise, network, or control over resources.
By doing this, you’ll learn who needs to be given the most attention so you
can gain their input from the start.
Here’s how to think through this:
Action Power Interest
ese are the key players who must be fully
engaged and managed closely.
High High
ese stakeholders should be kept satisﬁed;
engage them enough to maintain their
support.
High Low
K eep these stakeholders informed to ensure
they understand the change and its beneﬁts.
Low High
M onitor these stakeholders; engage them
minimally, but keep them informed as
necessary.
Low Low
Once you do that, it’s time to prioritize each stakeholder and plot them on
a power-interest grid: “power” on one axis and “interest” on the other.
For a Project M anager, it will help visualize who needs the most attention
and how to prioritize concerns to understand where potential risks may be.

---

## Page 113

Figure 4.6 e Power Interest G rid to M anage Stakeholders
Once this is done, you should have a clear understanding of how each
stakeholder impacts your project. is knowledge can potentially save you
hours of work.
Be sure to regularly check in with your stakeholders and monitor the
changes in their attitudes and level of support. Be prepared to adapt your
approach based on feedback and the evolving dynamics of the change process.
is ﬂexibility ensures the change initiative stays on track. Lastly, always

---

## Page 114

consider your communication plan and how the message per stakeholder
group may diﬀer. For example, a CRO may care about how a new CRM
improves reporting and forecasting, while Sales Representatives (Reps) may be
more focused on how it helps them be more productive. Consider these
elements in your communication.
Once you’ve identiﬁed the stakeholders involved and mapped out the
impact they have, it’s time to create a stakeholder engagement strategy—the
blueprint for how you’ll approach stakeholders.
Crafting Stakeholder Strategies
At M yG ym24 (a ﬁctional ﬁtness SaaS company I just made up), leadership
implements a new CRM  system to scale growth. M aria, the Head of RevOps,
takes charge and gathers the senior executives for a formal meeting. She shows
the strategic beneﬁts and paints a clear picture of increased sales eﬃciency and
improved customer retention. Her conﬁdent, data-driven presentation shows
how the CRM  ﬁts into the company’s long-term goals, and the leadership team
sees its value and potential.
Next, M aria brings the middle managers into an interactive workshop. She
breaks down the practical beneﬁts, showing how the CRM  simpliﬁes their
daily tasks and reduces administrative burdens. By encouraging open
discussion, she listens to their input and handles concerns about the transition.
Finally, M aria meets with frontline employees in informal team meetings.
She eases their concerns and highlights how the new system makes their jobs
easier and more rewarding. By oﬀering hands-on training and ongoing
support, she builds conﬁdence and boosts enthusiasm.
is story shows the strength of a strong stakeholder engagement strategy.
M aria applies diﬀerent communication techniques and tailored messages to
unite everyone around a common goal.
Let’s dive into how to do that for your business.
Finding Executive Sponsors and Advocates

---

## Page 115

RevOps operates by driving impact across the entire revenue organization
despite having limited authority. W e want to align diﬀerent teams and change
how a company operates. W e do this for the best interest of the business and
our clients. In theory, everyone in the business has the same goal.
But in reality, people are driven by their own motivations.
e leaders of each department that RevOps supports have their own
priorities, targets, and career goals. is is completely normal. Although we all
work towards common goals, don’t assume that everyone will welcome your
ideas with open arms. Front-line employees may not welcome every change
you propose; they’ve developed processes that work for them. Sure, there might
be a better way to do it, but if they just get told what to do, chances are high
that the better solution will fail due to poor adoption.
At some point, RevOps becomes political. You have to adjust to this, and
one eﬀective way is to ﬁnd executive sponsors and advocates for your projects.
An executive sponsor is a senior leader who believes in your project.
Sometimes, this person will be easy to identify because they’ll bring a project to
you. Other times, you need to ﬁnd one. A good starting point is to ﬁnd
someone who has objectives that align with yours. For example, your goal
might be to improve the hand-oﬀ process between Sales and Customer
Success. e Customer Success leader will beneﬁt greatly from this as they have
a better start with their customer. Your objectives are aligned, and your projects
should have a positive impact on their work. Once they stand behind it, they
are likely to drive projects with other leaders.
e next key step is to identify and convert advocates. Advocates are those
who are often passionate about day-to-day operations. You can identify these
individuals because they are likely to give feedback, are recognized as experts
by their peers, and are power users of the systems or processes you are working
on. To get them behind your projects, you need to understand their pain
points, show them the beneﬁts of the new solution, and involve them early on
in the process.
All of that will help make them advocates for your project.
Lastly, never forget to give recognition to those who contribute to a
project. Praise goes a long way.

---

## Page 116

Involving Stakeholders Early
Another powerful method to drive change in an environment without direct
authority over stakeholders is to involve them early on so your stakeholders feel
heard. is increases their commitment and reduces resistance. It helps them
feel valued and part of the journey rather than being passive recipients of the
change.
A great way to handle this is to introduce pilot programs for your
programs. For example, if there is a new process that you want to try out with
a small group of people, have them be your advocates. Another method is to
create an interactive workshop where stakeholders can discuss the proposed
changes, provide feedback, and suggest improvements. is not only generates
valuable insights but also fosters a sense of ownership.
The Importance of a Com m unication Plan
A communication plan is a strategic document that outlines how information
will be shared with stakeholders. e example with M aria earlier illustrates
how she engaged senior executives, middle managers, and frontline employees
diﬀerently but with the same end goal. All of this is the hallmark of a good
communication plan.
Your communication plan needs a clear objective. is includes stating
what you will do and why. M aria’s communication plan highlights why
implementing a new CRM  will help tackle new growth requirements. e
purpose of the communication plan is always the same, but diﬀerent beneﬁts
can be highlighted for diﬀerent stakeholders, as we have seen in the example
earlier.

---

## Page 117

Figure 4.7 Components of a Communication Plan
One of the beneﬁts of completing the stakeholder analysis is that it can be
used to tailor the communication plan to diﬀerent stakeholders. It’s not
limited to senior executives, middle managers, and frontline employees. Since
M arketing, Sales, and Customer Success all have diﬀerent priorities, your
communication plan can diﬀer for each department. Highlighting the project’s
beneﬁts for each function will further strengthen its adoption.
W here to Share the Plan
It not only matters how you say it but also where you say it. ere’s usually a
wide range of communication channels to choose from. Should it be in a small
group or a big group? W ill it be through online communication or in person?
Should the content be written, recorded, or both?
In most cases, it will be a combination of various channels and formats. For
example, the change can be announced during an all-hands meeting and then

---

## Page 118

summarized in an internal newsletter. en, you can schedule dedicated
working sessions that provide more detailed information about the project.
e key idea here is that your message gets enough repetition. Some people
will miss an initial announcement, while others will not fully understand it.
Some might even ignore it. at’s totally ﬁne. at’s why a communication
plan is important. You want to ensure that your message reaches everyone
eventually and that it receives support.
D on’t leave the last part to chance.
You want feedback for your communication plan. You can do this by
periodically:
•Sending out a survey after major communication to understand if your
stakeholders received all the information they need.
•W orking with feedback forms or creating a general questionnaire to send
to stakeholders during the project.
•U sing informal meetings or coﬀee chats to get a pulse check from your
stakeholders.
In RevOps, we are structured and organized when it comes to project
delivery. Applying the same diligence to communication will skyrocket the
success of projects.
Communication is not just important to drive change, but it’s one of the
most important skills in RevOps. at is why in the rest of the chapter, I’ll
hone in on communication skills.
Meetings for Alignm ent
Nobody likes spending all day in meetings. It can feel unproductive. However,
RevOps should not neglect the power of meetings. ey are a powerful tool for
alignment.
e modern sales landscape demands more than isolated eﬀorts from
G T M  teams. W hen these teams operate in silos, it often results in missed

---

## Page 119

opportunities and a disjointed customer journey. M eetings bring people
together in this disjoined environment.
A typical company has various kinds of meetings, such as stand-ups, weekly
team meetings, forecasting meetings, or sales calls, among others. But are you
familiar with GT M  M eetings?
GTM Meetings
RevOps is about the alignment, collaboration, and integration of the G T M
teams. One eﬀective way to do this is to have GT M  meetings.
RevOps is in a great position to facilitate these meetings and transform the
outcomes into eﬀective processes. e meetings can happen bi-weekly or
weekly, depending on your needs. RevOps can help prepare the agenda and
involve the relevant stakeholders in the meetings.
G T M  meetings can have diﬀerent scopes. ey can be strategic, tactical, or
operational.
Example topics can include:
•Customer Journey M apping
•Customer feedback
•Account-based marketing
•High-value deals
•Hand-oﬀ process
e members of these G T M  meetings will be dependent on scope, but
they should all be relevant to your G T M  strategy. Participants may include
representatives from M arketing, Sales, Customer Support, Product,
Partnerships, Finance, or Legal. Overall, these can be seen as G T M  leadership
meetings, so having department leaders attend is a good starting point.
You can invite subject matter experts from speciﬁc teams to the meetings,
depending on the topic. e important point is not to include anyone who is

---

## Page 120

not relevant to the topic of conversation; nobody has time for a meeting for
the sake of the meeting.
Meeting Cadence
I highly recommend adding G T M  meetings to your meeting cadence. A
meeting cadence is a representation of which meetings occur at what time,
who is attending, and why. is helps to create visibility for meetings and
avoid awkward moments where a stakeholder asks: “W hy was I not invited to
the meeting?” e table below shows what such a meeting cadence could look
like.
Commu nication Skills for Transform ation
I have talked with hundreds of RevOps professionals, and the most successful
ones have one trait in common:
ey are all great communicators.
eir paths to success all diﬀered: some climbed the corporate ladder, while
others launched their own businesses. Either way, their success is attributed to
their communication skills. is doesn’t mean they lack technical skills. ey
simply recognized early on that success requires strong communication.

---

## Page 121

At the start of this chapter, we zeroed in on M aria’s communication plan.
It’s a reminder of the importance of sharing information in a way so all parties
can consider the implications. W e don’t want to bulldoze our way to a
solution; that’s a recipe for failure. Instead, we have to ﬁnd ways to
communicate with our stakeholders to share our knowledge and understand
their points of view. Only then can we come to a consensus.
Communicating well becomes harder as the organization grows. One
aspect of human nature is that there’s a strive for power, along with a need for
authority. is starts in smaller organizations and ampliﬁes as the organization
grows in size, and more layers of management are added. e need becomes
even bigger to understand the intrinsic motivation of each stakeholder to ﬁnd
consensus.
For us in RevOps, especially those who want to become a RevOps leader,
developing excellent communication skills is essential. e majority of your
projects are cross-functional. M ost of the time, you are not their ultimate
owner. You need to support all of the stakeholders to accomplish your goals.
is is why stakeholder management skills are so crucial.
M anaging stakeholders requires an understanding of their diﬀerences and
personalities.
To do that, you ﬁrst have to understand yourself.
The Power of Self-Awareness
You can’t understand others if you don’t know who you are. G reat
communicators know their own strengths and weaknesses.
e easiest way to ﬁnd your own is to complete a personality test. e
most popular ones include D ISC, M yers-Briggs, Enneagram, and the Big Five
personality traits. W hat they all have in common is that they follow a
methodology for group characteristics. e goal is to develop a clear language
to describe these traits and identify your strengths and weaknesses within
them. M any companies do a personality test during onboarding or as a team
activity.

---

## Page 122

M y personal preference is the Big Five personality traits, as it’s the most
scientiﬁcally robust. Researchers all over the world from various disciplines,
such as psychology, neurology, marketing, evolutionary biology, or genealogy,
study the Big Five and ﬁnd more evidence for them. As someone who values
scientiﬁc progress, it’s obvious to favor the Big Five over any other personality
test.
e Big Five personality traits are listed below:
Category Synonyms to better understand the concept
Openness To experience, curiosity, novelty seeking, broadmindedness,
and interest in culture, ideas, and aesthetics.
Conscientiousness Self-control, willpower, reliability, consistency, dependability,
trustworthiness, and the ability to delay gratiﬁcation.
Agreeableness W armth, kindness, sympathy, empathy, trust, compliance,
modesty, benevolence or peacefulness.
Stability Especially emotional stability, adaptability, equanimity,
maturity, or stress resistance.
Extraversion Friendly, gregarious, talkative, funny, expressive, assertive,
active, excited, seeking, and being socially self-conﬁdent.
Each category is then measured on a scale. Either you score high in the
category or the complete opposite. For example, in the case of extraversion, an
extreme example would be someone who thrives in social settings and actively
seeks everyone’s attention. ink Jim Carrey, Oprah, or W ill Smith. On the
other end, you have the quieter people who avoid crowds and people as much
as possible. ink George R. Martin or Keanu Reeves.
It’s proven that the Big Five personality traits follow a bell curve with a
normal distribution. In layman terms, most people fall around the average for
these traits, with only a small percentage at the extreme ends.

---

## Page 123

Figure 4.8 Normal D istribution of Big Five Personality Traits: M ajority in the middle.
is also means that the Big Five can not be binary. One is not introverted
or extroverted; there is a degree to each trait, and most people fall somewhere
in the middle. Lastly, it’s important to note that the Big Five traits are not
correlated with one another. is means if one person scores high on stability,
it gives no indication of their openness or extraversion.
e Big Five personality traits give us an indication of the tendencies each
person has. But it’s not ignorant that we as human beings are quite nuanced.
Nonetheless, the personality traits are quite consistent over the lifetime of a
person. Of course, you can work on traits and improve certain ones (if you’re
interested in that). W e’re able to “fake” extrovertism, but this can be
exhausting, and especially in stressful times, it’s easier to return to default
mode.
Big Five Personality Self-Assessm ent
e beautiful part about the Big Five test is that you can complete a self-
assessment in under two minutes. e psychologists Beatrice Rammstein
and Oliver John published a Big Five scale in 2007 called the BFI-10,
which uses just 10 questions.21 e fascinating thing about this test is that

---

## Page 124

taking it again in a few months will yield almost the same result. (For the
geeks: the test-retest correlation is about 0.8. Considering 1 would be an exact
match, then this is damn good). Additionally, the test also correlates very
accurately with the scores on much longer personality tests. (For the geeks
again: the correlation coeﬃcient is 0.82 for longer personality tests). is allows
us to do the self-assessment right now.
Below is a table where you can rate yourself on a scale from 1 to 5 where:
1 = disagree strongly,
2 = disagree a little,
3 = neither agree nor disagree,
4 = agree a little,
5 = agree strongly.
Answer the following statements:
I See Myself as Someone Who
Question Score
1. Has an active imagination. ______
2. Has few artistic interests. ______
3. D oes a thorough job. ______
4. Tends to be lazy. ______
5. Is generally trusting. ______
6. Tends to ﬁnd fault with others. ______
7. Is relaxed, handles stress well. ______
8. G ets nervous easily. ______
9. Is outgoing, sociable. ______

---

## Page 125

10. Is reserved. ______
Two questions are paired for each of the ﬁve categories. To derive the ﬁnal
score for each trait, you need to subtract the even answer from the uneven
answers. Scores can range from -4 (very low on the trait) to +4 ( very high
on the trait), with 0 being about average. For example, if you score a 5 on
the ﬁrst question about active imagination and a 2 on the second question
about artistic interests, then you do the following calculation: 5 minus 2,
which leads to the ﬁnal score of 3 for openness.
U sing the Big Five for RevOps: Personal
Example
Once you understand your Big Five personality traits, you can then reﬂect on
how they impact your private and work life. In this section, I’ll focus on my
work in RevOps based on my score.
Openness (3)
I am always on the lookout for ﬁnding a better way of doing things. I like to
experiment with new tools or processes. I can conceptualize a solution in my
head, and most times, it will work like that in reality. Not because I am blindly

---

## Page 126

conﬁdent that it will work but because I consider nearly every possible scenario
of what might happen. In the space of RevOps, this is very beneﬁcial. But at
the same time, I need to be self-aware that other people may not be as open.
W ith certain stakeholders, I need to express my ideas diﬀerently.
Conscientiousness (3)
For me, the long-term view is very important. How will a project impact the
company’s performance in the long term? Knowing this, I focus on the big
picture, which can also be a limitation. Sometimes, we need a short-term
solution that may be against my preference for a long-term solution. Being
aware of this struggle is helpful to ﬁnd the best result. Another key focus is
building trust with my stakeholders because, without it, achieving our shared
goals becomes impossible.
Agreeableness (0)
On the one side, I am all about processes and compliance of those processes as
they beneﬁt an organization as a whole. But at the same time, I am the ﬁrst
one to break them when they don’t make sense to me. I am also someone with
strong opinions (this is probably why I started my own business, and many
other founders share this trait), and I tend to openly disagree with people. At
the same time, I’m mindful of this, so I choose my battles wisely and only
focus on those worth ﬁghting.
Emotional stability (3)
Over the years, I have become stress-resistant. M y mantra is, “Every deadline is
man-made,” so why should I stress out?
At the same time, I am aware that deadlines can be very serious for others.
It may even give them anxiety. So I take them seriously, but they don’t impact
me. I also know that I am not fully emotionally stable. I feel stressed when I’m
placed in situations beyond my control or faced with problems I cannot solve
but am still expected to resolve. I experienced this situation once with an
employer, and the only possible solution for me was to leave the company. M y
mental health is still grateful for that decision.
Extroversion (-1)

---

## Page 127

I am introverted, which means that I feel exhausted easily in group settings.
Extroverted people gain energy in group settings. is knowledge is helpful in
a work setting. W hen I want to advocate for a new process, I try to speak with
key stakeholders in one-on-one meetings or in small groups. is allows me to
express my ideas better and win over executive sponsors and advocates (which
we discussed earlier in this chapter). Once we are in a larger meeting, there is
less pressure on me to present my idea and be overshadowed by extroverted
people (since I already have supporters on my side who advocate the idea).
Overall, knowing who you are and how it relates with others around you is
a huge beneﬁt in RevOps. You can only impact your relationships with others
when you know who you are.
W hy You Need to U nderstand Others
Once you understand yourself better, you will become more aware of the
diﬀerences in personality traits between people. Some are more introverted
than others and tend to prefer one-on-one communication over extroverts.
Others are less open and require more rational arguments to get behind an
idea. For others, it’s important to make their own experiences. at’s why it’s
important to understand the personality type of your stakeholders.
Nowadays, it’s common for businesses to organize company-wide
personality workshops to increase awareness on this topic. If your company
hasn’t done so yet, then I would recommend talking to your HR team to
organize a workshop for personality traits. It doesn’t matter if it is D ISC,
Enneagram, or the Big Five personality traits. W hat matters is that one
framework should be used company-wide. It would be challenging if one part
of the business uses D ISC and the other the Big Five, as they don’t always
match.
e results of the personality workshop enable three use cases:
1.Better team management.
2.Easier to facilitate change.
3.Be a better colleague.

---

## Page 128

Let’s look at each.
Better Team  Managem ent
As a manager, you are responsible for a team. Your goal is to see them succeed.
To do so, you will be required to understand them. Remember the quote from
Peter D rucker at the start of this chapter: e most important thing in
communication is to hear what isn’t being said.
If you have a better understanding of the personality types of your team
members, it will increase your chances of hearing everyone on your team. For
example, I managed a team once with a wide set of personality types. W hen we
were brainstorming during team meetings, it was common that you always
heard the same people speak. To better engage everyone on the topic, I
consulted the more introverted person prior in a one-on-one setting to hear
their perspective on the topic.
en you have the option to merge this during a meeting: “Sean, thank
you for that point. at relates to what I heard from Tom earlier today. Tom,
can you remind me what you said earlier?” is is a smoother transition to give
the more introverted person a chance to speak up in a safe manner. A manager
is responsible for creating an environment where the team can ﬂourish.
Easier to Facilitate Change
Remember the tools for stakeholder analysis from earlier in the chapter? Now,
you can combine the stakeholder analysis with personality types. It’s similar to
working with your team, but now you are doing it with a larger scale of
stakeholders.
For example, say you want to implement a new tool for sales engagement.
You identiﬁed that some of your stakeholders have a strong logical trait, while
others require more to make their own experience. W ith the logical group, you
want to have a presentation that highlights the rationale for the new tool and
how it will beneﬁt the team. is structured approach will not be so successful
with the experience group. You can invite people for a trial use case so they can
have their own experiences with the tool. is is much more relatable to the
group and will win their support.

---

## Page 129

U nderstanding the personality types of your stakeholders is a powerful tool
for change management.
Be a Better Colleague
In the end, if you better understand the personality types of others, then you
will become a better co-worker. You can even go so far as to say that you
become a better human being.
W e all know people who seem to be at ease in any social situation, and you
can see that manifest in their careers. Some are natural with communication
skills, but chances are that many have learned these skills. I speak here from my
own experience. As mentioned earlier, I am more of an introvert. But I learned
that if I want to drive change, then I need to become better at communication
and stakeholder management.
is becomes especially evident for RevOps leaders. You have to engage
with many stakeholders, but you don’t have the authority to make decisions on
your own. ere is no way around becoming a good communicator.
U nderstanding the personality types of others and yourself is the starting
point. is allows you to have impactful and meaningful conversations with
others.
W ith self-awareness as a foundation, it’s time to focus on practical skills
that can transform your approach to communication.
Being Reactive vs. Proactive
Proactive individuals anticipate challenges and opportunities and can plan and
act in advance. is contrasts with reactive individuals who respond to events
after they occur.
In RevOps, being proactive means anticipating stakeholder needs and
potential project hurdles, allowing you to address them before they become
issues. is proactive approach can lead to smoother project execution and
better stakeholder relationships.
e table below gives an idea of how reactive and proactive RevOps diﬀer.

I will not go through them in detail; the goal of this chapter is to highlight
the importance of change management and communication. However, a single
chapter or dozens of books will not be enough to teach these skills. ey are a
lifetime quest. e key is that becoming a RevOps leader not only enhances
your professional abilities but also helps you grow as a person.
e best way to develop these skills is through consistent practice, and
working with a coach oﬀers the guidance and feedback you need to succeed.
Working W ith a Leadership or
Commu nication Coach
D eveloping your soft skills is hard, especially if you are more introverted and
your strengths are more technical.
ese are common traits in RevOps, as the job attracts these types of
people. So it becomes very important to understand that you’re not homo
economicus—rational agents that make rational decisions. No, we are irrational
beings, making irrational decisions that are more often based on emotion than
reason. To navigate this landscape, you need new skills, and the best way to
develop those is to work with a leadership coach.

---

## Page 131

W orking with a leadership coach was, for me, one of the best decisions to
progress my career. I recommend working with one the moment you start to
lead a team, as from there on out, you will apply more of your soft skills than
technical skills. In RevOps, it’s not only that you work with your own team
but also with a wide set of stakeholders. A leadership coach will help you
manage those relationships to strengthen your position, and you will have
someone you can talk to openly during hard times.
M ost companies pay for leadership coaching once you enter a managerial
role, but don’t wait until it’s oﬀered. Remember to be proactive and ask for it.
Should they not pay for it, then try to negotiate that they pay for at least half
of it. is determination can help to convince your company to pay for all of
it. If this still doesn’t work, then I encourage you to pay for it fully out of your
own pocket. Start with ﬁve sessions and see how useful it is for you. M y bet is
the skills you learn can be transferred to any other job and to the rest of your
life.
Socratic Reﬂections
Reﬂect on these questions to improve self-awareness, strengthen
relationships, and prepare for change:
•ink back on a project that failed to deliver the desired change. How
did you feel?
•Knowing now more about change management, is there something you
would have done diﬀerently?
•W ho are your ﬁve most important internal stakeholders and your three
most important external stakeholders?
•How well do you know them? W hat can you do today to improve the
relationship? Pick one and do what you came up with.
•How will AI change your organization?

---

## Page 132

•Is your team equipped to embrace AI? W hat is missing?
•W ho are you? Do you know how you are perceived by others?
•ink of the last time you took the initiative and received praise. How
did you feel at the moment? Find one new initiative and be proactive
about it.
•W hat would you like to improve in your communication skills? List three
items. Ask ChatG PT  which one is the easiest to improve and to give you
further resources on it. Spend the next 66 days on improving only that
one communication skill.
•W hat is one thing that you can do the next time at work to be a more
kind colleague? Do it.
