Part 2
Mastering the Pendulum ’sWeights
Just like a perfectly balanced pendulum  transfers energy sm oothly, RevOps
relies on four core pillars to sustain m om entum  and drive growth: Enablem ent,
Process, System s, and D ata & Insights. Part 2 explores the role each pillar or
“weight” plays within a business. W hen they work in sync, energy ﬂows
seam lessly, creating the kind of forward m otion that sets high-perform ing
com panies apart.

---

## Page 134

CHAPTER 5
THE FIRST W EIGHT –
ENABLEMENT
“Tell me and I forget, teach me and I may remember, involve me and I learn.”
Benjamin Franklin
M y ﬁrst internship at an architectural ﬁrm was my ﬁrst exposure to great
enablement. W hen I arrived, I remember vividly that one partner sat me in a
room and gave me an architectural textbook to read. He said, “Read as much
as you can in the next three hours. en we’ll talk about it.”
e only reason I remember this is because of what happened next.
A fter half an hour, another partner walked into the oﬃce. He ﬁgured I was
the new intern and asked me point-blank—“why the fuck” was I reading that
book? I explained I was told to learn it. Instead, he told me to follow him and
brought me to an architectural model of a library. It was from a huge project
they had just been awarded. Part of the project was to add a new wing to an
existing library. He explained the requirements for it. “W e still need some
inspiration for the wing. Can you create a model for us in the next two days?”
at experience shaped the essence of enablement for me. Benjamin
Franklin’s words above echo the same ethos: Tell me and I forget, teach me and I
may remember, involve me and I learn.
Enablement is all about providing your G T M  teams with the information,
content, and tools they need to increase their performance. A t its core, it’s

---

## Page 135

about more than just imparting knowledge—it’s about creating an
environment where learning is an active, engaging, and continuous process.
is approach not only enhances the skills and capabilities of individuals but
also drives the collective success of an organization. e partner at the architect
ﬁrm knew the best way for me to learn was to put me to work on a mini-
project and let me ﬁgure it out on my own.
Learning is the lifeblood of any high-performing team. W ith A I at the
forefront, the ability to adapt, grow, and innovate is paramount. is is
especially true for G T M  teams. Eﬀective enablement equips these teams with
the education, documentation, and training they need to excel in their roles
and contribute to the organization’s overarching goals.
e story of RevOps, the Great Enabler, starts here.
Strategic vs. Tactical Enablem ent
Strategic enablement focuses on long-term goals, vision, and broader
organizational growth. It involves aligning enablement initiatives with the
company’s overall business strategy. For example: developing a three-year
roadmap for RevOps to support revenue growth goals and global market
expansion.
Tactical enablement focuses on practical, immediate actions and
applicable knowledge to solve day-to-day challenges. For example: creating
sales playbooks, delivering training on a new CRM  tool, or running quarterly
pipeline review workshops to improve close rates.
e recommendations in this enablement section are more tactical. ere
are also strategic insights, but the focus is on tactical applicable knowledge.
e reason for that is that for most businesses, a big focus on strategy is a
luxury.
W hen we look worldwide, more than 320 million companies are small-
medium enterprises (SM Es), or businesses below 250 employees. at means
99.88% of all businesses worldwide are SM Es.22 ose companies don’t have
the luxury to employ a dedicated full-time strategy person.

---

## Page 136

In larger enterprises, there are more complexities and the scale is bigger.
Having well-planned strategic initiatives is crucial not only for the overall
success of enablement programs at scale but also for the overall success of the
company. It’s a diﬀerent level of planning if you have to onboard 20 employees
in one quarter versus 2,000. However, at the same time, those initiatives are
more the exception, less than 0.01%. at’s why the focus of this chapter is
more on the tactical side.
I ﬁrmly believe that applying tactical knowledge and gaining experience
will make you a better strategist than the same lessons put in a book.
Building a Culture of Continuous
Improvem ent
Enablement is not a one-time event but a continuous journey.
e journey begins with instilling a culture of learning within the
organization; it’s the prerequisite for successful enablement. is is where
curiosity is encouraged, knowledge is shared freely, and personal and
professional growth is prioritized. In such an environment, employees are
empowered to seek out new information, experiment with new approaches,
and continuously improve their skills.
Learning becomes part of the D NA  of the organization and is embodied
through the leadership team. Leaders must model lifelong learning by
participating in training and development activities themselves. ey should
encourage open communication to create a safe space for employees to ask
questions, share ideas, and voice concerns without fear of criticism. If that
doesn’t exist, RevOps will inevitably fail.
e truth is, we have all experienced a leader who embodies the opposite of
a learning culture—the know-it-all type. ey tend to have a track record of
having experienced some form of success once and now seem to have ﬁgured it
all out. Now, I am not saying that experiences don’t matter; on the contrary,
there is a vast amount of value in them. e danger is that arrogance fosters
blindness to reality. Facts that don’t conﬁrm the existing worldview are often

---

## Page 137

ignored. e consequence is dim: a business that stops learning will ultimately
cease to exist.
W e can only enable the team with the right knowledge by continually
questioning what the best course of action is.
Fueling the Swing of RevOps
Enablement preceded RevOps; it originated in the early 2000s. Sales
Enablement, in particular, gained traction as companies such as Salesforce and
HubSpot began to emphasize the importance of supporting Sales teams with
the right tools, content, and training. Since then, it’s increased in popularity
due to the complexity of sales.
Nowadays, enablement is a key pillar for successful G T M  teams. In
companies with a RevOps department, enablement usually sits within that
department. is makes a lot of sense, as most RevOps projects require some
enablement. Be that a new adjusted sales process, a new slide deck, or a new
tool in the tech stack. Hence, enablement is one of the four pillars of RevOps
as it goes hand-in-hand with Process, Systems, and Data and Insights.
e three main objectives of enablement are:
•Onboarding,
•Continuous learning, and
•Content.

---

## Page 138

Figure 5.1 Elements of Eﬀective Enablement
e speed and quality of enablement are now more important than ever,
and are critical in creating a competitive advantage. A I is changing how G T M
teams operate. I am fully convinced that only the teams that successfully
deploy AI will stay competitive in the future.
W e’ll look at this later on in more detail to understand how A I is changing
the role of enablement. But ﬁrst, we need to clarify the diﬀerence between the
HR team’s role and RevOps’ involvement in enablement.
Enablem ent in HR vs. RevOps
A  key goal of the HR team is to integrate a new employee into an organization
by providing them with the resources, knowledge and support they need to
succeed in their role. It’s also meant to align company culture and increase job
satisfaction to assure retention.
is is a lot and is a shared responsibility. HR teams take care of practical
matters like compliance, as well as cultural and organizational integration.

---

## Page 139

W hen it comes to RevOps, enablement is all about aligning with the
department’s business objectives by training and supporting teams to achieve
their desired outcomes and results. Here’s how they diﬀer from an
organizational perspective:
Figure 5.2 Org Chart H R and RevOps comparison
You can see that the focus of the HR team is more organizational with
speciﬁc topics like payroll, compensation, recruitment, and health and safety.
Strategic topics, such as company culture, job satisfaction initiatives, or well-
being, are also covered.
e biggest overlap is within onboarding and training. e HR team
usually has some form of responsibility that employees are successfully
onboarding and are up-to-date with training. However, they’re typically more
involved in company-wide training such as compliance, well-being, or
leadership development. RevOps focuses more on subject matter expertise,
such as sales training, tools training, and any other aspects that beneﬁt the

---

## Page 140

G T M  teams. e expertise of RevOps comes especially handy here as they
have the most knowledge on topics like tooling, processes, and commercial
strategy. is makes your RevOps department a great training hub.
Reducing Ramp Tim e
W hen it comes to onboarding in RevOps, the North Star matrix is to reduce
ramp time—the time it takes a new employee to reach full productivity. is
doesn’t mean that aligning with company culture and job satisfaction is not
important. But let’s not forget that we optimize for revenue, and eﬀective
onboarding is one way to do that.
ere are three factors that have the biggest impact on reducing ramp time:
hiring the right talent, just-in-time training, and clear documentation.
Hiring the Right Talent
W ho you hire will impact ramp time signiﬁcantly on multiple levels. People
just starting their careers will need more time to ramp. e same goes if they’re
not familiar with your company’s tech stack
But by far, the largest impact on ramp time will be industry experience. For
example, if a salesperson has sold a marketing solution in the past, then they
can pretty much sell any marketing solution. e same person could also likely
sell a sales or customer service solution as well since it’s still the same G T M
tech stack. But if that same person has to switch to a sustainable software
solution (think G oogle Cloud’s Carbon Footprint Reporting Tools), then
they’ll likely have a much harder time as the person lacks the industry expertise
in the sustainability sector.
M any businesses make this mistake. People tend to say, “If you can sell one
solution, then you can do anything else.” But in my experience, this isn’t true.
U sually, when you hire someone from one industry and put them into another
one, it fails. In extreme cases, I see people with a PhD  in chemical engineering
sell to buyers with a PhD  in chemical engineering. On your end, what would
you rather do: teach your new salesperson chemical engineering skills or sales

---

## Page 141

skills? is is why hiring the right talent is so important. M ake the wrong
choice, and it will be an expensive mistake.
W ith the right talent in place, you need to introduce them with the right
tools and knowledge at the right time. is is where implementing the Just-in-
Time Training method becomes essential.
Implem enting the Just-in-Tim e Training
Method
W hat does a new Sales D evelopment Representative (SD R) need to send their
ﬁrst email to a prospect? A n email template that clearly outlines the value
proposition.
W hat about making their ﬁrst outbound call? A  basic understanding of the
product, industry, and company, all tied to the value proposition.
A nd for delivering their ﬁrst demo? M ore in-depth knowledge of the
product, combined with everything they’ve learned so far.
e pattern here is clear: knowledge builds incrementally. By applying this
approach to your team, you can train in small, focused steps. Your trainees can
apply the learning immediately. is method is known as Just-In-Time
training.
It’s adapted from Toyota’s Just-In-Time delivery system and supply chain
strategy, where goods are delivered to the production line only when they’re
needed to boost productivity. It’s the opposite of companies that store large
quantities of production parts in big warehouses. Similarly, in Just-In-Time
training, you teach what is needed to complete the next step. is increases
productivity and gradually builds up skills.
is approach allows your employees to learn one skill so they can directly
apply it. Contrast that to a two week training seminar, where by the end of the
second week they’ve likely forgotten some of the concepts. I certainly had that
experience after my two weeks of sales training at Oracle. It was amazing
training, but not all of it stuck.

---

## Page 142

W hile Just-in-Time training oﬀers immediate application, it’s not always
enough to ensure long-term success. at’s why clear, accessible documentation
plays a crucial role in supporting your team.
Creating Clear Docum entation
To hire the best talent, many businesses have a rigorous hiring process. But
once someone is hired, you might not provide them with the necessary
information to succeed. You’ll often hear from new hires, “W here do I ﬁnd
[insert helpful piece to do your job]?” ey’ll make an eﬀort to ﬁnd the
information they need, but they often can’t ﬁnd it, so they start to ask around.
W hat I’ve found is that this information tends to be in someone’s head.
Sadly, that’s not where it should be. It needs to be documented and easily
found when needed.
ere are plenty of excuses for the lack of clear documentation, but they all
boil down to time. However, that excuse isn’t valid anymore because we can
use generative AI to create it.
To do that, here are some ways you can use your favorite A I tool to create
clear and concise documentation:
•G ive it bullet points of your ICP and instruct it to write down a clear
description.
•Share a voice memo with your instructions and let it transform it to text.
•Share a screenshot of your M iro board that illustrates your sales process
and instruct it to explain the process.
•Share the transcript of an internal meeting in which you agreed on a new
deﬁnition for M arketing Qualiﬁed Leads (M QLs). Instruct the AI to
take the transcript and create a knowledge article.
You can even give it additional instructions to follow your brand voice.
W hile it may require a bit of editing, the results of this are often very good.
M ost importantly, though, you’re able to create clear documentation your team
can reference at least 80% faster than without AI.

---

## Page 143

In my experience, hiring the right talent, implementing Just-in-Time
training, and creating clear documentation are the three factors that have the
biggest impact on reducing ramp time. M entorship, goal setting, and tools will
further reduce ramp time as well, and we’ll discuss them later in the chapter.
Emb racing Continuous Learning
Learning never stops, and certainly not once the onboarding is completed.
e role of enablement is to support continuous learning. W hether it’s
adapting to new technologies, improving methods for getting things done, or
striving to become the best version of yourself, enablement helps you to keep
growing.
A gain, this is a shared responsibility between the HR and Enablement
teams. You can draw a blurry line and say the HR team is primarily responsible
for soft skills, while the Enablement team is responsible for hard skills. Soft
skills may include leadership coaching, cognitive bias training, or
communication skills. Hard skills may relate more to a speciﬁc role, such as
tool training, process training, or industry expertise. But it can also include
communication skills, social selling, or persuasions (this makes the line blurry
because some job-speciﬁc skills the Enablement team facilitates are more
general).
U nderstanding the division between soft and hard skills is important, but
the real impact comes when we apply that knowledge to critical areas of
ongoing training and development: process and tool adoption, product
knowledge, and industry knowledge.
Process and Tool Enablem ent
In the last chapter, you learned why change management is vital to the success
of RevOps. To make change successful, you need to ensure new processes and
tools are adopted. W ith that, enablement becomes the most critical aspect.
W ithout proper training and adoption, the whole project is in jeopardy.

---

## Page 144

e goal of adoption is to make the business more eﬃcient and reduce
costs to create more revenue. It’s not a good reﬂection on the RevOps team to
spend months of preparation, implementation, and training for a project to fall
ﬂat and not be adopted. at’s the biggest frustration I hear from my clients in
RevOps consulting: leaders are disappointed because they build very tiresome
processes or complex tools but are not able to educate the team on how to use
or derive value from them.
Process and tooling enablement is central to how your impact is perceived.
W ithout consistent processes, the business can’t generate reliable data or
insights. W ithout trustworthy data, you can’t build credible KP Is. at’s why
following a process is the foundation for everything RevOps does, which is
why process and tool enablement is so critical.
W ith that in mind, it’s better to have an average solution to a problem but
ensure that everyone is perfectly trained on it. is will drive more impact than
having something over-engineered but poorly adopted.
e words of ﬁghter and philosopher Bruce Lee hold true: I don’t fear
someone who has practiced a thousand kicks once, but I fear someone who has
practiced one kick a thousand times.
Product Knowledge
I strongly believe that everyone on the G T M  team needs to be an expert in the
product or service they are oﬀering, including in RevOps.
U nderstanding the product your company is selling helps provide better
enablement to the rest of the team. Client-facing teams like Sales and
Customer Success need to understand the product well. How often in a sales
call have you heard: “Can you let me get back to you on this?” I always advise
someone to say that when they don’t know the answer. But when you hear that
sentence ﬁve times within half an hour, then it reﬂects negatively on the person
and, by extension, on the company.
One of the most important processes is how new feature releases are
communicated to Revenue teams. A  good practice is to classify the changes to
high, medium, and low impact on clients and prospects. High-impact changes

---

## Page 145

should involve dedicated live training sessions led by the Product team for the
Revenue team. ey provide the most value. M edium-impact changes can also
be announced during all-hands meetings or team catch-ups. Low-impact
changes can be shown asynchronously with a recording.
Pro T ip: U se known training tactics, such as certiﬁcations, to re-educate your
reps and ensure alignment across the teams.
In all cases, it makes sense to show (and not tell) to avoid any
misunderstandings. It’s essential to have product documentation, including
guides, manuals, and an FA Q section. A n internal wiki that’s well maintained
becomes useful at this point. Lastly, everyone should have access to the
platform for testing purposes. ere is so much value in having a proper
feature release process, but still too many companies struggle with it.
On a side note, there is a point where a product can become very complex,
and companies require solution consultants. Sometimes, a company might
even have diﬀerent product categories and will have speciﬁc solution
consultants per product line. For example, Salesforce Sales Cloud vs. Salesforce
M arketing Cloud. It makes total sense to have solution consultants in this case.
e problem is only when they become a scapegoat for the rest of the team to
not learn anything about the product.
Industry knowledge
Earlier, I talked about how important it is to select talent based on industry
knowledge, as it’s the hardest to acquire. Sales training is clearly important, but
you can hire a sales coach or send someone to an external training session. It’s
not that easy to train industry knowledge. Learning it takes time.
For the Enablement team, it’s a continuous process. Creating a knowledge
base is a good starting point. is can include whitepapers, industry reports,
and market analysis. It should periodically have an updated competitor analysis

---

## Page 146

when possible; create it internally and complement it with external competitor
assessments.
Knowledge-sharing sessions are also an important part of enablement.
ese can include presentations from internal specialists and external industry
experts who provide valuable insights about your industry. A  dedicated
industry newsletter is also a great way to drive this knowledge. Eventually, you
may have an internal research team to share knowledge internally and
externally. It has become popular to have industry-speciﬁc content in the form
of blogs, webinars, and podcasts.
Finally, each team member has their own responsibility to stay up-to-date
with industry knowledge relevant to the job; there’s only so much an
Enablement team can do. is is the distinction between top talent and an
employee.
Content as the Catalyst to Power Enablem ent
Enablement is not just about training. Enablement is about providing your
G T M  teams with the content to perform at the highest possible level.
W hen I talk about content, I am referring to all types, as you can see in
Figure 4.2.

---

## Page 147

Figure 5.3 Types of Content in GT M  & RevOps
In short, it’s everything that represents the value proposition of your
company internally and externally to prospects. It helps the team communicate
value along the customer journey.
e most important element of content is consistency. is means that the
messaging a client sees is the same across a print ad, a newsletter, a demo, and
once they’re a client. It must be one coherent story.
Both young and mature businesses fall into the trap of being inconsistent.
Younger companies have less content to change but lack processes to facilitate
the changes. ey’re characterized by constant product updates and changes in
strategy. Content becomes outdated very fast. W hile at larger companies,
changes happen at so many diﬀerent places that one department might not
communicate with another. Sales might represent content in one way while
M arketing in another.

---

## Page 148

e consequence is customer confusion.
You’ve probably heard customers saying: “But you advertised this,” or,
“Your colleague said you can…” or, “‘You promised [X] will be possible.” is
can be extremely frustrating.
e same applies to your internal team. How often have you heard: “Is that
a new slide deck?” or, “W here did you ﬁnd that…?” or, “W ho has the most
recent…?” To avoid confusion in messaging, the internal content has to be the
same as the external content.
How can you do this?
By having content orchestrated centrally within RevOps.
RevOps Orchestrating Content Creation
In leading businesses, RevOps often oversees the orchestration of internal
content and also plays a role in managing external content for prospects and
clients. is doesn’t mean RevOps creates all the content or fully owns the
process. Rather, it ensures consistency across content, regardless of the source.
Content may come from various teams, with M arketing typically leading on
outward-facing messaging. Contributors might include specialists from
M arketing, Sales, Product, or other subject matter experts. is helps ensure
the content is accurate and comes directly from the source.
RevOps ensures that all content tells a cohesive story. Because they
collaborate closely with all customer-facing teams, they have a unique
understanding of the organization’s overall pulse and messaging.
Having RevOps oversee content creation has a wide set of beneﬁts:
•Consistent messaging across the company.
•Easier to change, improve, and update messaging.
•Easier to ﬁnd and share content.
•Everyone knows who owns the content.
•Faster execution due to fewer approval bottlenecks.

---

## Page 149

ese are all beneﬁts worth having.
e prerequisite for this to work is that you have a true RevOps function
that overlooks all revenue teams. If your RevOps team only supports Sales or
M arketing, then it won’t work. A nother important element is that you have a
dedicated enablement specialist on your RevOps team. D epending on the size
of your business, content can quite quickly become a large part of the business,
which is why you’ll need to work closely with other departments.
Close Coordination with Product Marketing
e content creation process requires close collaboration with the Product
M arketing team. Product marketing involves bringing a product to market and
ensuring its success by understanding customer needs, analyzing market trends,
and diﬀerentiating the product from competitors. It includes deﬁning the
product’s unique value, planning and executing launch strategies, and creating
campaigns to generate demand. Product M arketing teams have a close link to
both the Product and M arketing teams.
In many businesses, Product M arketing is also responsible for enabling
Revenue teams. is enablement can be delivered through RevOps, where
Product M arketing ﬁrst trains RevOps, who then trains the front lines (we’ll
explore this later on), or directly by Product M arketing to Sales and Customer
Success. Often, it’s a hybrid approach. e best format depends on the unique
skill set within your organization. ere’s no right or wrong solution here as
long as there’s strong collaboration between Product M arketing and RevOps.
at partnership is key to driving consistency, with RevOps playing an
orchestrating role to ensure it all aligns.
The Role of Generative AI for Content
G enerative A I has become especially helpful in creating and curating content.
W hile they’re useful across all G T M  teams, RevOps can beneﬁt from them in
their day-to-day activities.
Here’s how:

---

## Page 150

Consistency in Messaging
A I can be trained to adopt a brand identity so every piece of content represents
your core brand. is can include small details such as referring to your
product as a “solution” instead of “tool.” You can also ensure it focuses on a
single key selling point that is conveyed in all messaging. is extremely helps
with brand consistency.
Pro T ip: Consistent messaging gives you a competitive advantage. RevOps helps
ensure the key value proposition stays aligned across all communication. AI can
support this by generating emails, call scripts, and slides that reinforce those key
messages.
G enerative A I can also learn the style of an individual representative, which
helps the person create personalized emails faster while staying on brand.
Hyper-Personalization on Scale
It has never been so easy to personalize outreach. G enerative A I platforms such
as n8n, Copy.ai, or Loyee A I can do account research, combine it with intent
data, and create a personalized message that can be sent to prospects. is
process could have taken a Sales Rep easily anywhere between 15 to 30
minutes. Now, it can take minutes.
Content Managem ent System s and Internal Knowledge Managem ent
G enerative A I addresses the challenge of outdated content libraries and
internal knowledge bases. ese systems often become obsolete quickly and
may require dedicated staﬀ to maintain them. W hen not properly updated,
they lead to outdated information, diﬃculty in ﬁnding resources, and
unnecessary delays.
A I-enhanced content libraries help by:
1.Flagging duplicated automations, helping you keep the system clean.
2.Facilitating review cycles to keep the system up-to-date.

---

## Page 151

3.A llowing you to directly ask questions so you can quickly get answers.
A ll of this removes roadblocks to make knowledge more accessible to your
team. is brings us to an essential component of enablement: training styles
tailored to diverse learning needs.
Driving Impact and Growth
Training refers to providing your team with ongoing education on processes,
techniques, tools, and new features—anything that helps boost productivity
and generate more revenue.
Tailor Training to Different Learning Needs
W hy do so many students dislike universities?
M ost of them rely on one-sided learning and standardized testing because
alternative approaches would be too expensive to scale. In addition, they need
a simple way to compare students. eir focus is on quantity over quality.
However, you’re not a university. You’re a for-proﬁt business that wants to
maximize revenue and increase proﬁts. You don’t have the luxury to ignore the
learning needs of your staﬀ, nor can you hire an inﬁnite amount of people and
hope for the best. e cost of hiring and onboarding employees is high. So,
you have to be smart on how you upskill your workforce.
at’s why its important to apply diﬀerent training methods to maximize
eﬀectiveness and make the biggest impact on your workforce. Let’s go through
some of the most eﬀective ones.
One-on-One Training
One-on-one training involves direct interaction between the two parties
involved. It’s usually the most time-consuming and resource-intensive method
because it doesn’t scale. However, its beneﬁt is that it is tailored to an
individual’s learning needs. On top of that, it provides immediate feedback and
adaptability so you can adjust your other training types accordingly.

---

## Page 152

One-on-one trainings are best used for:
•Leadership coaching or professional development of high-potential
individuals.
•Complex processes and setups that don’t change often.
•Resource-intensive training that requires an individual’s 100% focus and
attention.
Certiﬁcation Program s
Certiﬁcation programs are structured initiatives designed to validate
individuals’ expertise, skills, and knowledge. ese programs often culminate
in a formal certiﬁcation, demonstrating that the individual has met a
standardized set of criteria, typically through assessments, training, and exams.
Certiﬁcation programs are best used for:
•A n organization where validating speciﬁc skills and knowledge is critical.
•Regulated industries where certiﬁcation is needed to stay compliant.
•Organizations where certiﬁcation enhances employees’ worth (ﬁnancial
advisor, health, security, etc.).
•Security compliance.
e problem with certiﬁcations is that they’re often too standardized.
M any people don’t like them, or even resent them. e worst is they don’t
guarantee competence in the job. at’s why I recommend the above use cases
and not wider adoption of certiﬁcations.
Train the Trainer
is means to empower the team leads. In return, they will train team
members. is is usually a great method as the team leads understand the
learning needs of each of their direct reports.
In most organizations, the team lead also has a mandate to educate their
teammates. So they’re usually very open to this model.

---

## Page 153

Here’s when to use the train-the-trainers model:
•M ore complex projects that impact multiple teams (new tools, cross-
department alignments, new process deployment).
•Strong level of compliance required (new forecasting process).
•Team leads giving the training helps with better adoption. You convince
one stakeholder, and that creates a network eﬀect.
Train the U ser (or Group)
is means directly training the front-line workers who sell your product or
service.
It’s a great method, as you’ll be in direct contact with your employees who
are customer-facing. For example, you’ll train employees on Salesforce, and
they’ll train the customer. is not only helps you gain valuable feedback from
the end user but also helps you develop your own training methods.
W hen to use train the end-user model:
•Training applies to a group of people with similar roles or needs.
•Processes and services that are not too complex.
•Processes and services that require refreshers and reinforcement.
Walk-in Sessions (A.k.a. Ofﬁce Hours)
W alk-in sessions are a great way to continuously educate the team. ey are
very helpful after a project release or a software implementation, once the team
has had time to work with the change. ey help to share knowledge, get
feedback from end-users, and identify potential bugs. On top of that, the
questions asked during a walk-in session can help you understand how end
users interact with systems.
Here’s when to use walk-in sessions:
•D uring and after a large implementation that’s split into phases.
•Continuously educating the team on new features or developments.

---

## Page 154

•Creating support for projects.
Here are some best practices for walk-in sessions:
•Record the session (if online) and make it available with a summary of
key points covered.
•Schedule them bi-weekly or weekly.
•A ttendance should be optional.
On-demand Training: Knowledge Center and Async Training
On-demand training has become increasingly popular with the widespread use
of remote work, but it was also common in the form of a knowledge center.
Software providers like Loom make creating async training even more
attainable for smaller organizations, since you can easily record training
sessions and upload them to a central hub.
e beneﬁt of on-demand training is that once you create it, it can serve
multiple people at once. One drawback is that it’s less eﬀective, as it doesn’t
allow for interaction.
W hen to use on-demand training:
•Complementary to the direct training methods.
•W hen having a distributed team in various time zones.
•A s a reference resource that becomes practical when new employees join.
On-demand training is also a form of documentation. I’ll cover
documentation later on, but for now, it’s good to understand that it can also be
used as training material.
e problem with on-demand training is that it’s not as eﬀective; hardly
anyone learns by handing them information. If that were true, then we’d all be
experts in everything. W e live in a time where we can just google or ask
ChatG PT  to ﬁnd the answer to anything. D o we ﬁnd the answer to our
problem? Yes. Do we improve that actual skill? No.
at’s why in-person training is so relevant.

---

## Page 155

The Different Training Methods Available
H ow you deliver the training directly impacts the quality of learning. You will
most likely apply a mix of diﬀerent methods within your training. is
increases the amount of team members who beneﬁt, as everyone has diﬀerent
learning styles.
Presentation
is method involves a trainer delivering information to learners, typically in a
lecture or seminar format. It’s eﬀective for introducing new concepts or
providing detailed knowledge. e use of visual aids, such as slides or videos,
can enhance engagement and aid in the retention of information.
Role-playing
Role-playing is an interactive method where trainees act out scenarios relevant
to their roles. Imagine a mock call session between BD Rs and the BD R
manager. is approach is particularly beneﬁcial for developing soft skills such
as communication, problem-solving, and conﬂict resolution. It allows learners
to practice and reﬁne their skills in a controlled, low-risk environment.
Job Rotation
Job rotation involves moving employees through a variety of positions within
the business. is exposes them to diﬀerent aspects of the business and
enhances their overall understanding of the company. It’s an eﬀective way to
develop a more versatile workforce and identify potential areas for employee
growth. It’s a popular method with traineeships and internships. e goal is
usually to have talent stick with the company for a long time.
Shadowing
Shadowing allows an employee to learn by observing a more experienced
colleague. It is particularly useful for understanding speciﬁc job roles and
workﬂows within the organization. is method provides real-time learning
opportunities and can be a powerful tool for on-the-job training.

---

## Page 156

Gam iﬁcation
G amiﬁcation incorporates game design elements such as points, badges, and
leaderboards into training to make learning more engaging and interactive.
is helps to motivate learners and encourage friendly competition.
is approach is eﬀective in both e-learning and in-person training
environments. For example, there’s software that simulates a horse race. e
more calls a Sales Rep makes, the faster their horse moves, ultimately leading
to a winner.
Third-Party Training
is method involves external professionals or organizations to deliver
specialized training. It’s an excellent way to bring in expert knowledge and
fresh perspectives. ird-party training can cover a wide range of topics, from
technical skills to leadership development.
You use third-party training when you lack the skills in-house or you need
to execute fast.
Case Studies
U sing real or hypothetical scenarios, case studies oﬀer a deep dive into speciﬁc
situations relevant to the trainees’ roles. ey encourage critical thinking and
problem-solving, and help learners apply theoretical knowledge in practical
contexts. D iscussion and analysis of these cases foster a deeper understanding
of complex business issues.
Sim ulations
Simulations replicate real-world scenarios in a controlled environment. ey
are particularly useful for technical or crisis management training, where
hands-on experience is crucial. Simulations can range from computer-based
models to full-scale role-plays, oﬀering a practical learning experience without
real-world risks.
D oing practice assignments in the Salesforce Trailhead Sandbox
environment is a perfect example of a simulation.

---

## Page 157

Coaching
Coaching is a more personalized approach, where an experienced individual
guides a learner in developing speciﬁc skills and achieving goals. is method
is highly eﬀective for personal development and addresses individual learning
needs. Regular feedback and one-on-one sessions help build conﬁdence and
skills in a focused manner.
AI Coaching
e new A I coaching methods allow for cost-eﬀective and high-quality
training methods.
Here are a few options:
Live Call Assistant
W ith a live call assistant, your reps receive live coaching during a call. e A I
technology will analyze the call and give feedback in real-time. It can provide a
resolution for an objection, remind the seller to ask a certain question or
recommend a change in the tone of voice. is technology allows us to identify
what best-selling reps are doing and instantly apply it to others.
Speciﬁc AI Feedback
A Is are also very good at giving tailored feedback. e A I analyzes all the calls
of a speciﬁc rep and compares them with the best practices of other reps. is
gives them targeted feedback about what they should continue doing and, in
reverse, which areas can be improved. is is very powerful because a manual
comparison would require a lot of time and a very talented trainer.
AI Role Play
A I can also be used for role-play, such as mock calls. ey can objectively take
the perspective of a client and use your ideal customer proﬁle (ICP) and buyer
persona to create the perfect training partner. W ith this, you can better
compare the performance of individual reps.
One of the main beneﬁts of using mock calls is that you don’t burn real
leads.

---

## Page 158

Measuring the Mom entum  and Evaluating
Enablem ent Success
Every enablement program is an investment. erefore, it’s more important
that you’re able to measure the eﬀectiveness of your programs.
M easuring the training eﬀectiveness isn’t always simple. Let’s take the
example of onboarding a Sales Rep with a two-month ramp-up period before
they’re fully onboarded. e question then becomes: if the rep isn’t performing
after two months of training, is the issue with the training program? Or are
other external factors at play, like the market environment?
M any factors impact the eﬀectiveness of a training program. at’s why it’s
important to tailor to diﬀerent learning needs. A  too-standardized enablement
program could backﬁre and undermine potentially great talent, which is a huge
risk for your business (the same way standardized testing has failed many
students in universities).
is doesn’t mean you can’t measure the success of training programs. You
can, and there are methodologies to help with that.
Kirkpatrick’s Four-Level Training Evaluation
Model
M easuring enablement success requires a structured approach, and this is
where Kirkpatrick’s Four-Level Training Evaluation M odel comes into play.
D eveloped by D r. D onald Kirkpatrick in the 1950s, this model provides a
framework for assessing the eﬀectiveness of training programs.
It’s composed of four levels that help you ﬁnd areas for improvement.

---

## Page 159

Figure 5.4 Kirkpatrick’s Four-Level Training Evaluation M odel
Level 1: Reaction
e ﬁrst level of Kirkpatrick’s model focuses on understanding an employee’s
reactions to the training. is initial step is about gauging their immediate
feelings and satisfaction. Imagine the participants just ﬁnished a session—their
feedback is fresh and most honest at this point. D id they ﬁnd the training
engaging? W ere the materials relevant and well-presented? Collecting this
feedback can be done through surveys, feedback forms, or even brief interviews
at the end. is data is critical as it helps to tweak and improve future sessions
to better meet participants’ needs.
Level 2: Learning
M oving beyond initial reactions, the second level delves into what the
participants have actually learned. is involves measuring the increase in
knowledge or skills as a result of the training. ink of it as checking the
academic progress of a student after a semester. is can be done through pre
and post-training assessments, quizzes, tests, or practical exercises. e goal
here is to quantify how much participants’ knowledge or skills have improved.
By comparing their performances before and after the training, you can
objectively assess the learning outcomes.
Level 3: Behavior

---

## Page 160

e third level addresses a critical question: are participants applying what they
learned during the training to their jobs? is is where the rubber meets the
road. It’s not just about acquiring knowledge but about changing behavior and
improving performance.
Observing participants over time, conducting performance evaluations,
and gathering feedback from their supervisors can provide insights into how
eﬀectively the training has been transferred to the workplace. For instance, if a
sales training program was conducted, look for changes in how a Sales Rep
approaches their customers and whether they are using the new techniques
learned.
Level 4: Results
Finally, the fourth level measures the ultimate impact of the training on
organizational goals. is is where you connect the dots between training and
business outcomes. Has the training contributed to increased sales, improved
customer satisfaction, reduced costs, or higher productivity? A nalyzing KP Is
and business metrics helps in understanding the tangible beneﬁts of the
training. is level focuses on the big picture and the ROI of the training
program. By measuring these results, you can see how the training has
contributed to achieving strategic business objectives.
Applying Kirkpatrick’s Model: A Practical Exam ple
To illustrate the application of K irkpatrick’s model, let’s consider a company
implementing a sales training program. Here’s how the model could be
applied:
Level 1: Reaction
A fter the training, participants complete a survey, rating their satisfaction with
the training content, instructor eﬀectiveness, and overall experience.
Level 2: Learning
Participants take a test before and after the training to measure their
knowledge of sales techniques and strategies.

---

## Page 161

Level 3: Behavior
M anagers observe and evaluate whether participants are applying the new sales
techniques during customer interactions and review sales performance data.
Level 4: Results
e company analyzes sales data to determine if there has been an increase in
sales revenue, conversion rates, or customer retention as a result of the training.
By evaluating training programs at all four levels, organizations can gain a
deep understanding of their eﬀectiveness and identify areas for improvement.
Kirkpatrick’s model serves as a robust framework and ensures that the journey
of enablement is not only continuous but also makes a massive impact.
Socratic Reﬂections
Consider these questions to elevate your enablement programs:
•In your own words, what does a culture of learning look like?
•W hat can you do this week to move your business one step closer to that
culture?
•W hat would you improve in your company’s onboarding program?
•W hat is the most valuable content for your business? How can you get
more of it?
•How can you encourage people to continuously learn?
•How eﬀective is your current enablement?
•W hat is your favorite training style?
•How can you leverage AI in enablement?
•How can you make training more exciting?

## Page 162

CHAPTER 6
THE SECOND W EIGHT –
PROCESS
“Plans are useless, but planning is essential.”
D wight D. Eisenhower
In Berlin-M itte, nestled among trendy coﬀee shops and boutique sports clubs,
the ﬁctional SaaS M yG ym24 ﬁnds itself at a critical turning point. ey just
raised $12.4 million in Series A  funding. It’s a signiﬁcant milestone that ﬁlls
the team with excitement and ambition. However, with this new capital comes
immense pressure to scale operations and deliver exponential growth.
A mid the euphoria, the founders of M yG ym24, A lex and M ia, realize their
current ad hoc way of managing growth won’t suﬃce. ey need a robust
framework to guide their expansion eﬀorts and ensure that every team member
is aligned and productive. W ho is best to set those up? e newly founded
RevOps team.
Processes are what allow for repeatable success in business. For SaaS
companies like M yG ym24, this is even more crucial because the business
thrives on recurring revenue and exponential returns.
W ell-established processes will:
•Increase revenue
•Increase proﬁts

---

## Page 163

•Increase retention
•Reduce churn
•Reduce costs
Let’s look at what strong processes actually deliver:
Beneﬁt D etail
Reduce risk Processes reduce the risk of making errors.
Create clarity People have expectations about the task involved in reaching a
goal.
Establish responsibilities e team understands who does what and at what time.
Have accountability Clear accountability is present, allowing for further
improvement of the process.
Less dependency K nowledge is spread over multiple people and documented,
creating less dependency on a single person.
Reduce frustration Not knowing what is happening is frustrating for coworkers
and clients.
Scale Once a process is established, the business can scale the process
to adjust for growth.
Processes have plenty of beneﬁts and are a necessity for businesses. A t the
same time, overdoing processes can annoy people. Let’s look at a few
complaints people have about processes.
M isconception Reality
Processes slow things
down.
is happens often in businesses that move through stages,
e.g., from founder-led sales to its own Sales team or from a
single product company to a multi-product company. Before,
1 to 2 people made a decision, and it was easy to ﬁnd out what
to do. W ith added complexity, it’s hard to understand what to
do, and the risk of error increases. A  well-deﬁned process in the
end is faster than no process at all.
Processes are
complicated.
Yes, they can be complicated, but so are businesses. You can
simplify them, but even that can still be complex. e
alternative is chaos, so there isn’t much choice.

---

## Page 164

W e don’t need a process
for that.
Said the company that went out of business. Early-stage
companies can postpone having clear processes. But quite
quickly, processes become essential.
W hen it comes to processes, the negative can be seen as daunting. But
hopefully you, like A lex and M ia, understand that the beneﬁts are worth it.
W ell-deﬁned processes and documentation help your company run as
eﬃciently as possible. I can only recommend starting early-on internally or
with external expert guidance.
The Role of Processes in RevOps
RevOps processes often touch multiple G T M  teams and can be business-wide
on occasion. Contrast that to M arketing, Sales, or Customer Success
operations, which focus more on their own departments. ese speciﬁc ops
processes are still relevant, but they’re also a reason for:
•Revenue leakages
•M isaligned teams
•Siloed departments
•M issed revenue opportunities
RevOps has a holistic view of the business and provides a solution to the
above problems.
is doesn’t mean you aren’t focused on speciﬁc processes for these other
teams. ey are still relevant. A nd depending on your RevOps deployment
model, they might be integrated within RevOps or work closely with RevOps.
One does not exclude the other. e point is that many businesses have never
considered the integrated view.
W hich is why this is such a massive opportunity.
is chapter focuses on cross-functional processes where RevOps adds
signiﬁcant value, and we’ll use M yG ym24 as an example. M yG ym24 manages
gym subscriptions and oﬀers clients an app to guide their workouts and book

---

## Page 165

classes. A s their ﬁrst RevOps hire, your role is to help them invest their funds
wisely to drive both revenue growth and proﬁtability.
First stop: growth planning.
Market Size: TAM, SAM, and SOM
Total A ddressable M arket (TA M ), Serviceable A ddressable M arket (SA M ), and
Serviceable Obtainable M arket (SOM ) might sound like the melody of a song,
but it’s actually an important exercise to deﬁne your market size. is helps to
attract investors and sets the foundation for your growth planning.
Figure 6.1 TAM , SAM , and SOM  Illustration
e mission of M yG ym24 is to become the leading provider of gym
solutions across Europe. e latest numbers from 2022 show that there are
119,356 gyms in Europe.23 e annual license of their SaaS, with all add-ons,
is priced at $15,000. at would represent a TA M  of $1.79 billion.
However, a more realistic approach is to focus on SA M . W hile it varies
from business to business, the fundamentals to determine it are the same: you
look at diﬀerent assumptions and operational realities. M yG ym24 operates

---

## Page 166

only in G erman-speaking countries: G ermany (D ), A ustria (A ), and
Switzerland (CH)—in short, D A CH. In total, there are 11,000 gyms in the
D A CH area. Out of all the gyms, they focus on those located in metropolitan
areas since they’ve noticed the demographic is more receptive to their
solutions. (M ore about this later in the Ideal Customer Proﬁle (ICP) section.)
is brings the SA M  down to 7,000. If we multiply this number by the
$15,000/year, we end up with a SA M  of $105 million.
Lastly, you calculate the SOM  to understand your current market share. To
do this, you multiply last year’s market share with this year’s SA M .
For example, let’s assume last year’s SA M  was $100M, and M yG ym24 had
$15 million in revenue, giving them a market share of 15%. Hence, this year’s
SOM  would be:
If M yG ym24 makes more than $15.75 million in revenue this year, they
gain a market share. If they make less, then they lose market share. A ltogether,
we end up with the following numbers for M yG ym24:
Knowing the TOM , SA M , and SOM  isn’t just important for investors.
ey provide an indication of the market size.
e next step is to plan how to conquer this market. For that, you need
sales territory and capacity planning.
Sales Territory
A  sales territory helps you systematically capture the market share of your
SA M . It’s a key process in your G T M  strategy. e typical way people think
about a sales territory is that it’s all about sales. is makes sense since “sales” is
in the term itself. But if we look at the deﬁnition, it expands beyond it:

---

## Page 167

“A  sales territory is the customer group or geographical area for which an
individual salesperson or a Sales team holds responsibility.”
But even from that deﬁnition, it’s not entirely clear. Is it a customer group
or a region? Is it for one salesperson or a whole team? e truth is that sales
territories are more of an art than a science. A nd they don’t just aﬀect sales.
ey touch almost every part of your GT M  strategy.
Function Territory Impact
M arketing M arketing campaigns are speciﬁc to territory characteristics
such as region, language, or segments. For example, account-
based marketing campaigns will require clear territories.
Sales ese are the accounts that Sales focuses its eﬀorts on. is
impacts team composition, as this team may require certain
language skills or industry expertise. For example, the EM EA
market will require the team to speak multiple languages.
Customer Support Team composition will be impacted here as well, in terms of
language and expertise. For example, if you serve clients in
G ermany or France, it’s advisable to have local language
support reps.
Product is might impact your product roadmap to tailor to a speciﬁc
market. For example, if you sell HR software and a local
market requires you to prepare time reports in a certain
format.
Not everything above applies all of the time. Sometimes, you’ll only
experience an impact on Sales and M arketing. But if your company wants to
sell within Europe, then you will often experience an impact in all categories.
Types of Territory
e most common way to structure a territory is based on geography. But
there are seven diﬀerent options to structure it.

---

## Page 168

Figure 6.2 Types of Territories
G eographic Territories: You can split territories based on regions like the
East Coast or where speciﬁc oﬃces are located. is is typically how local Sales
teams operate, such as handling accounts in New York, New Jersey, and
Pennsylvania. is setup works well for face-to-face meetings and provides
speedy support, especially for high-value tools.
Industry or Vertical Territories: is one’s about grouping accounts from
similar industries or verticals together. It helps reps speak the same language as
their prospects and showcases relevant use cases during calls.
Customer Size Territories: You can categorize customers as SM Bs, mid-
market, and enterprise tiers for tailored sales territories. Bigger clients may

---

## Page 169

need longer sales cycles and personalized demos, while smaller ones could
beneﬁt from quick video demos and self-service onboarding.
Product Line Territories: For companies with multiple products, align
reps with speciﬁc solutions. Oracle oﬀers cloud computing as a service line and
enterprise software. is way, reps master each product’s capabilities, making
them product experts during sales pitches.
New Business vs. Renewals Territories: Separating accounts for new
business and renewals can be game-changing. BD Rs focus on acquiring new
customers, while renewal Reps nurture existing ones. is division helps
maintain a clear focus on distinct sales processes.
Channel Sales Territories: In cases where sales involve reseller partners,
having dedicated Partner A ccount M anagers can streamline operations. For
example, an EM EA  Channel Partners Territory M anager could handle
partnerships in that region to boost revenue.
Named Accounts Territories: A  named account is a strategic account like
a Fortune 500 company. In this instance, it’s not uncommon for one rep to
only manage one or two strategic accounts. ese named account territories
receive the necessary sales and marketing resources to close big deals
successfully (think multi-million, if not billion-dollar deals).
W hile the territories listed above are the most common ones, it doesn’t
mean they are the only ones. You might have a grouping variable that’s unique
to your industry. It also doesn’t mean you have to stick to one grouping
category. You have the option of combining them, which we’ll touch on in the
planning section (up next).
Territory Planning
Territory planning ensures that your teams are focused on the right markets,
maximizing coverage and revenue potential while avoiding overlap and
ineﬃciencies. By aligning territories with clear data-driven insights, you can
prioritize the most promising opportunities and set the foundation for scalable
growth.
Let’s go through this exercise for M yG ym24 so you can get a sense of what
it will look like.

---

## Page 170

Since we deﬁned the market size earlier, we know that M yG ym24’s primary
focus is on the D A CH region. W ithin the D A CH, the focus is on the
European M etropolitan Region (EM R). If we boil it down to G ermany, then
we can identify 11 EM Rs—62.5% of the total population lives there.24 is
dense population is perfect for M yG ym24.
W ithin these territories, there are also sub-territories. For example, the
Rhine Ruhr EM R is the largest, with 11 million people. is EM R is a
combination of three metropolitan areas—all large cities, D üsseldorf being one
of them. For M yG ym24, this is important as they know most gyms are located
within big cities.
e next step is to assess the quality of the accounts in the target market.
is step is important as it serves as a preparation step to understand the
quality of a territory. A t the same time, this step also has limitations. You won’t
always know the exact list of accounts within the territory or their
characteristics. A nother aspect is time. G athering the details can take months,
depending on the level of detail and availability of data. is can be qualitative
or quantitative scoring criteria.
In the case of M yG ym24, it’s important to look at health clubs. ese are
small businesses that are not listed, meaning there isn’t any public ﬁnancial
information available, such as revenue, operating costs, or proﬁts. In this case,
you can ﬁnd the number of locations per health club instead. is is a good
proxy for maximum purchasing power.
Lastly, some health clubs are chains that operate in multiple cities and
across countries. ese are high-quality, which is why they are designated as
named accounts for M yG ym24.
Overall, when you look at a territory, it’s important to have a high share of
ICPs within it. at is one of the best indicators of a great theory. (W e’ll cover
ICPs in more detail in the next section.)
But remember, not all territories are made equal.
ere are quality diﬀerences in territories, and a poor territory can lead to
frustration for Sales Reps. W hen I worked for Oracle, my ﬁrst territory fell
into that category. A t that time, I was in the Enterprise Resource Planning
(ERP) Cloud Solution team with the goal of selling ERP cloud solutions to

---

## Page 171

businesses that were on-premise (i.e., had their own servers). I was assigned the
territory of G erman health insurance companies. ere were a few factors
against me:
1.Health data is very sensitive. Moving it to the cloud in the early days of
cloud computing was met with skepticism.
2.is is SA P’s home market—the market leader in ERP. ey have a
95% market share in the ERP space.
3.Health insurance companies in Germany have their own legal entity,
which is partly private and partly state-owned. is makes sales a
bureaucratic nightmare.
4.eir business model is so custom that our solution was not ﬁt for their
purpose. ere are competitors that build tailored ERPs just for that
industry, including our own Oracle Health Cloud. is meant I was
competing against my own company.
You can imagine that territory did not lead to any sales. Luckily, I had
another territory in the manufacturing sector that worked a lot better.
A s you can see, a territory with bad quality can be very frustrating and a
waste of resources. In the case of Oracle, it was part of their strategy to do
maximum account penetration, meaning to throw as many Sales Reps as
possible into every available territory.
M ost companies don’t have the luxury of having so many resources for
such experiments. For them, proper territory scoring is important to be as
eﬃcient as possible. W ith a territory scoring exercise, you can assess the quality
of a sales territory. For example, how many ICPs are within one territory (or
other criteria that measure the quality).
M yG ym24 uses data regarding the account quality (number of locations
per health club) and the G D P per capita as a proxy to score their territories. It’s
well-known that people with higher incomes spend more on their health,
which is why gyms are more commonly found in aﬄuent areas. In addition,
the same demographic wants a ﬂawless consumer experience, which puts

---

## Page 172

pressure on health clubs to provide that experience. Hence, they are a great
target for the M yG ym24 SaaS product.
To determine the territory scores, combine the territory stats from the ﬁrst
step with the territory quality. e rating goes from A  (best) to D  (worst). is
rating is determined by a relation of the three factors: population by the EM R,
G D P per capita, and the number of named accounts within an EM R.
One goal of territory planning is to create relatively equal territories. From
the M yG ym24 example, you can see these territories aren’t equal. Same with
the Oracle example. is is normal. You can strive for equality, but it is
important to understand that you may never reach it.
U ltimately, you want to align the right salespeople with the right customers
or regions to maximize success rates. To do that, you need to understand the
likelihood of an individual rep to win under certain criteria.
For example, you can assess past data to understand if a rep is performing
better when they’re selling to a speciﬁc industry, company size, region, etc. To
understand that, you can look at the characteristics of the Sales Rep, such as
education level, introvert vs. extrovert, personal interests, location, language, or
years of experience. is results in a better understanding of how well a rep
could perform in a certain territory. Please note that you’re predicting the

---

## Page 173

future based on historical data and judgments. It’s simply a prediction of future
performance, not a sure guarantee.
In a perfect world, we’d always assign territories to the best-ﬁtting reps.
A nd in some cases, that’s possible. But in reality, you’ll face constraints. For
example, if your business model requires ﬁeld sales, then location becomes a
constraint. A nother constraint might be language. ese need to be factored
into the assignment of a territory.
M yG ym24 understands that in-person visits to health clubs are essential
for their business model to succeed. To support this, they hire remote
salespeople who are each assigned to a speciﬁc EM R region, which is a key
regional constraint for their operations. W ithin larger EM Rs, they have
multiple reps. is allows those territories to be further divided into sub-
territories. For example, the M unich territory might be split into M unich
North and M unich South, based on zip codes located in the northern and
southern parts of the city.
Businesses that have a BD R team in addition to a Sales team often assign
the same territories to the BD Rs. In most cases, there are fewer BD Rs than
Sales Reps, which means a single BD R may be matched with multiple A ccount
M anagers (A M s). W hen assigning BD Rs, it’s important to apply the same
qualiﬁcation criteria you would use for the AM s.
e resulting sales territory is summarized in the table below.
Territory Account M anager Business D evelopment
Representative
Rhine-Ruhr EM R A nna Frank
Frankfurt/Rhine-M ain
EM R
Tom Elena
Berlin/Brandenburg EM R Tina M ike
W hen going through this process, keep these best practices in mind:
Involve experienced Sales Reps in the process: Involving experienced
Sales Reps helps to gain qualitative insights into the territories that might have
been overlooked whenlooking at quantitative data alone. Qualitative insights

---

## Page 174

can include account history, competitor knowledge, or cultural nuances—the
type of information you wouldn’t usually ﬁnd in a CRM  or database.
Build in review cycles: Your product, regulation, or the market
environment can change the eﬀectiveness of your territory. erefore, it’s
important to periodically check the viability of the territory. A  common review
cycle might be once a year, but it may diﬀer if any of the factors change faster.
For example, a new regulation comes into place like G D PR, or a new
technology disrupts the market (G enerative AI).
Reassign, re-distribute, and reserve: Your sales force will change over
time; some people will leave, and others join. is will require your territories
to be reassigned or redistributed. is part will usually require alignment with
the existing reps on how to best take over.
Some organizations follow a yearly account distribution strategy. In most
cases, I would not recommend that. Sales is about relationships and planning.
Having to rebuild them every year is not constructive. I would not recommend
this for any B2B sales motion. Some B2C companies might do it, but even
there, it could be avoided. Often, you will have to reserve territories for new
hires.
A t the beginning of the year, you should do capacity planning to
understand how much manpower you will need to reach your target. is
involves a hiring plan that indicates at what time a new salesperson will join.
is will require you to reserve a territory for the person or to pre-plan a
redistribution. W hichever method you follow will depend on your sales cycle,
short-term goals, and team capacity.
Sales Capacity Planning
Sales capacity planning is a process for determining how many salespeople a
company needs to hire to reach its targets.
For example, if your revenue goal is $10 million, but the current team can
only reach a target of $6 million, you’ll need to hire more reps to ﬁll in the
gap. It’s a delicate process. You can overhire, meaning there is not enough
demand, which leads to high operating costs. On the other end, you could

---

## Page 175

underhire and leave huge parts of revenue on the table because you can’t serve
the market.
Proper planning is important.
e majority of businesses already perform sales capacity planning.
Historically, it’s an initiative led by the VP of Sales and supported by SalesOps.
But it’s also a task within RevOps. e holistic view of the business in RevOps
tends to improve its accuracy. D epartments like M arketing, Customer Success,
HR, and Product all have an impact on your plan.
For example, M arketing needs to provide a realistic estimate of how
attainable it is to generate enough leads to fulﬁll a certain target. Customer
Success insights can provide an estimate on how much of the revenue goal will
be determined by upsell or cross-sell opportunities. A nd the product roadmap
can anticipate demand for a major feature or product release. is requires
organizational-wide collaboration to have an accurate plan.
Here’s an overview of the various stakeholders involved:
Stakeholder Role
CRO/VP Sales Project owner and approval
RevOps Project lead
M arketing A ligns marketing activities with the plan
Customer Success U psell and cross-sell plans
Product Product roadmap
HR Hiring and training plans
Finance Financial viability of the plan
It’s important to keep in mind that sales capacity planning is an attempt to
predict the future. M ost attempts fail, or else we would all be investing in the
right spot. e best we can do is make an informed decision based on past
experiences. e more data you have, the more accurate your capacity
planning will be.
is doesn’t mean that you can’t do capacity planning without data. If you
are a young company, then you naturally don’t have any. In those cases, it’s

---

## Page 176

advisable to consult industry benchmarks and make educated assumptions.
at’s also considered planning.
W hen it comes to hiring, you have to factor in ramp time, which is the
time until a new hire is expected to reach the full quota. Plus, there will be
attrition: a metric that quantiﬁes the rate at which employees depart an
organization, whether voluntarily or involuntarily. ese data points are just
the beginning. e key is the more data points you include in your capacity
planning model, the more accurate your model becomes.
D ata Points at Impact Capacity Planning
•Historical Sales Performance •Ramp time •Previous quota
•Productivity •Selling time •Quota attainment
•A ttrition •Training time •M argin of safety
•Team composition •Seasonal ﬂuctuation •A verage deal size
•Sales cycle •M arket conditions •Price increase
•Territory speciﬁcation (languages)
ese are just the minimum requirements to get started. You need to
understand the new target, last year’s targets, and quota attainment. Often,
businesses don’t reach their target, so you need to factor in a margin of safety
when creating a new one.
Pro T ip: Since this can quickly become complex, I’ve put together a detailed
explanation and a formula template to help you set your own targets and
complete sales capacity planning for your business, along with an example from
M yG ym24. You can ﬁnd it at https://revenuewizards.com/revops-book.
D on’t start this process too late. W hen you do, it creates a bottleneck in
your sales capacity, and you increase the risk of overhiring.

---

## Page 177

Sales capacity planning is a continuous process, and it shouldn’t be done
just once a year. Instead, it should happen quarterly or even monthly,
depending on the size of your organization. By doing so, it helps ensure your
targets stay realistic by using the most recent data. Internal and external factors
can shift quickly, and by planning consistently, you reduce the risk of
underhiring and leaving potential revenue on the table. A t the same time, it
helps you avoid the costs of overhiring when revenue goals can’t be met.
In small companies, this can be done in a spreadsheet. But once your team
reaches some level of complexity, you should consider using dedicated
planning software. is allows for more accuracy and reliability.
e results of sales capacity planning not only shape the Sales hiring plan
but also help align the entire G T M  function. M arketing can time campaigns
to match the onboarding of new hires to ensure a steady ﬂow of inbound leads,
while Customer Support can adjust its resources and renewal team capacity to
keep pace.
Eﬀective sales capacity planning ensures alignment across the G T M
function, but having the right team in place is only part of the equation. To
truly maximize eﬃciency and impact, your eﬀorts must be targeted toward the
right audience.
Deﬁning Your Ideal Custom er Proﬁle and
Buyer Persona
Often, I receive emails asking if I need an aﬀordable tech developer for my
company. e problem is, we’re a consulting company without any software.
Other times, another company promotes freight services for large containers,
and I’m not sure what we would ship as a consulting company. But the best is
when I receive promotions in a language that I don’t speak.
is type of sales outreach is called spray and pray. W ith a bit of luck, the
message will reach the right person.
One problem with the above method is that it hurts your brand reputation.
Recipients are likely to report you as spam. e other problem is that your

---

## Page 178

Sales and M arketing teams are wasting time and resources on prospects who
would never buy your oﬀering.
To avoid this problem, create an Ideal Customer Proﬁle (ICP).
Your ICP deﬁnes the ﬁrmographic, environmental, and behavioral
attributes of accounts that are expected to become a company’s most valuable
customers.
D eﬁning an ICP was an activity that was done long before RevOps was
introduced. It’s an exercise that’s usually led by the M arketing team. is
approach can turn out great, but it also can lead to shortcomings.
For example, if M arketing performs the exercise, the view of marketing will
likely be dominant. It’s the same if any other department takes it on: Sales will
have a sales perspective, while Customer Success will have another. is doesn’t
mean the perspective of these departments aren’t relevant. eir contribution is
crucial, but it can be better organized.
RevOps is the ideal facilitator for an ICP project. e ICP deﬁnition gets
more accurate because you receive input from M arketing, Sales, Customer
Support, and even Product. It represents a more holistic view of the business.
In addition, RevOps is best suited to complement it with data, which paints a
more accurate picture.
e result will impact and beneﬁt the whole organization. M arketing
campaigns will depend on it, Sales will optimize its targeting, Customer
Success understands which clients might be more at risk, and Product knows
which roadmap items to prioritize.
Determining Your ICP
D eﬁning your ICP diﬀers depending on whether your company sells B2C or
B2B. For B2C, you’re interested in the characteristics of an individual, while
for B2B, you’re interested in the characteristics of a company.
To start identifying your ICP, begin with a brainstorming session that
includes a project team across diﬀerent departments. e goal of this workshop
is to deﬁne ICP criteria relevant to your business (I’ll share some of these
criteria later on). is can be company data such as size or region. You also

---

## Page 179

want to gain individual data such as age or education levels. D eﬁning this
criteria will often lead to interesting discussions, as there isn’t always a common
agreement. A n alternative approach is for RevOps to do a primary analysis,
which is a great starting point for the discussion.
M y preference is for a middle-ground solution. RevOps can do some
research and show the fundamental criteria but doesn’t provide all of the
information. is helps facilitate the conversation between teams.
W hen gathering criteria, it’s important to consider how easy it is to get this
information. For B2B companies, data like industry, region, and headcount are
usually publicly available, and data providers have them as part of their
oﬀering. Other data points might be interesting, such as burn rate, revenue, or
what technology they use. However, these data points might be impossible for
an outsider to get (like burn rate). Other points like revenue can be available
for companies that are required to report their ﬁnancials. K eep the accessibility
of the data in mind when coming up with your criteria list during that ﬁrst
workshop.
Pro T ip: G enerative A I and automation platforms like n8n or Copy.AI allow for
a lot of help when ﬁnding this criteria. ey can be used to scrap websites and
search for speciﬁc information. For example, they can search if the company is
hiring for speciﬁc roles or if they follow speciﬁc initiatives that relate to your
oﬀering, like capturing CO2 emissions. ese A I automations are a great
complement to existing company data and intent providers.
Once you have your criteria, go one step further and add another
dimension by grouping them into three categories:
1.Best ﬁt = W here you put the most focus on.
2.G ood ﬁt = ey also make a good client.
3.Bad ﬁt = To avoid at all costs.

---

## Page 180

e beneﬁt of having three categories is that it’s more realistic. A n ICP
that’s too narrow can be limiting in scope. e danger here is that the G T M
team will neglect potential buyers who are in the market for your solution.
is often manifests in lead rating systems, where some leads are assessed as
low priority and are not followed up on, leading to missed revenue
opportunities. Having a good lead category helps to limit these types of issues
and helps the team prioritize them properly.
Lastly, having a bad ﬁt category is explicit in what to avoid. is is very
helpful because a prospect might indicate a purchase interest, but you know
with certainty that it’s best not to serve them. For example, if the potential
customer is from a region with a lower purchasing power and your prices aren’t
competitive enough. D eﬁning a clear bad ﬁt helps to train the whole team on
the topic.
Pro T ip: You can transfer the lead scoring methodology into your CRM . M any
CRM s have a built-in scoring mechanism, but it’s recommended to use your own
scoring. It can be a simple drop-down that scores leads. For example, Best Fit =
L3; G ood Fit = L2; Bad Fit = L1. You can use any scoring you like as long it is
simple to use and talk about.
Here’s an example from M yG ym24.

---

## Page 181

Note: You can have multiple values in one category as in the “country” column.
Buyer Personas in B2B Com panies
For B2B companies, you are also interested in potential buyers within a
business. ese are called buyer personas. In B2C, your ICP is directly the end
customer. In B2B, you have the additional layer of the company and the
stakeholders involved in the purchasing decision. A.k.a., the buyer persona.
at’s why you should create an ICP classiﬁcation for the company and a
buyer persona classiﬁcation within that ideal company proﬁle. e best way to
do this is to complete a similar exercise as above to list out the criteria that are
relevant to evaluate buyer personas and add the categories of best, good, and
bad ﬁt. Instead of listing company criteria, use individual criteria. is can be
demographics like job title, seniority, or languages. But more importantly, add
characteristics like pain points, challenges, or needs.
Here’s how this looks for M yG ym24.

e next step is to create a buyer persona card. is is a representation of a
speciﬁc ﬁctional buyer. e goal is to list out all of the individual criteria of the
buyer persona so that it’s easy to share with your team. is helps you
personalize the value proposition of your product.
Buyer Persona Card

Persona Name John Enterprise
