Creating your ICP and buyer personas are foundational steps for your
whole business. Here’s how they can be used:
•W ebsite copy and marketing material.
•Find leads and intent data.

---

## Page 183

•Lead scoring.
•Impact on territories.
•Train the Revenue teams.
It’s important to note that you should review your ICP and buyer persona
at least once per year—even more so if you’re a new business. It takes time for
a young business to ﬁgure out the right ICP (and you’ll likely get it wrong at
ﬁrst). A t the same time, your ICP can change independent of maturity level.
For instance, a new industry regulation might come into play that requires
time tracking, so those businesses are now on the hunt for a solution.
So be sure to review it often.
Constructing Your Custom er Journey Map
U nderstanding your ICP helps you to understand who best to engage. Once
you know your ICP, then you want to understand how the customer interacts
with your company at every stage of their journey. e best way to do that is
with a Customer Journey M ap.
W hat is Custom er Journey Mapping?
Customer Journey M apping is a visual representation of every interaction
customers have with your business. is includes every step—from the moment
a prospect might discover you through a G oogle search to their interaction
with Sales, to the purchase, and even up to renewal or churn (if it’s a recurring
business model).

---

## Page 184

Figure 6.3 Customer Journey M ap Stage with customer and company goals
M apping out all of the various stages helps a business fully understand the
customer’s perspective and how your company meets their expectations. It’s
good to complete this exercise for every customer, whether they’re a best (your
ICP), good, or bad ﬁt customer.
D one well, it can be a transformative exercise, as hardly anyone in the
business is fully aware of the entire customer journey. D iﬀerent departments
are usually preoccupied with the complexities of their own line of work. It
becomes easy to lose oversight of how connected the customer journey is.
Creating a Customer Journey M ap is a helpful exercise to further align,
integrate, and foster collaboration between departments.
RevOps is in a great position to lead this process since its focus is across all
G T M  teams. W hen M arketing or Customer Success leads the project, there’s
always a risk of bias. Or, they are simply less aware of what is happening in the
rest of the business. is doesn’t mean that RevOps will do everything on their
own. e role of RevOps is to coordinate the project and work with other
departments to complete it.
Steps to Create a Custom er Journey Map
To create a customer journey map, you need a clear understanding of your ICP
and the various buyer personas. So please complete the ICP exercise before

---

## Page 185

starting this one.
e best way to visualize a Customer Journey M ap is to use a collaboration
platform such as M iro. ere are many frameworks to help you through this,
and they all vary in the number of stages and how they deﬁne them. But the
most common ones have six stages: awareness, consideration, decision,
onboarding, retention, and loyalty.
Stage W hat?
A wareness H ow does the customer become aware of your activities?
Consideration W hat triggers the customer to consider your product or service? A re
they comparing it with other alternatives in the market?
D ecision W hat concrete choices does the customer make to decide to
purchase?
Onboarding W hat steps does the customer take to get started with the product,
and what does success look like for them?
Retention W hat steps are in place to retain the customer? W hy would the
customer care to stay?
Loyalty W hat can be done to encourage the client to endorse our company?
H ow can they go from a user to a fan?
To map these six stages eﬀectively, display them horizontally across your
canvas. en add three vertical categories:
1.Customer
2.Business
3.Review
In the customer journey, aligning what customers want with what the
business needs is critical. Customers are motivated by their desire to solve
speciﬁc problems or achieve a certain outcome, while businesses typically focus
on metrics like inbound leads, revenue growth, or market share. For example, a
business might aim to generate more inbound leads through its content or
campaigns, but if that content doesn’t directly address the solutions customers
are seeking, it fails to resonate.

---

## Page 186

e key is to understand where these two perspectives align and where they
don’t. Take the perspective of the customer and understand what their goals
are. W hat activities are they performing? en, you can ensure the company’s
goals (e.g., lead generation) are achieved in a way that feels natural and
valuable to the customer. For instance, if customers are seeking compliance
solutions, providing educational resources, case studies, or tools that solve
those speciﬁc compliance challenges positions the business as a trusted partner.
is creates a win-win: customers ﬁnd solutions, and the company earns
qualiﬁed leads.
List Out Important Criteria
e next step is to list important criteria for customers. W hile working on
dozens of these projects, I’ve seen time and again that identifying customer
goals, activities, and touchpoints works the best. ese three categories capture
how prospects interact with your business.
For example:
1.W hat are the goals of prospects? is could be to increase revenue,
improve customer retention, or stay compliant with a new regulation.
2.W hat activities will they perform during a speciﬁc stage? is could be
to search the web, talk to peers, or ask for referrals.
3.W hat touchpoints will the customer experience with your business?
is could be on a website, with a chatbot, at a conference, or an in-
person meeting.
A nswer these three questions for each of the six stages.

---

categories are around business goals and measuring success (in other words,

•A ctivities: W hat activities are you performing?
•M essaging style: How do you communicate with the customer?

•Technology: W hat tools are supporting this process?

e best way to ﬁll out this canvas is through a workshop. W hen possible,

revelations and insights as possible.
e workshop isn’t the entire process, though. You also want to
complement what’s identiﬁed with data. is could be data about touchpoints,
CRM  data about conversion points, or customer feedback. You also have the
option to interview customers to learn more speciﬁc insights.

Applying the Custom er Journey Map to Your
Business

---

## Page 190

Remember, your customer sees you as one company, not a bunch of separate
departments. If you book a ﬂight with D elta and they lose your luggage, you
don’t blame their delivery or supply chain team. You blame D elta because, to
you, it’s all part of the same journey.
A  Customer Journey M ap helps your business see their experience in the
same way. It helps teams be hyper-aware of how the customer ﬂows through
the business and ultimately improves business performance. M arketing may
identify better promotional opportunities with existing clients; Customer
Success can better understand when to get involved with Sales, and Sales better
understand where inbound leads are coming from and how to engage them.
Teams are often so busy in their own speciﬁc domain that it becomes easy to
overlook what is happening elsewhere in the business.
On that note, the map is a valuable tool for identifying gaps in your
customer journey, especially at critical conversion points, such as the transition
from the awareness stage to the decision stage. If you can pinpoint where
clients are dropping oﬀ, you’ll be able to address how to improve them. is is
critical because it helps win more of the same level of prospects or helps to
retain more existing customers. Both boost your company’s performance.
A  well-constructed Customer Journey M ap not only reveals opportunities
but also highlights potential areas of misalignment between teams. One
moment that can signiﬁcantly impact the customer experience is the handoﬀ
between Sales and Customer Success. W hen this transition is seamless, it sets
the stage for long-term customer success.
Sales to Custom er Success Hand-off
In SaaS, the hand-oﬀ stage between Sales and Customer Success is crucial as it
sets the course for the entire customer success journey, which is when the
prospect becomes a paying client to the point where they achieve their desired
outcome (and continues to ﬁnd value over time).
e customer success journey starts with the sales process. No matter how
good your Customer Success team is, they’ll be set for failure from the get-go if

---

## Page 191

the product is oversold or if the salesperson overpromises on adoption or
implementation timelines.
To assess this hand-oﬀ process, you need to look at these seven questions:
Question 1: W hat was prom ised?
Sales is under a quota to close deals. To persuade a client, the product or
service is presented in the best possible way to show value. M ost of the time,
this presentation represents what the product can do, but sometimes, features
that can’t be delivered might be promised. is can happen without bad
intentions. Nonetheless, it’s a problem that can result in a bad customer
experience or churn.
Pro Tip: Create a sales culture that encourages honesty. Sales Reps should feel
safe slowing down the sales process when needed. If they are unsure about an
answer, it’s better to say, “I’ll ﬁnd out and get back to you,” instead of guessing.
In the long-term, it creates a more sustainable business model.
To understand what’s promised to the clients, you can use these three
methods:
1.Ask the Sales Rep: e simplest one is for Customer Success to simply
ask the Sales Rep. is is feasible with low volume items that have a
high price. Sales are usually nuanced and complex, so having this
conversation helps transfer knowledge if both actors act in good faith.
W ith higher volume and low ticket size, this method is harder to scale;
the time and eﬀort required doesn’t pay oﬀ. A more structured process
is required.
2.Check the documentation: A more scalable option is to share
documentation with the Customer Success team before handing the
client oﬀ to them. is should cover relevant information they should

---

## Page 192

know, such as the onboarding timeline, requirements for
customizations, or new stakeholders involved. Documentation like this
would best live in the CRM  alongside other customer information.
3.U se sales intelligence tools: You can use sales intelligence software to
record sales calls and capture relevant insights for Customer Success. AI
can automate the process: it can ﬂag everything that’s relevant and
update the CRM  accordingly. is is a great time saver and trust creator
for each team.
e one limitation is that some prospects might not be comfortable
with call recordings. In some industries and cultures, privacy is a big
topic, and call recordings are not accepted. In those cases, it is good to
have a backup process. Should the prospect decline a sales recording,
then the Sales Rep can manually ﬁll out the section. W hat’s important
is that the data ends up in the same destination. is helps to aggregate
the data and with accessibility.
Question 2: W hat are the expectations?
From the start, both parties need to know what to expect. is helps to reduce
unmet expectations and provides clarity. is includes:
•e contact details from both parties.
•e timeline for the onboarding process.
•W hen ROI can be expected.
•W hat is needed from the client.
•Speciﬁcations relevant to your product.
D epending on your business, this list will expand in scope.
Question 3: W hat does the client want?

---

## Page 193

It’s obvious but still overlooked. It’s crucial to capture a client’s goals.
A sk yourself:
•D o you know what the goal is with your product (service)?
•D o their needs ﬁt your capabilities?
•D id they purchase the right product(s) to solve their problem?
e last point can be common when certain features are included in
diﬀerent packages. e customer might assume it’s part of the purchased
package, but actually, it’s not. is can be very frustrating, as they would need
to invest more to get the desired results. ey’ll likely feel betrayed. A void this
if you can!
It’s recommended to apply the SM A RT  framework when capturing a
client’s goals: Speciﬁc, Measurable, Achievable, Relevant, and Time-bound.
A s M ick W iejers, Founder of Customer Success Snack, puts it:
“W ithout a SMART-formulated ‘desired outcome,’ the Customer Success
department needs to do a re-discovery. is is among the biggest frustrations of
Customer Success departments worldwide.”
Question 4: W hen is Custom er Success
introduced?
W hen to introduce Customer Success to the client can vary depending on your
product and the service. is can mean:
•Complexity of the product.
•Stakeholders involved.
•Pre-sales capabilities.
For a more complex product with many stakeholders (e.g. reporting
solution), it makes sense to involve the Customer Success team before the sale
takes place to establish a relationship.

---

## Page 194

In other cases, you might have a dedicated pre-sales team with trained
solution engineers. In such cases, it’s tempting to postpone Customer Success
as you already have experts on the team. One pitfall I have often seen is that
pre-sales teams create demo environments that don’t match the actual
production environments. e consequence is that the initial demo sets a
reference point, and the implementation has to live up to it, which means
there’s a high risk of unmet needs. A gain, this is extremely frustrating for a
client and sets the Customer Success team up for a bad start.
You can avoid this by either having a clear documentation on what is
discussed with the BD R team, have Customer Success involved in the new
business phase or have the BD R team connect with the implementation and
Customer Success team.
Question 5: How is the hand-off executed?
e actual hand-oﬀ sets the stage for the rest of the relationship, so you want
to consider how this process should be handled.
If there wasn’t any contact with Customer Success beforehand, a simple
method is to introduce them with an email. is option might be viable for a
transactional sale but not a complex one.
For more complex products that require personal onboarding, the better
method is to have a joint call between the client, Sales, and Customer Success
teams. is warm introduction is very personal and allows for a smooth
transition. is is what I recommend in most cases; it’s the most personal
handoﬀ process. It sends the message that you care.
In addition, train the team to get into the habit of sending summary notes
for every hand-oﬀ.
Question 6: W hat data can Custom er Success
access?

---

## Page 195

M any onboardings have a bad start due to bad data or Customer Success
having limited access to data. It’s ideal they have access to:
•Contract information.
•A ll sales conversations and emails.
•Client-related notes from sales.
•A ny interaction with marketing content.
Question 7: How is the handoff process
docum ented?
e documentation holds the whole process together. Everything that could be
relevant to Customer Success should be documented.
is includes:
•Having a custom object in your CRM  for the hand-oﬀ (or a good
integration to your Customer Success platform if you use a separate
platform).
•D ocumenting and organizing internal and external facing notes.
•Ensuring everyone knows where to look for the information they need
for their job.
•U sing external notes to share with the client in the hand-oﬀ email.
ese seven questions provide a structured approach to identify gaps and
set expectations, which sets the stage for happy customers from the start.
Sales to Custom er Success Handoff:
Ensuring a Seamless Transition
Aigniﬁcantly Improves Your Tim e-to-Value
By Dr. Lukas M ohr, Revenue & Pricing Operations Lead at M A RK T-PILOT

---

## Page 196

e transition from Sales to Customer Success (CS) is one of the most
critical moments in the customer journey. A fter investing time and eﬀort in
winning a deal, a smooth handoﬀ ensures a strong foundation for customer
satisfaction, retention, and long-term success.
Imagine your organization has just signed a new contract, and suddenly, the
customer ﬁnds themselves interacting with an entirely new team from CS.
How do you ensure continuity, maintain trust, and deliver a seamless
experience? To answer this question, I’ll share practical insights into how we
at M A RKT -PILOT, a SaaS company targeting enterprise customers in the
machine manufacturing industry, handle this topic.
Creating a Seam less Transition: A Hospitality Analogy
To illustrate the ideal Sales-to-CS handoﬀ, let’s use an analogy from our
personal lives: imagine you are traveling and staying at a hotel chain. A fter a
pleasant stay at one location, you check into a second hotel from the same
chain in a diﬀerent city. You expect (at least sometimes) continuity in
service: everything you appreciated in the ﬁrst hotel should be eﬀortlessly
replicated in the second without you requesting it.
For instance, if you had dinner at 6 PM  at the ﬁrst hotel, the second hotel
proactively reserves a table for you at the same time. If you enjoyed an early
breakfast at 6 A M  (because you have the “pain” that you need to start
working at 6.30 A M  the whole week), the second hotel is already aware and
prepares a table accordingly. W hat happened here? Your preferences and
experiences were seamlessly transferred, ensuring a smooth and personalized
experience.
is is exactly what a Sales-to-CS handoﬀ should achieve. e customer
should not have to repeat themselves or feel like they are starting from
scratch. eir goals, pain points, and preferences should be proactively
addressed because this information was already gathered and shared
eﬀectively in the sales process and is stored in your CRM .

---

## Page 197

How to Achieve a Seam less Sales-to-CS Handoff?
W hile this may sound complex, in reality, much of the necessary
information is already available in your CRM , and if not, just take this as a
wakeup call that you need a structured and methodology-based sales
process. e key to success is to ensure that this information is structured,
transferred eﬀectively, and acted upon in a timely manner.
W hat Needs to be Done?
Sales plays a crucial role in gathering concrete insights during the sales
process. rough qualiﬁcation methods like M ED D PICC, BA NT, or other
frameworks, valuable customer-speciﬁc information is captured in the
CRM . is data acts as a catalyst for CS enablement, ensuring they
understand the customer’s needs from day one.
Once a deal is closed, the handoﬀ process should follow a structured
approach:
1.Complete the CS Handoﬀ Playbook: is document serves as a bridge
between Sales and CS, capturing key details from the sales process. It
should be partially pre-ﬁlled with critical insights such as:
Customer’s pain points
G oals and objectives
Customer metrics for success
Compelling events that inﬂuenced their decision
2.Two Additional Required Fields I recommend:
A personalized note from Sales: is should include any nuances not
captured in the CRM , such as speciﬁc expectations, decision-making
dynamics, or personal rapport insights.
Pro T ip: Be aware of the personal component. If Sales discovered personal
information from the buyer side, include this here. is helps to ﬁnd personal
connections and builds trust.

---

## Page 198

Kickoﬀ meeting details: Sales is responsible for organizing the ﬁrst
call between CS and the customer. Since Sales has built the initial
relationship, they should schedule and attend the meeting, verbally
introducing CS and ensuring a warm transition. From there on, CS
can start their magic.
Best Practices for an Effective Handoff:
To further enhance the transition, consider the following best practices:
•M ake the Handoﬀ M eeting Count: is meeting should not just be a
formality but a well-prepared session where Sales highlights key takeaways
learned during the sales process, introduces CS, and reassures the
customer that they are in good hands. is meeting can also be used to
conﬁrm customer goals that were discovered during the sales process and
to agree on a timeline for the next steps.
•Provide Context, Not Just D ata: W hile structured ﬁelds in a CRM  help,
context matters. Sales should share insights beyond numbers, such as how
the customer prefers to communicate, internal challenges they face, or
speciﬁc individuals who are champions within their organization.
•Set Clear Next Steps: Before concluding the handoﬀ meeting, align on
the next steps, milestones, and expectations. Ensure the customer feels
conﬁdent in the transition.
Com m on Pitfalls to Avoid:
1.Lack of Preparation: Entering a kickoﬀ meeting without a proper
handoﬀ document leads to disjointed conversations and customer
frustration. So be sure that RevOps creates such a template. In some
cases, it is advisable to create multiple templates based on the tier or
vertical your customer is in.
2.Over-Reliance on CRM  D ata: W hile structured data is helpful, missing
the human element can make CS interactions feel impersonal.

---

## Page 199

erefore, consider adding a “personal interaction” property in your
CRM  that captures this type of information.
3.No shared information between your tools: Often, CS works in a
diﬀerent tool than Sales does. CS should still be able to receive all
information automatically. But you, as the responsible RevOps who
builds the bridge, should not blindly push all information to CS but
rather create a tailored playbook to reduce information overload.
4.M ake the customer repeat: Clarifying goals with the customer in a
handoﬀ call is important, but don’t make the customer repeat
everything. You should present the customer with the goals that Sales
discovered and let the customer conﬁrm if those goals require an update.
If you get any updates, make sure to update your CRM  as well.
W hy Must the Handoff Matter for You in RevOps?
Ensuring customer ROI through clear processes and eﬃcient information
ﬂow is extremely important. A  key KP I for all SaaS companies should
always be time-to-value—the time a client receives their ﬁrst value from a
product. A n eﬃcient handover is an important catalyst for improving this
timespan, and RevOps certainly plays a major role here by outlining the
overall process and building a seamless technological infrastructure.
Focusing on Custom er Health
For a long time, SaaS companies neglected Customer Success despite the clear
importance of recurring revenue. is was largely due to the abundance of
capital in the SaaS sector over the past several decades. In the so-called
“G olden A ges of SaaS,” the focus was on hyper-growth at any cost.25 is
growth was fueled by new business expansion. e customer experience was
often neglected.
On the buyer’s side, cash was also more abundant, and purchase decisions
were often made without extensive consideration. It was low-risk simply

---

## Page 200

because there was enough cash to invest. M any companies ended up buying
multiple tools that had the same functionality that were rarely used. at may
have been acceptable when capital was loose. But now it’s diﬀerent.
e fundamental challenge in SaaS revolves around the “leaky bucket”
phenomenon across multiple dimensions. A  leaky bucket occurs when
resources such as customers, revenue, or operational eﬃciency are constantly
draining away. is can manifest as churn (customers canceling), declining
usage within existing accounts, ineﬃcient spending on unused software, or
even talent attrition within teams. Combined with decreasing Net Revenue
Retention (NRR), these leaks represent a signiﬁcant challenge for sustainable
growth.
e market consistently shows that investors and boards focus on unit
economics and sustainable growth patterns. ese “leaks” materialize in key
metrics like NRR, which remains one of the most important valuation drivers.
Companies with strong NRR (>120%) consistently achieve higher valuations
than those struggling with retention and resource eﬃciency, demonstrating
how fundamental organizational health is to long-term success.
is adds additional pressure on tech vendors. e gap is shortening, and
retaining customers is becoming harder and more expensive. Companies are
now literally forced to invest more in Customer Success.
at’s why healthy customers are so important.
Healthy customers are critical for businesses with a recurring business
model. ey’re customers who have a high likelihood of renewing their
subscriptions.
A  huge appeal of SaaS companies lies in their ability to generate recurring
revenue from clients. A  common rule of thumb is that the Customer
A cquisition Cost (CA C) for new customers is 4 to 5 times more than the cost
of keeping a customer.26 In other words, keeping customers is way cheaper
than acquiring new ones.

---

## Page 201

Figure 6.7 Revenue D istribution from Existing Customers and New Customers
e shift in how we view customer retention highlights the value of
maintaining a healthy customer base. Even if more investment is required to
keep customers, the margins remain highly proﬁtable. Putting money into this
area makes the business more sustainable in the long run, and the best way to
do this is by building a Customer Health Score.
Building a Custom er Health Score
A  Customer Health Score represents the risk of a customer churning.
U nhealthy clients are more likely to churn over healthy clients. In either case,
it’s the likelihood of an event happening, not a certainty. A ndreas D rakos,
Senior Revenue Operations D irector at Superside, puts it this way: “Your
existing customer base is your most important revenue stream. You’ve already spent
thousands to acquire them, so monitoring and understanding their health will help
you anticipate future situations—both good and bad.”
M any businesses attempt to build a Customer Health Score but often don’t
end up using it because they don’t perceive it’s working. at certainly can

---

## Page 202

happen, but that can mostly be traced back to insuﬃcient data.
ere are many factors that impact the accuracy of your Customer Health
Score. e biggest one is your volume. If you have 100 renewals per year
compared to 10,000 per year, the statistical signiﬁcance is very diﬀerent. e
latter will have a lower margin of error. Businesses with a larger volume ﬁnd it
easier to have a more accurate Customer Health Score. But volume isn’t
everything. It also matters what you measure.
e goal with every score is that it’s easy to use. A nyone in the business
who sees the score should understand the condition of the client. at can be a
scale from A  to E, it can be a star system from 
  to 
  
  
  
  
 , or words
like “very healthy” to “very unhealthy.” Pick one that best ﬁts your culture.
Simple health scores only measure usage data. A  common model to
measure uses:
1.Frequency: How much time users spend on a product and how often
they log in.
2.Breadth: How many users within an account are using the service.
3.D epth: How many key features are being used.
W hile these are a great starting point, they can all be marked green, and
you might still experience signiﬁcant churn.
A  more robust Customer Health Score will include factors related to the
users of the platform and the company. ere are many factors that will impact
the accuracy of your renewal. A  customer might churn even if the user factors
are in a perfect state, but you miss that the company is going through a
restructuring.
e table below provides an overview of the various factors grouped into
four categories. Take this as an inspiration, but your own composition will be
unique to your business. You can ask yourself the following questions to derive
relevant factors for your business:
•D o you expect a certain usage from the user?
•W hat would good usage look like?

---

## Page 203

•D o you know the characteristics of clients that renew? Can you measure
those?
•W hat characteristics do good customers have?
•W hat external factors inﬂuence your industry?
G etting a robust Customer Health Score will also require that you measure
what you want to report on. For example, your product needs to be able to
keep track of logins, session duration, or the usage of key features.
Once you can measure all data points, you can aggregate them in one view.
ere are tech solutions that can help with each part of the process. In the case
of your product data, you can use product analytics solutions such as
M ixpanel, A mplitude, or June to get those data points exposed. ey can send

---

## Page 204

the data points to a dedicated Customer Success Platform (CSP) or your
CRM .
D epending on the complexity of your Customer Health Score, it helps to
send the data points to a data warehouse. You would do the scoring there and
then send it to the CSP or CRM .
For each factor, I recommend you ﬁrst check the technical feasibility of
capturing it. I have worked with clients where the Customer Success team
wanted to have a score that monitored the usage of certain parts of the
product, but the Product D evelopment team was not able to build it without
major changes to the product roadmap. It’s okay to start with a lighter version
and extend it over time.
A n easy-to-use score doesn’t necessarily indicate a simple underlying model.
e goal is to provide a user-friendly output for teams in Sales, M arketing, or
Customer Success. W hile the model itself may be complex, the end user
doesn’t need to see or understand how it works, only that it’s actionable.
e ﬁrst step is to transfer the end user scale, A  to E in this example, to a
point system. To keep it simple, 0 to 100 ﬁts best.
e next step is to understand how the points are distributed across the
diﬀerent categories.
A  good starting point is to assign equal weight to each category, especially
if historical data is limited. However, let’s say M yG ym24 has data showing that
the ‘Company’ category is particularly important in predicting customer
health. In that case, it should be weighted more heavily. For example, assigning
it 60 points.
W ithin the ‘Company’ category, speciﬁc attributes might carry the most
inﬂuence. If so, those attributes could receive the majority of the weight, say,
40 out of the 60 points.

---

## Page 205

Finally, give each factor a range of points that can be attained. e table
below shows an example of the usage category. Repeat it for each category.
U sage (20p)
Log-ins frequency 5
Time in app 5
A ctive users % 5
U tilization of key
features
5
e ﬁnal step is to link what you measure to your points system.
For example, log-in frequency could be measured by how many days a
week a user logs into the app. For each day someone logs in, they get 1 point.
A nything above ﬁve logins is 5 points. A nother option would be to count the
number of login days and divide it by seven for days per week. For ﬁve days,
that would be around 0.71. en multiply the number by the maximum
points (5 in this case), and you’ll end up with 3.6 points.
It helps to create diﬀerent test versions of the score and then match it with
historical data to see which model would be more accurate in predicting churn.
Building a Customer Health Score can quickly become a complex exercise,
but it’s also time well invested.
U sing the Custom er Health Score to Your
Advantage
e main purpose of creating this score is to take a proactive approach to client
retention. e status quo is that businesses don’t pay attention to an account
until they receive the cancellation request. A t that point, it’s too late to save it.

---

## Page 206

But many clients can be prevented from churning with a health score. A  well-
implemented one serves as a warning signal. It drives the attention of the
Customer Success team where it’s needed.
Imagine you only have the time to pay attention to one customer out of
three. Two have a score of B and one of C. It’s clear where your attention
should go. e score made the decision for you.
Once you examine an individual customer, the underlying score becomes
more meaningful. For example, you might notice that engagement is low while
other categories appear healthy. is could indicate that the client is fully self-
suﬃcient. Or it might suggest that current engagement methods aren’t
delivering value. In that case, the focus shifts to ﬁnding new ways to provide
meaningful value to re-engage them.
Pro T ip: M ake use of a “rescue mission” status in your CRM . It can be a ﬁeld
that is triggered automatically when a score reaches a certain limit or a manager
marks it as such. is drives more attention to save the prospect from churning.
It also allows you to evaluate how many of your rescue missions were a success.
It’s also important to be realistic. You can’t save every client, and sometimes
it’s okay to let a client go. If your product changes signiﬁcantly, some early
adopters might not like it anymore. at’s totally ﬁne, as long as there are more
clients who prefer the new version. Knowing when to let a client go is very
healthy.
Once you’ve established a strong focus on customer health, you’ll want to
leverage the Revenue and Product teams to amplify your eﬀorts.
How to Build a Client Health Score that
Reﬂects True Custom er Satisfaction
By Dr. Lukas M ohr, Revenue & Pricing Operations Lead at M A RK T-PILOT

---

## Page 207

Every patient is diﬀerent, and so is every client. However, this doesn’t mean
you need to treat every client diﬀerently to assess your client’s health: key
metrics are typically shared among all your clients. Just as heart rate and
blood sugar levels are universal indicators of a patient’s health, certain
customer metrics should inﬂuence your Customer Health Score, regardless
of the type of customer. To build a health score, you need to know the
underlying foundation of what makes your customer’s heart beat.
How to start with your health score
Building a health score is an iterative process, and you likely won’t get it
right from the start. A t M A RKT -PILOT, we have iterated for more than 6
months and are still in ﬁne-tuning mode. To distill the most important
metrics that should inﬂuence your health score, you need to understand
your customers’ pain points and the solutions, basically the job to be done,
they seek. For that, you should always include internal and external
perspectives on your health score.
Internal Factors Directly Inﬂuenced by
Custom er Success Managers (CSMs)
Internal factors are those that your company, especially your CSM , can
directly inﬂuence. Key internal factors include:
•CS Pulse: W hat is the CSM ’s overall sentiment regarding the customer?
at should always be kept up to date by the CSM  and can be
represented as healthy, concerning, or poor. As simple as that.
•Last M eeting Date: W hen was the last meeting with your client?
Including this component in the health score provides immediate
visibility into customer engagement and CSM  activity. A regular meeting
cadence of 1, 3, or 6 months should be maintained based on your
business model. is information can easily be updated automatically:
simply set a timestamp in your CRM  for when your CSM  has a meeting

---

## Page 208

with the customer. Via an automated workﬂow, the timestamp then
updates the date ﬁeld that inﬂuences your health score.
I use timestamps for almost every occasion. D o you want to understand when a
certain activity occurs in your sales process or customer journey? U se a
timestamp. D o you want to calculate time to value? U se a timestamp that is set
when you update a certain ﬁeld with information that serves as proof that the
client has realized their ﬁrst value. en, easily calculate the duration between
the contract start date and the timestamp.
External Factors (Custom er-Driven and
Calculated Metrics)
External factors are those that the CSM  cannot directly inﬂuence and are
either provided by the customer or derived from calculations (such as ROI):
•Customer Engagement: is includes the last login data and the number
of activities within a given period. Based on your product, deﬁne healthy,
poor, or unhealthy usage patterns. For complex products, consider also
tracking the number of features used. Having an understanding of what
features are used is also a very valuable upsell potential metric.
•ROI: Measuring ROI is challenging, as it often includes assumptions
such as savings, productivity, or eﬃciency gains when a certain number of
users engage with your solution. Let´s be honest here: don’t include any
vanity or wishful thinking metrics. If the ROI is not accepted by your
client, then don’t include it in your measurement. I always ﬁnd it
amusing when a solution provider sends me their assumptions regarding
the ROI we’ll receive since it’s often very hypothetical and not based on
hard metrics.
•Tickets and support requests: How frequently does the customer submit
tickets or report bugs, and what number indicates a healthy customer?

---

## Page 209

•Survey responses: is may include the NPS, though some prefer a CSA T
instead. Personally, I am not a big fan of the NPS. I consider the
questions meaningless, and they’re very inﬂuenced by cultural aspects. If
you prefer a single-question approach, consider the Sean Ellis Score,
which asks: “How would you feel if you could no longer use [product]?”
If the user doesn’t feel bad about not using your product anymore, you
certainly have an issue.
•Company-speciﬁc metrics: To create these metrics, ask yourself what the
core job-to-be-done by the product is and how often, daily, weekly, or
monthly, that “job” should be executed by your clients. If you have this
understanding, start tracking the numbers and factoring them in.
M A RKT -PILOT, for example, enables customers to make price changes
for their spare parts based on the market intelligence we provide.
Naturally, a key metric for us is the number of price changes made by a
client within a certain period.
Weighting the Health Score Factors
e weighting of factors is a crucial element that determines the success or
failure of your health score. A  best practice is to use a 0–100 scale,
categorizing customers into:
•G reen (Healthy)
•Yellow (M oderate Risk)
•Red (High Risk)
To deﬁne the weightings of each factor, conduct an exercise with your CSM
team, asking them to assign weight to them. Collect their insights and
calculate an average. is is a great way to establish a data-driven starting
point and to increase the acceptance of the health score by your CS
department. Your weighting will likely change based on the feedback you
receive from CS when they start working with the health score. But this
ﬁne-tuning is part of the process.

---

## Page 210

Individualization vs. Standardization
A lmost every RevOps M anager is a fan of standardization and scalability,
and you should not lose this passion when creating health score(s). M ultiple
versions of health scores make it hard to maintain and create confusion
among the management team. Even though many businesses have diﬀerent
customer segments, it’s best not to create too many diﬀerent health scores. I
suggest creating diﬀerent versions based on two criteria:
•Customer Tiers: You can create separate health scores for diﬀerent
customer tiers, typically ranging from three to ﬁve. W hile the key
inﬂuencing factors should remain consistent across tiers, the weightings
can be adjusted. For example, a healthy engagement for a small customer
might involve quarterly meetings, whereas for a Tier 1 customer, monthly
meetings may be required.
•Lifecycle Based: It can be helpful to create two to three diﬀerent health
score models based on the customer’s lifecycle stage. For example, an
onboarding health score should prioritize time-to-value as a key factor,
while a separate health score can be used during the success stage of the
customer relationship.
Taking Proactive Action Based on the
Health Score
If a health score remains consistently high or low over a certain period,
consider implementing automated notiﬁcations and playbooks:
•Consistently High Score (e.g., for 6+ months): Automatically create a
CSM  note to assess potential upsell opportunities.
•Consistently Low Score: Trigger a “Code Red” playbook, assigning the
CSM  a task to conduct a health check meeting with the client. A code
red playbook can include a written description of the customers’ situation

---

## Page 211

and a step-by-step action plan on how to improve the health score
systematically over the next few weeks and months.
e Customer Health Score is not set in stone. A s your business, products,
and customers evolve and change, so should your health score. But keep in
mind: Changing your health score too frequently makes it hard to measure
historical data, so aim for a consistent pattern with longitudinal data.
Gaining Feedback Flow: The Secret to More
Custom er Input
Everyone I speak to, no matter the industry–whether it’s tech, manufacturing,
or retail–wants more customer feedback.
Feedback is gold. But it’s hard to get.
at’s where better alignment between the Revenue and Product teams
comes into play.
Product Feedback
e Revenue team is in continuous contact with prospects and customers.
ey receive feedback on a daily basis. is feedback is essential for the
Product team to improve the product.
W ithout feedback, the Product team faces the risk of prioritizing the wrong
roadmap features or completely missing ones that would provide value for the
customers. Even the emergence of new products is possible through product
feedback.
The Effect on Monetization
New features and new products signiﬁcantly impact its pricing. It’s essential for
the Product team to consider how a new product feature can be monetized
during the development phase. Here, RevOps proves invaluable as it helps the

---

## Page 212

Product team adopt a revenue-centric approach, which product owners and
managers might not always naturally embrace.
RevOps should deliberate on several critical questions: which tier is
appropriate for this feature? Is it suitable to be oﬀered as an add-on? D oes it
justify a price increase?
However, this kind of evaluation is also inﬂuenced by competitors and how
they price similar features. For instance, security features like Single Sign-On
(SSO) are increasingly seen as standard oﬀerings rather than premium
enterprise add-ons. ere’s an ongoing debate about whether basic security
features should be included in base packages or oﬀered at a premium. I
support the perspective of developers who believe that features like SSO should
be part of the base package.
Establishing a pricing committee is highly recommended. is committee,
facilitated by the RevOps team, should include members from teams like Sales,
M arketing, Customer Success, and the Product team to ensure comprehensive
representation. e details and functions of pricing committees will be
discussed later in this chapter.
Bugs, Complaints, and Product Feedback
Every time a customer complains about your product, it’s an opportunity to
turn their experience around and make them happy.
Product feedback, including bugs, can be managed successfully. Success
does not necessarily mean that the issue is resolved or that a product feature
will be built. Success is about managing the expectations of the client.
W ith product features, this means:
•U nderstanding why a feature is important.
•W hat a client wants to accomplish with the feature.
•If there’s an option to solve the request with existing means.
M ost companies have a good process for providing feedback to the Product
team. But many struggle to keep the client informed about updates or don’t

---

## Page 213

even attempt to reach back out to the client. is creates frustration and can
lead to a lost customer.
e solution is very simple: make sure you provide updates to your client.
Your feedback loop should be bidirectional, meaning it’s easy for the client to
provide feedback, but also easy for your team to keep the client up-to-date.
M any companies struggle to get feedback loops right. M ost have none,
some have a good feedback system, and a few have a bidirectional feedback
system.
Pro T ip: Building a bidirectional product feedback loop can boost customer
satisfaction while also generating ideas for product improvements and new
oﬀerings. e key is to keep your clients informed throughout the process. is
transparency builds trust and leads to happier customers. You can implement
this using project management tools integrated with your email platform or by
adopting dedicated customer feedback solutions.
Once you’ve got a strong feedback loop in place, it’s time to further align
internal teams, like Deal Desk and Legal, to streamline your B2B sales process.
Optimizing B2B Sales with a Deal Desk
e D eal D esk team is a mix of diﬀerent subject matter experts from Legal,
Finance, and SalesOps. It’s a team that helps manage complex sales in a B2B
business.

---

## Page 214

Figure 6.8 RevOps Organizational Chart with D eal D esk
In smaller companies, this responsibility might be shared, but as volume
increases, it will develop into a dedicated full-time team.
Figure 6.9 M oving from a distributed team to a dedicated team with deal complexity
How deeply involved the D eal D esk is will depend on the complexity of
your sales. Should your Sales Rep still lead the communication with the client
during the negotiation phase?
You can see it as a dance. If your buyer has subject matter experts from the
Legal or Procurement teams on the project, then you should do the same. Your
Sales Rep will be more useful spending time on other deals than spending it on
administrative tasks.

---

## Page 215

It’s more important for enterprise clients. For them, additional
requirements are in place to close a deal. is includes:
•Custom contract negotiation.
•Request for Information (RFI) and Request for Proposal (RFP)*
•Security Questionnaires.
•Custom payment plan.
•Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG ) performance.
*If your business is very reliant on RFI & RFP responses, you might want to
consider a dedicated team for that.
Enterprise companies need to screen their own operations and supply
chains regarding ESG  criteria. is means small businesses that supply
enterprises are also impacted by the regulation, albeit indirectly. If they don’t
screen suppliers, they face penalties. is means an additional reporting burden
on SM Es, including startups.
Can your Sales team do this on their own? M aybe, if they spend all their
time on it. But then, they won’t be closing much.
at’s why it is so important to invest in a Deal Desk.
So, what are the pros and cons of a Deal Desk?
Pros Cons
Time-saving and increase in sales velocity A dditional investment required
Subject matter experts on the task Internal communications need to be
excellent
Reduced bottlenecks
Overall, the D eal D esk is a new function that has gained popularity as it
increases sales velocity. If you think your business can beneﬁt from it, then you
should deﬁnitely invest in one.

---

## Page 216

Pro T ip: Invest early on in a D eal D esk if you sell complex B2B enterprise deals.
is will signiﬁcantly reduce your sales cycle and increase revenue.
Access to Contractual Inform ation
One important goal of the D eal D esk, and especially the Legal team, is to
provide transparent access to key legal information. In fast-growing companies,
what often happens is that a contract gets signed, everyone is happy, and then
someone stores the contract information in a folder that only a few people
have access to.
In a slightly better scenario, somewhere, there is a note about the contract
in the CRM . You can imagine that it’s easy to make a mistake, such as missing
a renewal, during this process. It’s important to provide access to contractual
information.
In the end, these are the most important data points you need to make
clear:
Renewal
is includes renewal terms, auto-renewal clauses, indexation, cancellation
terms, break-out clauses, or anything else relevant to the renewal.
Pricing Cap
Enterprise clients often negotiate such a clause into the contract, so any
information that indicates a price cap should be noted.
Being able to report on this information will be important when you plan
to increase prices. ose with a custom price cap will require unique
messaging.
Logo U se
Capturing logo use is another important part of the contract process. is will
make the life of the M arketing team easier. Often, you’ll see the following
terms:

---

## Page 217

•U nrestricted logo and name use.
•Logo and name use only with permission.
•Logo use prohibited but name can be used.
•Prohibited logo and name use.
The Closed-Won Process
I recommend having a solid closed-won process in your CRM , so you have
accurate information about the contract.
W hat Does Closed-Won Mean?
W hen you set an opportunity to “closed-won,” you are sealing a ﬁle. e
record in question should be locked and used only for reference (read-only).
e equivalent is a tax advisor who closes its annual book.
Similar discipline is required when setting an opportunity to closed-won.
e closed-won will impact your revenue numbers and the information you
report to the board. Hence, maintaining accurate data tracking is critical.
To mark an opportunity correctly as closed-won, you can follow these
seven steps:
1.Receive countersigned contract.
2.D eal Desk or deal owner populates contract data in CRM .
3.A ttach contract copy to CRM  (and other storage systems).
4.Conﬁrm the accuracy of all ﬁelds required for closure.
5.Request approval for closed-won.
6.Set to closed-won.
7.Inform relevant parties that it is closed (manual or automated).
e scope may diﬀer at times, and some of it can be automated, especially
if you have a high volume of sales. However, the idea is to build a solid control
system for your closed-won opportunities. e quality of your reports,
forecasting, and customer experience will depend on it.

---

## Page 218

Pro T ip: W ork with “exit” criteria in your pipeline and CRM . Exit criteria can
be optional or mandatory values in the CRM . M eaning that optional values are
not required to proceed in the CRM , while without mandatory values, one can
not progress the deal. For example, to set an opportunity to closed-won, a full
billing address is mandatory, while a purchase order (PO) number is optional,
as not all clients have one. I recommend deﬁning exit criteria for each deal stage.
A s you reﬁne your control system for closed-won opportunities, it’s time to
address one of the most impactful elements of your sales process: pricing.
Forming a Pricing Com m ittee
Is an important activity you can do to increase revenue. A  pricing committee
takes a strategic view on pricing decisions. W ithout a pricing committee,
companies face the following pitfalls:
•Loss of credibility.
•Suboptimal pricing.
•Pricing does not reﬂect value.
•W rong people making the decision.
•Product updates not reﬂected in pricing.
A  pricing committee can solve all of these issues. Too often, companies
neglect their pricing strategy. Or, pricing changes happen ad hoc. It also helps
to avoid ambiguity in the sales cycle. Sales Reps sometimes don’t have clear
guidelines for when and how to apply a discount. In worst-case scenarios, they
take pricing into their own hands and quote a price or discount that was not
pre-approved. ese issues happen when there is no clear guidance on pricing.
e pricing committee has a wide set of responsibilities, as seen in the next
ﬁgure.

---

## Page 219

Figure 6.10 Responsibilities of a Pricing Committee
e ideal cadence to hold these meetings is once a quarter. If changes are
identiﬁed, a working group for the respective change is launched.
W ho Should Be Involved
e goal is for all G T M  departments to share their input, which assures that
the views of each business unit are reﬂected—unlike when, for example, only
Sales and M arketing make a pricing decision. Chances are that critical views
that impact pricing will be missed. is is especially important for complex
products.

---

## Page 220

It’s best for the committee to have a project coordinator. e RevOps
leader is best suited to guide these meetings due to their holistic view of
revenue, and they should also actively contribute to the meeting.
ere should be one decision-maker for the pricing committee. It’s
important to understand that it’s not a vote. One person will consider the
input of all participants and make a decision, which is typically the CEO.
Here’s a typical breakdown of participants.
D epartment Representative
Revenue/Sales CRO
M arketing M arketing leader
Finance CFO
Product Product leader
Success Success leader
D ecision maker CEO
Project coordination RevOps
*e above is an indication. M ake your own committee according to your own
needs so that it reﬂects your business.
W hile the pricing committee ensures that pricing decisions are strategic
and aligned across departments, the eﬀectiveness of these decisions is only as
strong as the documentation supporting them.
Establishing SOPs
Let’s imagine a scenario where Susie has to submit the RFP for a big banking
client she’s been working on for weeks. She emails Tom from D eal D esk to
help her submit the RFP in the client portal.
Her heart sinks when she receives an out-of-oﬃce (OOO) reply: “I’m sick.”
She’s in shock. She’s had bad experiences with this in the past. A t her
previous company, she lost a deal since nobody else aside from the D eal D esk
had the information she needed. She’s about to panic.

---

## Page 221

In his reply, Tom states that A nna is covering for him. She reaches out, and
A nna is able to pick up where Tom left oﬀ. A nna is from Finance and has all of
the information since it’s well documented in the CRM . Even though A nna
has never worked with the client’s speciﬁc procurement platform, she knows
exactly what to do. Her A I-assisted tools execute the required steps. ey are
able to submit the RFP well before the deadline.
A  successful organization can be measured by its level of documentation. If
one employee is out-of-oﬃce, someone else can jump in and take over the role
by reading the required documentation.
For start-ups, this might seem unthinkable, but as the companies get larger,
this is normal. A  multinational company like A mazon could not operate
without exceptional documentation. A mazon is famous for its writing culture.
Not only are all processes documented, but they prepare elaborate written
brieﬁngs after each meeting so that everyone has the same base level of
understanding.
Part of the documentation process is to create a Standard Operating
Procedure (SOP). ese are documents that provide standard guidelines for
your team members to complete a given process. SOPs are often used to ensure
that operations run smoothly: with cohesion, performance uniformity, and in
compliance with safety measures and regulations.
A s you can imagine, this level of documentation impacts the whole
company. I believe that good documentation must be the foundation and
habit of every department—from M arketing to Sales to Operations to
Product.
Let’s review some best practices when creating SOPs:
Written by Subject Matter Experts
SOPs should be written by individuals knowledgeable about the activity. A
team approach is beneﬁcial, especially for complex processes, as it incorporates
diverse experiences and promotes acceptance of the document.
Clear W riting and AI

---

## Page 222

SOPs should be written in a concise, step-by-step, easy-to-read format.
Information should be unambiguous and straightforward. U se the active voice
and present tense. Avoid redundancy and excessive length.
Here’s an example of unclear writing:
“D uplicate entries should be reviewed and adjusted regularly, when needed,
to keep the data organized.”
Here’s a clear version:
“Review and clean up duplicate entries in the CRM  every Friday to ensure
data integrity.”
U se G enerative A I tools for consistency across your SOPs. e A I can be
trained in your company style so that it has your speciﬁc company voice. You
can also easily generate translations for every language your company needs.
Describing Purpose and Scope
Begin with a brief description of the purpose of the work or process, including
any relevant regulatory information or standards. D eﬁne specialized or unusual
terms (e.g. a glossary). Use illustrations, tables, and diagrams for clarity.
Review, Approval Process, and U pdates
SOPs should be reviewed and validated by individuals with appropriate
training and experience. Signature approvals indicate management review and
endorsement. ey should also be systematically reviewed. e review schedule
can depend on the speciﬁc need of the organization.
Maintaining a Master List
M aintain a master list of all SOPs, indicating details such as SOP number,
version number, date of issuance, title, author, status, and historical
information on past versions. is list can be crucial for audits and for
ensuring current practices are followed.

---

## Page 223

Keep it U p-to-Date
Processes change, and so should the documentation. Create review cycles for
the most important aspects of your documentation. e need for consistent
updates isn’t just a nice-to-have; it’s fundamental to maintaining organizational
trust and eﬀectiveness.
is will be especially important as the amount of SOPs increases. Creating
proper documentation gives you a competitive edge over your competitors.
A lmost everyone agrees that it’s important, but few are doing it. e challenge
goes beyond mere maintenance. W hen documentation becomes outdated,
teams quickly lose trust in all documented processes, which creates a cascading
eﬀect that undermines operational eﬃciency and conﬁdence. Each update
reinforces organizational reliability, while neglect erodes the foundation of
trust you’ve built. ink of documentation like a map guiding your
organization through complex terrain. A n outdated map doesn’t just lead you
astray once; it makes people question whether to trust any direction at all. In
the end, the quality of your documentation reﬂects the quality of your
operations. It’s either a cornerstone of excellence or the ﬁrst crack in your
foundation.
How to U se Your Docum entation
Here are a few important use cases.
Input for Your Internal AI
Your documentation is the lifeblood of your internal A I. e strongest use
cases of A I for businesses are generative A I and Large Language M odels.
Reliable and up-to-date data is a necessity for your A I, or else it will provide
you with false information or the so-called hallucination. (I will discuss
hallucination in more detail later on, but for now, just know that bad data makes
your AI less eﬀective.)
But let’s assume you have solid documentation in place; then, the A I
elevates its eﬀectiveness. Here are a few examples of what good documentation
enables:

---

## Page 224

Onboarding new employees: e better your documentation, the faster
and easier new employees will be onboarded. W ell-structured documentation
can facilitate the onboarding of new employees. If you add A I to this, it
becomes even more eﬀective. M ost businesses use internal wikis like
Conﬂuence, Notion, or ClickUp. ose tools have an A I chatbot on their
platform, allowing you to interact directly with the content. e times of
endless searching are over; just ask the AI and ﬁnd out what you need.
Continuous training and enablement: A s you know by now, training
doesn’t stop with onboarding. Having your training documented serves as a
great reference point for all your employees. People miss training or join later.
Having everything documented and recorded helps share knowledge. Lastly,
they serve as additional input for your internal AI.
Systems, integration, processes, and instruction: Imagine you document
the entire operation of your company. W hen an employee leaves or is sick, they
shouldn’t become a bottleneck. Someone else can proceed with the work
because it’s documented. is level of documentation is very rare, but I have
seen it in some businesses. e companies that deploy it are highly successful.
Take Susie, for example. She didn’t lose a deal simply because someone was
sick.
is is very powerful.
Playbooks
Playbooks are detailed documentation for a speciﬁc business function or job
roles. ey include everything a person needs to operate in their role. is can
be a playbook for a BD R, customer success agent, or marketing.
e beneﬁt of a playbook is that it only shows information that is relevant
to that person. is results in more structure for the individual and addresses
security concerns. Rarely do people in a company have the same security
clearance. For example, it doesn’t make sense for a BD R to have access to all
HR procedures.
Pro T ip: D eploy internal A I tools that are job-speciﬁc. For example, you can
have a BD R Bot and a Legal Bot. Both should have access to the same general

---

## Page 225

company information, but they should each have their own domain expertise
unique to the speciﬁc bot. is makes the bots more reliable and secure.
W hen creating a playbook, there are a few things to consider. First, it
should be a living document that receives continuous updates with its own
review cycles. Second, it’s good to have multiple contributors to the playbook.
For example, managers and individual contributors can jointly contribute to
the various sections. A daptation gets better when everyone has skin in the
game. Lastly, apply the same best practices as with the SOP.
e result will be playbooks that are unique and relevant to speciﬁc
business functions.
U sing Tools to Your Advantage for
Docum entation
You can ﬁnd many tools to help you facilitate the documentation process.
A t the time of writing, I recommend the following platforms:
•Company W iki: ClickUp, Notion, or Conﬂuence. Any of these tools are
great to build out your internal knowledge base. Most likely, you already
have one of those tools in-house.
I don’t recommend investing in a separate knowledge base because:
1.M ore tools means less chance of adoption.
2.e above tools all invest heavily into their product, making them
better over time.
•Screen Recording: Loom or Zoom Clips. I use a screen recorder on a
daily basis. ey are great to explain a new process, pre-meeting brieﬁng,
or to solve a request. e best feature about screen recordings is that you
can reuse them.

## Page 226

Setting the Protocol: SLAs for GTM Team s
Traditionally, a Service Level A greement (SLA ) is a formal document that
deﬁnes the level of service expected between a service provider and a client. A
very common example is the uptime of a software solution. A  client expects
that a service will be available from the time they purchase, and a service
provider guarantees a certain uptime, for example, 99.5%.
You can apply the concept of SLA s to diﬀerent departments of the
business. In the end, they are a commitment device, ensuring that something is
going to happen.
Here are four examples of SLA s between diﬀerent departments:
1.M arketing and Sales
“Every month, M arketing will deliver 40 marketing-qualiﬁed leads to Sales.
A nd Sales will contact each lead within 12 business hours of receiving it.”
2.Customer Success and Product
“Customer Success will invite the Product team to 12 customer calls every
month to gather product feedback. Product will attend all of those customer
calls.”
3.Sales and Finance
“Sales will submit all contract and pricing details to Finance within 16 business
hours of closing a deal. Finance will review and approve the contract within 24
business hours to ensure timely invoicing and revenue recognition.”
4.RevOps and GT M  Teams
“RevOps will conduct a monthly performance review meeting with
representatives from Sales, M arketing, Customer Success, and Product. Each
team will present their key metrics and progress against SLA s. RevOps will
facilitate the discussion to identify and resolve any misalignments or
roadblocks.”
In the examples, you’ll notice that the commitment goes both ways.
M arketing will do one thing, and Sales will provide a response to the activity

---

## Page 227

that M arketing did. is is what creates alignment between teams.
Socratic Reﬂections
•D o you have a clear ICP? W hen did you last review your ICP?
•How can your company beneﬁt from a Customer Journey M ap?
•W hat three actions come to mind to improve the conversion points
between teams? Execute the easiest one this month.
•W hat did you learn in last year’s sales capacity plan? W hat will you
improve next time?
•W hat can you do to make the procurement process easier for your seller?
•W hen was the last time you received a compliment for the clear
documentation that you created? Never? You know what to do.
•W hen was the last time you said thank you to your Legal team for
helping close a complex negotiation?
•W hat could be improved in your pricing strategy?
•How is AI changing your pricing strategy?
•W hat SLA s does your business need?
