## Page 228

CHAPTER 7
THE THIRD W EIGHT –
SYSTEMS
“Any suﬃciently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic.”
Arthur C. Clarke
Systems include all of the technology that executes the G T M  and RevOps.
ey build the foundation for your business and help you stay eﬃcient while
you scale.
is pillar is ultimately connected to integration, one of the core elements
of RevOps. Integration allows for better collaboration and alignment. It
provides the so-called 360° view of your business and leads to automations that
make your business more eﬃcient.
Deﬁning Ownership and Accountability
Systems are an important part of RevOps, but who should own it? As a
company, you have three options: each department, the Information
Technology (IT ) team, or RevOps.
As a reader of this book, you’d probably guess by now that the ﬁrst option
is against the core philosophy of RevOps—to avoid silos. If you take this
approach, you also risk tech overspend. W hen each department purchases its
own solution, the company can end up with diﬀerent solutions that do the

---

## Page 229

same thing. I have seen companies with less than 50 employees maintain two
CRM s. is method may work temporarily for small businesses, but it will
quickly lead to problems: more overhead is required in managing the two
systems, there’s inconsistent data between them, and more time is spent on
training users. Not to mention, it further increases isolation between teams.
at’s everything we want to avoid in RevOps!
e central management of systems should be a shared responsibility
between RevOps and your IT  team, depending on your company’s maturity.
W hen RevOps manages systems, it includes all of the systems related to the
G T M  teams. is may include a CRM , a revenue intelligence tool, or a
M arketing Automation Platform (M AP).
e ones that are typically outside the scope of RevOps are all non-G T M
systems, such as Human Resource Information Systems (HRIS), email clients,
or all your developer tools.
is raises an important question: where do you draw the line to determine
which systems fall under RevOps and which should remain with IT ?
RevOps is a young discipline. M any companies that implemented RevOps
teams around 2017 integrated G T M  systems under the RevOps’ ownership. In
our research paper “RevOps in 2025: D ata, Process, Leadership, and AI,” we
surveyed over 180 RevOps professionals to understand who owns their G T M
tech stack. It shows that RevOps is mostly responsible for G T M  systems.
Notably, the survey respondents were primarily from companies less than 10
years old, where RevOps has managed GT M  systems from the start.27

---

## Page 230

Figure 7.1 D istribution of who manages G TM  Tech Stack
ere are many beneﬁts to having RevOps own the G T M  systems. For
example, changes are made faster because there are fewer stakeholders involved.
e link between business requirements and systems capabilities is better as
RevOps understands both well. is also leads to better integration.
Furthermore, there is less overspend, as there is a better understanding of the
capabilities of the tools.
One downside is that RevOps is perceived as a system administrator.
Technically, that perception is correct; RevOps does operate systems, but it’s
strategic about them.
U ltimately, the system admin perception stays with many RevOps teams,
especially in small businesses with only a few employees or an individual.
Continuous communication helps to get rid of the stigma, but getting rid of
the system administration part may be the better solution.
In most enterprises, all tech systems are owned by the IT  team. e reasons
are related to governance, security, and centralized management. Even if an
enterprise has a RevOps department, systems would still reside within IT. is
isn’t a surprise: the majority of those enterprises existed before RevOps was

---

## Page 231

even invented. In an enterprise, you can’t introduce RevOps and hand them
the responsibility to take over all revenue technology. In some cases, it’s better
to leave revenue systems with IT, like if you’re an enterprise deploying the
Enforcer M odel (discussed in the ﬁrst chapter).
In that case, it’s better to leave revenue systems with IT, who can be the
ultimate owner of all systems. W hat will vary is the degree of control that the
RevOps team has over them. is may range from some additional system
permissions up to full administration rights. U ltimately, this will diﬀer per
company and system.
Revenue Systems IT Systems
Customer Relationship Platform (CRM ) Human Resource Information Systems
(HRIS)
M arketing Automation Platform (M AP) Email Clients
Sales Engagement Platforms Project M anagement Software
Revenue Intelligence Internal Communication Systems
Enterprise Resource Planning (ERP) System
e main downside is the speed of execution. W hen RevOps is in full
control, then changes can be deployed much faster than when IT  manages it.
At the same time, smaller companies can take more risk as errors are less costly.
Enterprises can aﬀord fewer errors. In a small company with just ﬁve users, a
change in the production environment that causes an error might lead to a
productivity loss of 10 minutes per person. at’s a total of 50 minutes – not a
big deal. But now, imagine the same error happening in a company with 1,000
users. Suddenly, you’re looking at 10,000 minutes of lost productivity! At this
scale, even small errors can have a massive impact.
is is why most enterprises adopt standardized systems, procedures, and
security protocols. It’s the most eﬃcient way to manage them at a large scale.
Building the Fram ework: The Foundation of
RevOps Systems

---

## Page 232

Businesses have foundational systems that are deemed necessary for revenue
teams to function. Here are the top three:
Custom er Relationship Managem ent
Your Customer Relationship M anagement (CRM ) is the foundation for your
Revenue team. In the most simple terms, a CRM  is a system for managing all
of your company’s relationships and interactions with customers and potential
customers.
e majority of tools used by Revenue teams are designed to integrate with
your CRM .
Marketing Automa tion Platform s
M APs help automate various marketing activities, such as email marketing,
behavioral targeting, lead prioritization, and personalized advertising. ey
allow for detailed reporting and help you understand the ROI of your
marketing eﬀorts.
Spreadsheets and Business Intelligence
Spreadsheets and Business Intelligence (BI) software help to guide decision-
making. BI comprises the strategies and technologies for data analysis and
management of business information. W e’ll explore these tools in more detail
later on.
Beyond these three foundational systems, you’ll also need productivity
tools to keep track of projects. Emailing and data storing solutions help you
engage with clients and prospects, while signature solutions allow you to sign
contracts.
Key Components of Essential Revenue
Solutions

---

## Page 233

Revenue solutions build upon the three foundational systems and make your
revenue processes more eﬃcient.
Data Enrichm ent
ese are solutions that provide you with customer data, such as account
information like industry, revenue, or company size. But it can also be contact-
speciﬁc data, such as job title, email address, or phone number.
Recently, there’s been a rise of intent data. As the name suggests, intent
data provides an intent if a customer is actively looking for a solution or even if
they are interested in it. is helps you target prospects more eﬀectively, as
they’re more likely to be within a purchasing window.
Sales Engagem ent
is type of solution allows your Sales team to engage more eﬃciently with
prospects and clients. ey automate or semi-automate sales cadences, which
includes emails, calls, or social media interactions such as LinkedIn messages.
Since most of the engagement can be automated, Sales Engagement
solutions can easily be misused, since they invite abuse and spamming of
potential prospects. is can damage your reputation in the long term.
As a response to spam, G oogle and Yahoo updated their sender guidelines.
Since February 2024, stricter rules apply to those sending more than 5,000
emails per month to personal email addresses, so typically in B2C contexts.28
G oogle, for example, only allows a spam rate of 0.3%. at means that only
three out of 1,000 emails can be ﬂagged as spam before G oogle blocks your
organization from sending out any more emails.
is shows how important it is to be responsible when using these types of
solutions, especially now, as new AI tools have the potential to send unlimited
amounts of email with automated research and personalized email at scale.

---

## Page 234

Pro T ip: Sales Engagement solutions allow  you to put certain measures in place
to avoid being ﬂagged as spam. ese include authorizing your domain, setting
delays betw een email send, daily send limits, and email w arm-up services. ese
are quick wins you can do to improve delivery.
Conﬁgure, Pricing, Quoting and Contracting
Solutions
In high-transactional businesses with complex deals, you’ll require a Conﬁgure,
Pricing, Quoting (CPQ) solution.
e lines between CPQs and Contract M anagement solutions are
becoming blurry. In recent years, Contract M anagement solutions have
evolved to provide a holistic approach to contracting, connecting CRM  and
ﬁnance systems for automated invoicing. Certain CPQs can directly issue a
simple contract, and some Contract M anagement Solutions allow for quoting.
is is a good trend to see.
For some businesses, having one quote that serves as the contract is
suﬃcient.
Revenue Intelligence
Revenue Intelligence solutions gather, analyze, and interpret data across your
funnel to make informed decisions, taking out the guesswork. ey do this by
pulling data from various sources, such as the CRM , marketing, call
recordings, or emails, into one central location. en, they run various AI
algorithms to make informed decisions.
Custom er Success Solutions
For SaaS companies, it’s important to manage your recurring revenue properly,
as the majority of revenue comes from the recurring part of the subscription.

---

## Page 235

Customer Success solutions help to maximize the customer lifetime value
(CLT V) of their clients.
ese solutions are still in their infancy compared to others, as Customer
Success has traditionally been neglected within the SaaS community since
there’s been a stronger focus on closing new business than keeping existing
customers. As I noted earlier, this trend will change because buyers have
become more critical of their technology stack due to the market environment.
Hence, sellers need to invest more in customer retention to keep existing
clients. Customer Success solutions are a part of this investment.
Other Revenue Solutions
ere are many more solutions that help you become even more eﬃcient in
your revenue engine. ese include solutions that help you respond to requests
for proposals (RFPs) or security questionnaires. Solutions exist for lead scoring,
lead routing, account-based marketing, product data integration, partner
management, and more. is makes the revenue solutions market ﬁercely
competitive.
Related Systems
In the ﬁnance domain, the ERP system and billing solutions for issuing
invoices ﬁt into this category. ese systems help automate the quote-to-cash
(Q2C) process. Systems related to products and data, which may capture user
behaviors relevant to calculating a Customer Health Score, also fall into this
category. A data warehouse is included in this group as well.
W hile all of these are powerful solutions for your business, there is one
emerging category that has the potential to have a massive impact on the
eﬃciency of your team.
You guessed it: AI.

---

## Page 236

Mapping Your Infrastructure: Tech Stack and
Systems Roadma p
e sheer amount of technology systems, AI or not, can be overwhelming for
any business. It becomes important to keep track of it all. Companies that lose
track of their technology systems face these risks:
•Overspending on technology that’s not used.
•Purchasing similar products that do the same.
•Suﬀering from bad adoption and low ROI.
•Tools that start to precede processes.
•Losing track of renewal dates.
All of these risks can be traced back to mismanagement. e worst case of
it is shelfware, which occurs when software is purchased and never used. is
often happens due to a poor onboarding process or simply a shift in priorities.
W e want to minimize shelfware and avoid it completely if we can.
e best way to avoid the above is to have clear oversight of your
technology systems. e creation of a tech stack map and a systems roadmap
helps.
Create a Tech Stack Map
A tech stack map is a visual representation of your existing revenue technology.
You map how the diﬀerent platforms are integrated with each other.

---

## Page 237

Figure 7.2 Revenue Tech Stack M ap Example
e beneﬁt of creating this map is that almost no one in your business has
a bird’s eye view of the tech stack. is helps you further identify where you
have overlapping apps and where you have gaps.
Pro T ip: T o create a tech stack map for your ow n business, you can use the
following template: https://revenuewizards.com/revops-book.
Put Together a System s Roadm ap
Creating a tech stack map helps you understand your existing technology, but
it also signiﬁes where you might have technology gaps. at’s why the next step
is to create a systems roadmap.
Creating a systems roadmap ensures you address gaps at the right time. It
can be tempting to invest in new technology, but doing so at the wrong time
typically means you’re wasting money on it. It’s important to have a well-
deﬁned and tested process in place before introducing new tools; building a
process around a tool after the fact is very discouraging. As we often say,

---

## Page 238

“processes precede tools.” A systems roadmap also helps you plan and budget
more eﬀectively for future technology investments.
Here’s what a systems roadmap looks like:
Figure 7.3 RevOps System Roadmap
To build one yourself, map out the various departments in the columns:
M arketing, Sales, Support, Legal, RevOps, etc. In the rows, add an indicator of
growth, such as the number of clients, leads, or partners.
Note that you don’t want to use only one indicator of growth. Chances are
that if your forecast is connected to time, it will be oﬀ. Having actual client
numbers to indicate growth is more accurate. Instead, use a time frame
reference that relates to your growth numbers.

---

## Page 239

W hile growth indicators are essential for accurate forecasting, another
critical area that requires careful consideration is your purchasing strategy. Just
as you wouldn’t rely on a single metric to gauge growth, you shouldn’t rely on a
single factor when deciding on software purchases. W ith so many options
available, it’s easy to be swayed by persuasive marketing.
The Purchasing Process
ere is plenty of software you can purchase. W hat seems important may
actually not be needed. It’s very tempting to buy new software that aligns with
the pain points you experience in the business. But you have to be careful.
M arketing teams will craft persuasive messaging around those pain points and
tie them directly to their value proposition, serving you a remedy to your
problem by just buying their software. A U serlane and PwC study from 2023
shows that 55% of software implementations fail to deliver an ROI.29
at’s why it’s important to be strategic in your software purchases. e
best way to do this is to develop a purchasing strategy and process that aligns
with the company’s needs and trajectory. e goal of this is that you have a
systematic approach to purchasing software. e beneﬁts are:
•M aking purchasing decisions at the right time.
•Adding technology that ﬁts your processes.
•Adding technology that ﬁts your existing tech stack.
•Technology ﬁts with your requirements and goals.
•G etting the best value out of a purchase.
The Principles at Play W hen Purchasing
Your purchases should be guided by the internal discoveries of stakeholder use
cases, existing infrastructure, and requirements for the tool. All of these
ﬁndings would ideally result in a list of principles—a list of non-negotiables
that the product should always have.

---

## Page 240

An example list could be:
•Cloud-only products.
•A native integration to Salesforce is a must-have.
•Single Sign On (SSO) required.
•EU  server location required.
•ISO or SOC2 certiﬁcation.
Principles help you ﬁlter software that aligns with your long-term strategy.
Purchasing Authority
Software purchases diﬀer in their scope. Some have minor impacts, such as a
tool that reviews spelling, while others impact your entire business, like a
CRM . You want to deﬁne who can make purchases and at what level.
Here’s how a client of Revenue W izards deﬁned this:
“Every team can propose solutions to purchase. e H ead of RevOps w ill
assist with the process. e Business U nit team can make the purchasing
decision on its own as long as it complies w ith the purchasing principles and
the purchase is below $10,000. Purchases above $10,000 require approval
from the CFO and CT O.”
D eﬁning purchasing authority creates clarity for the whole team.
Evaluation Criteria
One mistake I often see is when people make purchase decisions based on a
requirement sheet with strict M oSCoW  criteria: “must-have,” “should-have,”
“could-have,” and “won’t-have.” ese concepts were invented in the early
2000s for Product D evelopment teams. Some IT  teams love them, which also
explains why they made it into the software evaluation process, but they’re not
ﬁt for this purpose.

---

## Page 241

ey give an indication, but who buys software just because of its features
nowadays? Features are a commodity. Agile work methods, improvement in
D evOps, and AI technology allow companies to ship features within weeks,
not years.
Clearly, your core feature requirement is important, but that’s just part of
it. It’s more important that it stays within your principles, and that will make
your decision much easier.
W hat’s even more important than features is how well your team will adopt
the new tool. M any companies purchase a tool but can’t realize the ROI
because of poor adoption by teams. One way to solve this is to involve end
users of the software in your evaluation process.
ey understand the day-to-day work and will quickly notice how good a
potential new tool is. ese early adopters also serve as ambassadors for the rest
of the team, which helps drive adoption.
Lastly, you want to get referrals about the product. But don’t get them from
the oﬃcial vendor-vetted reference. Ask your personal network for references,
or ask users in a popular Slack community. In the space of RevOps, that could
be Pavilion, RevGenius, RevOps Co-op, or W izards of Ops.
Once you’ve evaluated a potential tool, it’s time to focus on ensuring its
seamless integration within the current tech stack.
The Art of Integration
As you know by now, collaboration, alignment, and integration are the essence
of RevOps. You can integrate processes with each other, but there’s also a
technical element to it. In this section, you’ll learn the connection between
diﬀerent technologies.
Integrations are not made equally. ere are various types of integrations
that you want to leverage at diﬀerent maturity levels of your business and the
speciﬁc use case. ese include API integrations, embedded iPaaS, workﬂow
tools (e.g., Zapier, Make), and native integration.
Let’s look at each of them in more detail.

---

## Page 242

API Integrations
e Application Programming Interface (API) is code that allows two
applications to talk to each other. Nowadays, almost every application leverages
APIs in one way or another.
is can even be within the same product. For example, the web interface
(front-end) of a music streaming app talks to the database (back-end) to check
the duration of a song. at’s an “API call.” APIs also allow apps to
communicate with other apps on the internet. For example, an integration
between a CRM  and a M AP.
In short, APIs are fundamental. e whole internet as we know it could
not function without them. ey build the foundation of all other integration
types. App service providers provide you with API documentation. is shows
how to set up the API and what information you can integrate.
API calls between applications can be for free or paid. It will depend on the
service provider. For instance, if you want to access the API of X or Salesforce,
then you get a certain amount of free API calls, but you have to pay for
anything above the limit.
API calls are prized either because the data contains value (e.g., in the case
of X, the data is within each post) or because the volume is very high and
payment is justiﬁed to maintain the infrastructure to run the system. A
combination of both is possible.
Often, APIs are the only choice when there’s no native integration
available, or you require custom functions that a native integration might not
allow. APIs are great to use when you have the technical skills in-house to set
up and maintain them; coding skills are usually required. ey are best used in
enterprises due to their ﬂexibility. Overall, APIs create ﬂexibility but are harder
to deploy and manage.
Pro Cons
Tailored customization Coding skills required
Highly available M aintenance required
M anaging security

---

## Page 243

Price can be a pro or con. If you have the developer skills in-house, this can be an aﬀordable
option, but if not, it can also be expensive.
Em bedded Integration Platform -as-a-Service
Embedded Integration Platform-as-a-Service (iPaaS) applications, also known
as middleware, are plug-and-play integrations. is means you can connect
multiple SaaS applications together through the iPaaS without having to code
or manage the integration themselves. e iPaaS solution manages the API
connection of the various applications. ey do the heavy lifting and study the
various APIs of the most popular applications and provide you with a simple
user interface that allows you to connect various applications.
W hen to use?
iPaaS is best used when you want to connect multiple apps in your tech stack.
is will reduce, if not even eliminate, the need for coding skills to make your
integration work. ey are also very beneﬁcial when you have a high volume of
API calls, as they are usually priced per connector and not per API call. is
unlimited limit of API calls is beneﬁcial when sending data from your CRM
system to the ERP system.
Pro Cons
Operations users can use it High entry cost
Scale well Integration availability depends on the
vendor
Secure Available for most popular apps
Customization Vendor lock-in
Workﬂow Tools
W orkﬂow tools like Zapier are an easy way to start with integrations. ey are
simple to use for almost any business user, and they work with a wide set of
apps. U sually, you can start using them for free, and then you pay more as the
number of API calls goes up or for the integrations you are building.

---

## Page 244

You should use workﬂow tools when you want to get started with
automation and there are no native integrations available. Furthermore, these
tools are great for small businesses and when an integration needs to be quickly
deployed. It’s also a good option if you want to test a process.
However, you can create technology debt with workﬂow tools. I’ve seen
many organizations accumulate 100+ workﬂows over the years. ey’re now
stuck with them and can’t change them easily. In addition, a high volume of
API calls can become costly. ese tools often oﬀer limited customization and
control. For example, they may lack built-in features to prevent duplicates. As
a result, duplicate records are created when sending data to systems like a
CRM .
Pro Cons
Low entry cost Limits in functionality
Easy to use Tech debt
Secure Can become expensive with high-volume
Native Integration
Native integrations are usually part of your software package (sometimes, they
are part of a premium plan or as an add-on). e beauty of native integration
is they’re managed directly by the service provider you purchased the software
from. is allows them to continuously improve the integration and remain
open to feedback. A prominent example is the native HubSpot and Salesforce
integration.
A solid native integration can even be a reason to prefer a certain platform
over another. Having said that, there are also moments when the business
requirement outgrows the capabilities of native integration. U sually, this
happens with enterprise use cases as the complexities grow. D espite the native
integration, you could consider building a custom integration or using an
iPaaS solution.
Pro Cons
Low entry cost (or no extra cost) Not always available

---

## Page 245

Easy to use Some functions can miss
Secure
Improves over time
Integration and AI
e use of AI is brilliant for integration use cases. For an AI, this isn’t a
problem, as the coding skills required to connect APIs are rather simple. In
addition, the instructions for connecting the various platforms are also
repetitive, such as: combine this ﬁeld in platform X w ith the same ﬁeld in
platform Y. is makes it very easy to deploy AI for integrations.
All of the above use cases can be enhanced with AI. For example, you can
use a G enerative AI like ChatG PT  to read API documentation and help map
the ﬁeld names you want to connect, essentially taking care of the most
diﬃcult part of the process.
W orkﬂow tools and iPaaS platforms take this a step further by oﬀering
preconﬁgured integrations. M any now support natural language commands,
allowing you to say something like, “Connect platform X with Y and ﬁlter for
Z,” and the system handles the setup for you.
is will be the new standard on how to do integrations.
Once you know how to integrate your tech stack, it’s time to automate
them.
Enhancing Efﬁciency with Autom ation
Automation is a process where repetitive tasks are handled automatically by
technology, reducing the need for manual intervention and enabling your
organization to run more eﬃciently. ey’re extremely useful when scaling.
Building automations for the sake of automation is not ideal; you want to
use them when they make sense for your organization. Building out
automations can be time-consuming, and you most likely have limited
resources.

---

## Page 246

W hen automations make sense, they create many important beneﬁts for
your whole business.
Figure 7.4 Five Beneﬁts of Automation
e impact of these beneﬁts is more revenue, reduced G T M  costs, and
more proﬁts. G reat beneﬁts to strive for, but implementation at the wrong
time can lead to the opposite. Hence, it’s crucial to have a process ﬁrst and
follow a framework for when to automate.
Process Over Automa tion
In most cases, you want to draft a process before considering automation. is
helps you better understand if and when automations are needed. Businesses
that change their processes frequently should clearly focus on the process ﬁrst
before automating it.
e risk of premature automation is that it requires more resources to build
than the value it creates. It’s tempting to build out too much automation from
the start. But it can backﬁre. To help you ﬁnd the right timing, use the SERVE
framework.

---

## Page 247

is framework helps you to understand when you should automate a
process for your business:
1.Scale: Is the volume justifying automation?
2.Ease: How easy is the process to automate?
3.Risk: Does it reduce risk (e.g., human errors)?
4.Value: W hat value will it provide?
5.Expense: Does the expense justify the investment?
Let’s apply this framework to two examples.
Exam ple # 1: Autom ating Your Em ail Logs
Say you want to automate the emails your Revenue team sends to clients.
Check if it SERVEs you ﬁrst:
1.Scale: High, because many emails are sent daily.
2.Ease: Easy, because a native integration is available.
3.Risk: Reduces the risk of forgetting to log an email.
4.Value: Clear history of every interaction to better engage with the
customer.
5.Expense: Small expense and often included in a CRM  standard oﬀering.
Based on this evaluation, it is a good idea to spend the time and resources
automating it.
Exam ple # 2: Autom ating Invoices
Say you want to automate the creation of your invoices. Your business sells
enterprise products with a starting price of $400,000.
1.Scale: It’s low due to a high price tag.
2.Ease: It’s complex, as you need to connect a CRM , Quoting, and
Invoicing + Controls.

---

## Page 248

3.Risk: Reduced risk of sending out the wrong invoices.
4.Value: Time savings, as well as getting paid faster.
5.Expense: It’s an expensive project and might require additional software.
M ost likely, the cost will not justify the investment. In this case, it might
be better to improve the manual process ﬁrst by building in human controls
before sending it out. In many cases, a solid process will outshine automation.
How to Ensure Adoption
A PwC study reported that a staggering 55% of software projects fail to deliver
a ROI.30 e reasons for this are poor technology adoption. Still, too often,
software is purchased with the promise of delivering an ROI, but little eﬀort is
spent on implementing the platform and continuously training the users to use
it. e rest of this chapter will look at how we can increase technology
adoption and the corresponding ROI.
Build for the U ser in Mind
An eﬀective way to drive user adoption is to build a user experience (U X) that
is useful, easy, and eﬃcient.
e term U X is more commonly used in web and app design. U X
designers are fully aware that a better user interface (U I) creates a better
adoption of the app. U sers spend more time in the app and perform the
desired behavior.31
e same knowledge can be applied when RevOps deploys apps to internal
users.
RevOps has three options that impact the UX of an app:
1.App selection,
2.Customization, and
3.Custom developments.

---

## Page 249

e ﬁrst choice is to select apps with a strong U X that supports the desired
behavior.
Let’s say you need an app to dial phone numbers. e main requirement is
that it can be used where the reps spend most of the time, namely in the
CRM . You want an app that makes the call and logs it quickly. Let’s say a
dialer can save two clicks per call. ose two clicks take 10 seconds, and with
5,000 dials a week, you save 50,000 seconds or almost 14 hours! at’s the
power of great UX.
e next choice is about customizations, which usually create conﬂict
between RevOps teams and the stakeholders that RevOps supports in Sales,
M arketing or Customer Success. How can you make sure that it’s not a burden
for people to enter data in the CRM ?
Progressive Field Capture
In a full lead capture method, all of the ﬁelds are captured the moment a lead
is added to the CRM . e advantage is that you’d have all the data from the
start. But it’s often not practical because not all of the data points might be
known at this point.
e alternative is to use progressive ﬁeld capture. It ensures you’re
collecting relevant data at the right stage of the sales process without
overwhelming the lead while optimizing the information ﬂow for your Sales
team.

---

## Page 250

Figure 7.5 Full Field Capture vs Progressive Field Capture
There are three stages of it.
In the New Stage, the focus is on assigning the lead to the right salesperson or
team. To do this, you collect basic demographic information such as the lead’s
name, location, and company.
In the W orking Stage, the goal shifts to researching the lead to improve
outreach. At this point, you gather more speciﬁc information about the lead
and their company, such as their job title, company size, or industry, to better
tailor your approach.
In the Qualiﬁcation Stage, you collect decision-making data to better
position your product and prepare for an oﬀer. is includes knowing the
technology the lead’s company currently uses (to demonstrate alignment with
your product) and capturing the full address, which may be needed for
contracts or logistics.
is progression ensures you collect the right data at the right time and
makes the process eﬃcient and relevant. You’re able to capture data as you
progress with the lead along the sales cycle.
You don’t waste time on gathering data that may not be relevant later on.

---

## Page 251

Three-Click Rule
D o you know where to ﬁnd the setting to change your screen resolution? On a
M acBook, this setting is three clicks away from the home screen. is is Steve
Job’s 3-Click rule. M acbook design principles say that every feature should be
accessible with a maximum of three clicks.32
is is simplicity at its best.
In your line of work, you may not always be able to accomplish the 3-Click
rule as you probably don’t have a team of U X designers and engineers that can
optimize your platform for you. However, you can follow the same principle to
make your processes simpler. In the end, the 3-Click rule accomplishes a better
user experience and saves time when performing standard tasks. Your users will
thank you for that.
As a RevOps professional, it’s your duty to ﬁnd the most productive path
and reduce ineﬃciencies. In the end, this is RevOps. W e optimize the revenue
process to drive revenue.
End-U ser Advocates
Technology adoption starts with purchasing the software. As mentioned earlier,
having some end users on the evaluation team helps later on with software
adoption. is doesn’t mean your whole team needs to be part of the process;
depending on the size of the company, 2 to 4 people are enough. ese end-
user representatives will become advocates for the rest of the team.
Training
Training is critical for the adoption phase.
at’s why I want to reiterate the diﬀerent training methods we discussed
earlier in the Enablement chapter.
Training depends on how complex the software is. U sually, a mix of
diﬀerent methods works best. It’s important to train your current team and

---

## Page 252

also create a plan for onboarding future employees, including written and
video resources.
Change Managem ent
Software adoption can face resistance despite having end-user representatives
and a sophisticated training plan. e reason for that is psychological. On
average, people don’t like much change, so you need to ﬁnd ways to mitigate
the resistance to it.
A simple starting point is around project communication. Talk about the
beneﬁts the solution will bring to individual stakeholders. is communication
may diﬀer per persona. Imagine you are implementing a solution for RFP
management. A beneﬁt for the Sales team is that the tech will help them spend
less time on RFPs so they can focus more on selling. For the D eal D esk, the
beneﬁt is that project management is all centralized. For speciﬁc contributors
to RFPs, the beneﬁt is that they’re less interrupted in their day-to-day
workﬂow. e beneﬁts need to relate to the speciﬁc team so they see the
tangible impact it’ll have on their day-to-day work.
Complex software projects require a phased approach.
In a phased approach, the whole project is split up into multiple
implementations. For example, a CRM  implementation might be split up into
the sales, service, and marketing phases. Each phase can then have certain
subphases. For the service phase, the sub-phases might be email case
management, then web case and chat, and lastly, reporting. e beneﬁt of a
phased approach is that the end-users can become conﬁdent in one workﬂow
before learning a new one. is helps with the overall adoption.

---

## Page 253

Figure 7.6 Phased approach example for a CRM
As I stated earlier in Chapter 5, more complex projects will require a
Change M anagement team. Larger RevOps teams may have an internal
Change M anagement team, or they may consult external support from
consultants. is team will develop a structured approach to the deployment of
projects. External experts are a great help as they have vast experience from
other projects and help avoid major pitfalls. Change M anagement teams are
highly recommended for enterprises due to their complexity and higher risks.
RevOps transformation is 80% change management and 20% technology. e
hardest part is not implementing a new  CRM  or forecasting tool—i t’s getting
people to adopt it. e key to driving RevOps adoption is positioning it as an
enabler, not a controller. I’ve seen companies fail at RevOps simply because they
didn’t invest in internal buy-in. At Blink Ops, w e built our G T M  processes w ith
a stakeholder-ﬁrst approach, ensuring that every RevOps initiative had clear
value for the teams using it.”
- Leore Spira, D irector of RevOps, Blink Ops
Monitor and Audit

---

## Page 254

Once the product is live, it’s important to understand the performance of the
software.
To do that, you need to gather initial user feedback. is can be used to
make adjustments to the software. is is again a psychological element, as the
end-user feels heard and that their feedback is valuable. All of those soft
touches are what make a good RevOps department a great one.
Feedback gathering should continue after the software is deployed. ere
should always be an open channel where users can ask for help in the form of a
service desk. But there should also be a way for them to provide feedback. For
larger software, like a CRM , it can be helpful to send out an annual survey to
proactively ask for feedback. Finally, asking for feedback during one-to-one
coﬀee chats or keeping an open ear also helps with continuous improvement.
e important part is to create a culture where people are encouraged to
provide feedback.
In RevOps, data is gold. M ost software solutions oﬀer a variety of user
metrics, such as logins, session duration, or, in the case of a CRM , the number
of leads or opportunities created and how thoroughly ﬁelds are completed.
ese metrics reveal both the extent and quality of user adoption.
Poor adoption is when people are not logging in at all. is is a serious
problem as it creates a negative ROI—you’re paying for an unused solution. It’s
important to understand why people are not logging in and what can be done
to correct the issue. It may be that the solution was a mistake, and then the
best course of action is to discontinue it. But you may also ﬁnd out that certain
team members don’t see a beneﬁt for them. en, it’s an opportunity to train
them better.
M ore commonly, it will be that users log in, but the quality is lacking. A
common problem in CRM s is that data becomes outdated quickly (i.e., a close
date that’s in the past). en, it’s important to continuously educate
stakeholders. Ideally, you need inﬂuential stakeholders in the business that
follow best practices. is can be a manager or the best-performing colleague.
ey are automatically showing authority or acting as a role model for the
desired behavior.

---

## Page 255

Pro T ip: T o keep your CRM  up-to-date, you have various options at your
disposal:
•Create a culture that values CRM  hygiene. A top-dow n approach is key.
•Focus on what’s important to capture; you don’t need everything.
•U se tools that automatically log calls, meetings, and other interactions.
•U se AI tools that gather intelligence from meetings and update value
automatically.
•In extreme cases, you can link data hygiene to compensation.
M onitoring and auditing will help ensure you get an ROI for your tech
stack. In the end, unused technology is just useless lines of code.
Socratic Reﬂections
•W hat is your favorite tool? W hy? How does it help drive business
performance or improve the customer experience?
•W hat can you do to improve the adoption of your current systems?
•W hat’s a quick win your business could achieve by launching a new AI
project?
•Are you an AI expert in your company? If not, what is holding you back?
•Have you mapped all your systems?
•W hat gaps do you have in your systems infrastructure? Do you have a
roadmap to ﬁll them?

CHAPTER 8
THE FINAL W EIGHT – DATA
AND INSIGHTS
“In G od we trust; all others must bring data.”
W . Edwards D eming
As your business grows, so does your data. But who makes the best use of the
data?
RevOps is uniquely positioned to dive into it, thanks to its deep
understanding of your G T M  teams. e result? Next-level insights that drive
smarter decisions. at’s why Data & Insightsare a core pillar of RevOps.
In this section, we’ll explore the various components that make up this
pillar. ese include data governance, CRM  database design, reporting, and
forecasting—each critical for optimizing your business operations.
Let’s begin with data governance, which serves as the foundation for
everything that follows.
Building Your Analytical Foundation
D ata governance involves several aspects to ensure that your data is accurate,
secure, compliant, and eﬀectively used. Assuring data governance isn’t just a
function of RevOps; it’s a responsibility that spans your entire business. It’s

---

## Page 257

important to work closely with Legal to meet regulatory mandates in many
jurisdictions, as well as your IT  team to ensure proper data governance.
And it all starts with your legal requirements.
Regulatory Compliance, Data Security and
Privacy
It’s very important to protect sensitive information, especially customer data.
is includes implementing strong cybersecurity measures and ensuring
compliance with relevant laws and regulations governing data usage, such as
the G eneral D ata Protection Regulation (G D PR) in the European U nion or
the California Consumer Privacy Act (CCPA) in the United States.
is requires clear policies and procedures around who can access and
modify data to help maintain data integrity. It also reduces the risk of data
breaches or unauthorized access. To do that, you’ll need to establish clear
guidelines around:
•How long you can store customer data.
•D eletion policy (G D PR).
•Opt-out links in communications.
•W ho the data protection oﬃcer (G D PR) is.
•Permission settings for the various tools you use.
In larger companies, access control is a full-time job due to the complexity
of the business. It’s important to take data seriously. You want to avoid data
breaches, and the ﬁnes are massive.
Data Quality Managem ent and Lifecycle
Managem ent
D ata quality involves ensuring the accuracy, completeness, and reliability of
data. Customer data should be regularly cleaned and validated to maintain

---

## Page 258

high-quality standards. is is extremely important as all of your insights
depend on clean data. Outdated, duplicated, or false data will lead to skewed
results. is leads to unreliable reporting and forecasting, people losing trust in
the data, and, in the worst-case scenario, even fraud if the data misrepresents
reality.
Here’s what bad data can look like:
Have you seen similar data in your CRM ? I have seen this in dozens of our
clients.
is is what good data looks like:
W hat’s the diﬀerence between the two?
•Correct spelling.
•Consistent group names.
•Silicon Valley Bank was acquired by First Citizens Bank.
•Opportunity Name has a consistent naming convention.

---

## Page 259

•Correct industries selected.
•No blank ﬁelds.
•Close dates aren’t pushed to the end of the quarter; rather, they are
realistic.
One important aspect of improving data quality is to manage the lifecycle
of the data: from creation and storage to archiving or deletion. is includes
decisions on how long to retain data, when to archive it, and when to dispose
of it. Depending on which jurisdiction, the duration will be mandated.
For customer data, you also have to account for job changes. People get
promoted, they change jobs, or they retire. K eeping track of this information is
important for accurate data.
Pro T ip: D ata enrichment tools have advanced even further w ith the
development of AI. You can use them to keep your customer data up-to-date.
One eﬀective way to enhance data quality is by implementing a D ata
Quality Score in your CRM .
Data Quality Score to Im prove Data Quality
e goal of these scores is to monitor desired behaviors and maintain
consistent, reliable data. For example, ensuring accurate and trustworthy data
is essential for pipeline hygiene, as it directly aﬀects revenue predictability.

Data Quality and Sales Com m issions
Recently, I’ve worked with a few businesses that linked data quality to sales
commissions. In one case, 80% of the sales commission was tied to sales
performance, while the remaining 20% was based on data quality and
administrative tasks. W e developed dedicated D ata Health Scores and real-time
dashboards to monitor compliance. is allowed for reps to directly inﬂuence
their commissions by staying compliant. is model wouldn’t have worked in
the 2010s, as reps wouldn’t have taken a job with that rule; they’d simply work
at a diﬀerent business.
However, in the 2020s, this model has become more realistic. ere are
fewer sales jobs on the market, and the requirement isn’t a deal breaker
anymore. In my view, it’s a policy that’s long overdue. Tying incentives to
desired behavior makes sense. at’s how psychology works; we encourage the
behavior we want to see more of. Clearly, we want to see more sales.
Data Integration

---

## Page 261

W e talked earlier about the technical aspect of data integration and how
diﬀerent systems and platforms can eﬀectively share and use data. is is
essential for a uniﬁed view of customer information and eﬀective cross-
functional analytics. is is essential in RevOps. It’s where opportunities are
found, and the GT M  teams are aligned.
W ith integration, it’s important that it doesn’t undermine other governance
aspects. Poor integration can undermine data quality, leading to issues like:
•D uplicates.
•D iﬀerent formats (dates or currency).
•Bypassed validations.
•M issing critical ﬁelds.
M any software vendors will make the claim they can integrate into a
certain platform. It’s important you test these claims.
Data Literacy and Ethics
You can’t have good data without a good data culture. You need to create a
culture where your staﬀ understands the importance of data governance and is
trained in best practices. is includes regular training and updates on data
governance policies and procedures. Some of this is mandatory, while other
aspects are critical for your business to function.
M any businesses suﬀer from poor data quality, which makes it unusable or
produces skewed results. is becomes especially problematic for forecasting. A
solid data culture is key to ﬁxing this. And it starts at the top: leadership sets
the tone, which then cascades down to managers and the rest of the team. If
managers aren’t entering accurate data themselves, it’s no surprise when others
don’t either.
Lastly, consider establishing a set of ethical guidelines for data usage,
especially when dealing with customer data. is includes considerations
around consent, transparency, and fairness in data collection and use. Privacy is
a big issue worldwide.

---

## Page 262

Monitoring and Auditing
D ata governance is an ongoing activity you have to regularly monitor. In some
jurisdictions, there are compliance rules that require you to monitor them. If
you’re working with very sensitive data (healthcare or ﬁnancial), you should
also consider external audits.
I can’t stress enough how important it is to have clean data. It’s the
foundation of your analytics, reporting, and forecasting. Regular data
monitoring and auditing help keep it that way.
To support this, you need a database that’s both comprehensive and easy to
manage.
Designing the Hub: W hy Your CRM Database
Matters
Say you have a board meeting in two days, and you’ve received a request to
analyze the impact of marketing campaigns on renewals. How readily can you
provide this insight? If your answer isn’t “instantly,” then you may have a
clogged data ﬂow.
Buzzwords like “360-degree customer view,” “data-driven decision-
making,” “AI integration,” and “Revenue Analytics” are everywhere. W hile
dozens of revenue solutions promise to deliver on these capabilities, they all
face the same potential roadblock: a poorly structured data foundation.
A well-designed CRM  database allows companies to manage customer data
eﬃciently, improve customer interactions, and streamline business processes.
CRM  database design entails creating, implementing, and maintaining a
system to manage customer-related information. e primary objective of a
CRM  database design is to establish a logical model of the CRM  system.
It’s essential for ensuring data accuracy and consistency. It eliminates
redundant data, improves query performance, and enhances the overall CRM
system’s eﬃciency. Not to mention, well-structured data is crucial for AI
projects.
To ensure a good CRM  database design, consider the following principles:

---

## Page 263

Principle M eaning
M inimize data
redundancy
Avoid storing duplicate information, as it wastes space, slows
your system, and leads to data inconsistencies.
Ensure data accuracy
and completeness
A reliable CRM  database must contain accurate and
comprehensive information to support eﬀective analysis and
reporting.
Optimize data
organization
D istribute data into relevant tables (records) based on
customer attributes to reduce redundancy and facilitate easy
data retrieval.
Establish data
relationships
D eﬁne relationships between CRM  tables to represent
connections between customers, contacts, orders, and other
CRM  entities.
Support interactivity e CRM  database design should support seamless
interactions between CRM  operators and the system to align
Sales and M arketing.
e good news is that popular CRM s like Salesforce and Hubspot have
already done much of the heavy lifting in CRM  database design. Both are
relational databases, meaning they consist of tables that are interlinked to each
other through primary and foreign keys.

Best Practices in Data Analysis
e use cases for reporting are versatile. But here are a few that RevOps reports
on:
•Financial Health M onitoring•Performance M anagement•Investor Communication
•Strategic Planning •Risk M anagement •Identify Revenue Opportunities
•Identify Cost Cuttings •Identify Eﬃciencies •D ata-D riven D ecision M aking
Reporting is essential to steer a company. But there are also requirements
for it. Here are a few considerations to keep in mind:
Data Quality

---

## Page 265

W ithout good data quality, you can’t trust your data, which makes it
impossible to base decisions on it. D ata governance and CRM  design set a
great foundation that leads to good data quality.
Population and Sam ple Size
e population and sample of your data set are important to get reliable results
and predictions.
Population refers to the full set of available data. A sample size is a smaller
subset of that population, such as all your opportunities from the third quarter
(Q3). By analyzing this sample, you can make statistical inferences, such as
how Q3 results might reﬂect trends in the overall population.
A more common use case is using Q3 data to forecast results for the
upcoming Q4. However, the size of the sample matters. A minimum number
of data points are needed to ensure the results are statistically reliable. For
example, if Company A closes 30 out of 100 deals, then you have a win rate of
30%. Company B has the same win rate but for 300 out of 1,000 deals.
Assuming that each company loses 10 deals, the win rate of Company A drops
to 20%, while Company B drops to just 29%.
In this example, both companies dropped 10 deals, but the impact on
Company A was much larger. is is why sample size is so important.
Pro T ip: D ata is king, but intuition is often better w hen the sample size is too
small. Reporting at this stage is still advisable, but the results are not decisive. It
would be better to consider your intuition or other data points.
Analytics Skills and Context
W ho looks at the data is also important.
First, you need someone who understands data analytics to process the
data. Secondly, you need someone who understands the business context of
data. RevOps is in a good position for this because they understand the
business context.

---

## Page 266

Com pliance
For ﬁnancial data, you will have to adhere to accounting principles and
standards such as the G enerally Accepted Accounting Principles (G AAP) or
International Financial Reporting Standards (IFRS). For SaaS companies, only
“revenue” is regulated, and all other KP Is are just commonly accepted but not
mandated (more on this in the next chapter). For instance, there is no right or
wrong way to calculate churn as long as you explain your methodology.
Transparency (Methodology)
A transparent methodology is important for revenue KP Is as there is no
mandate on how to report those. A transparent methodology includes:
•W hat ﬁlters are applied.
•W hat range is considered.
•W here the data is coming from.
•W hat data manipulation was applied.
•W hat formula was used to calculate a KPI.
In short, you want to be transparent about all of the choices you make in
preparing and adjusting the data. is allows investors and analysts to
understand the performance of your business.
Levels of Reporting
Everything that can be measured can be reported.
is can be overwhelming and lead to decision fatigue or losing track of
what is important. Just run a G oogle search on KP Is that you “should” track,
and you’ll get a list of a hundred. at’s clearly too much and not beneﬁcial.
Reporting on Business Context
You can group KP Is by their business context. D o this using three levels:
strategic, tactical, and operational.

You may have noticed that this is a siloed approach since we look at each

Holistic Revenue Reporting
e unique trait of RevOps is that data is interconnected. is includes data
from M arketing, Sales, and Customer Success, but it can also include Legal,
Product, and Finance. e last three are especially important for reporting.
Legal holds the commercial agreement and provides the documents outlining
what was agreed upon with the client. Product has usage data that gives real
insights into how clients interact with the company once the product is sold to
them. Finance is tied closely to RevOps, as every initiative we take should have
an impact on ﬁnancial results. (M ore of this in the next chapter.)
As we’ve seen, RevOps focuses on Enablement, as well as Processes and
Systems. But D ata & Insightshelp us understand how these areas interact and
allow us to translate that information into a holistic view of the business.
In contrast, siloed operations have a bias or lack crucial information. Other
businesses might have a dedicated BI team with a holistic view of data, but
they might lack some of the business context.
RevOps has both the context and the ability to understand the data. is
means it can identify opportunities better, ﬁnd improvements, and is able to

---

## Page 269

tell a better story with the data.
Revenue Architecture and the Growth Form ula
Revenue architecture is a strategic and tactical framework that helps businesses
optimize all aspects of its revenue generation. It focuses on aligning M arketing,
Sales, and Customer Success functions with business models, technology
systems, and organizational processes to eﬀectively drive revenue growth. e
goal of revenue architecture is to achieve more sustainable growth. e
framework was pioneered by W inning By D esign.33 To ﬁnd out more about it,
read Revenue Architecture by Jacco van der Kooij.
e framework aligns beautifully with RevOps, as they share the same core
objectives. W hile the level of adoption may vary, it provides a valuable mental
model that supports clear thinking and strategic execution.
M y favorite mental model from Revenue Architecture is the Bow Tie and
corresponding G rowth Formula. It’s the most applicable and illustrates the
holistic nature of G T M  and RevOps. Natan Pollack, VP of RevOps at
TravePerk, puts it like this: “e Bow T ie M odel by W inning by D esign
emphasizes that recurring impact drives recurring revenue by shifting the focus from
one-time transactions to continuous value creation and ongoing customer
engagement. is approach helps organizations build sustainable, long-term
relationships that consistently deliver mutual value. is mindset shift from
traditional one-time transactions tow ard sustainable, long-term relationships is just
as applicable in RevOps operators as it is in sales professionals.”
e G rowth Formula shows that growth can be achieved not only by
increasing the number of customers and sales but also by enhancing the value
derived from each customer and optimizing margins.
e Bow Tie below shows the following:
•Customer Stages: Awareness, Education, etc.
•Internal stages: Prospect, MQL, etc
•e respective Conversion Rate (CR): e.g. from Prospect to M QL is
CR1
