e big advantage of RevOps handling analytics is their ability to instantly
connect insights to the business, thanks to their deep understanding of the
processes and context.
W hen analytics is handled by a dedicated data specialist, this beneﬁt is
often lost. W hile they can identify patterns and craft compelling stories from
the results, unique insights often emerge during the analysis itself and are
hidden in the raw data. I had “aha” moments while looking at data because I
had the right business context. W ithout that context, these opportunities are
easily missed.
Marketing Attribution
W hen talking about marketing attribution, focus on evaluating the marketing
touchpoints a consumer encounters both before and after the purchase. Post-

---

## Page 272

purchase analysis is essential if your business has a recurring revenue model,
but it also provides value for all types of brands.
Have you ever wondered why retailers give you a 10% discount at the
checkout if you sign up for their newsletter? ey want to persuade you to
purchase more of their products.
Because this impacts the whole revenue function, it’s not just led by
M arketing. It’s a perfect example of where RevOps adds value.
However, proper marketing attribution across the entire buyer journey is
diﬃcult. It requires you to invest in the right tools (we’ll touch on tools later)
and set up the right processes. It also requires discipline.
is includes:
•Clean data structure in the CRM .
•Clean data.
•Legal & compliance (G D PR, etc.).
•Capturing in-person interaction (conferences, trade shows).
If your business lacks two of the four above points, it would be wise to set
the foundations ﬁrst before attempting to build out proper marketing
attribution.
W hen you have the correct marketing attribution, what can you do with it?
You’ll be able to identify what campaigns are increasing revenue. Your Sales
team will be able to clearly understand how deals were inﬂuenced by your
marketing eﬀorts. e same goes for your Customer Support team, as they will
also be able to engage, if not upsell, your customers.
e common thread is that you will have a better visibility of your
prospects’ engagement and marketing activities.
Product and U ser Data
Analyzing product usage and user data is full of valuable insights, especially for
SaaS companies. is doesn’t mean that it’s only limited to SaaS. For example,
automotive manufacturers track how you drive and use that data, along with

---

## Page 273

sensor inputs, to predict when your vehicle might need servicing. It’s the
connectivity within products that makes this level of insight possible.
is is even possible when the product doesn’t have connectivity. In
healthcare, there are many prescription drugs that now work alongside an app.
e app allows you to track your usage and report symptoms. It can also
provide additional information for preventive care.
ere are apps that help you track the depreciation of your machinery,
equipment, and goods. Some use modern solutions like sensors installed on
machines, while others scan your equipment and provide recommendations on
whether it’s time for a replacement.
In these situations, the vendor beneﬁts from being closer to the customer,
making it easier to distribute their products, while the customer enjoys an
enhanced user experience. In the case of heavy machinery or medication, it can
even save lives.
Product data has incredible value and can be used to increase revenue for
your company.
But to do that, it needs to be seen by the right people.
I often see that a lot of companies have useful data, but it’s not exposed to
the right set of eyes. You see this in sales-led SaaS businesses that focus heavily
on revenue data but make little use of product or user-generated data. It sits in
the product database, and the Revenue team doesn’t have access to it, so it
becomes a missed opportunity.
W hen you expose this data to the Revenue team and especially the RevOps
team, they’re able to view it through a commercial lens, uncovering insights
that might otherwise go unnoticed. Here are some valuable “aha moments”
that can emerge, some of which I mentioned above:
•Clients that use feature Y are likely to churn.
•Clients that use feature X are likely to buy more.
•Free users with [this behavior] pattern are more likely to upgrade.
•e client is not using the product; what can we do to activate them?

---

## Page 274

•ey did not experience feature Z, so we should start an educational
campaign to show them its beneﬁts.
•Feature Z is less used, so let’s adjust our onboarding to further highlight
it.
e sources of inspiration are diverse, but they all share a common goal:
improving the customer experience to boost revenue. at’s why RevOps is in
the best position to assess this type of data.
Even if you’re not in the SaaS space, having wider exposure to data across
teams can lead to valuable “aha moments.” is is also why external
consultants often spot these insights more easily than internal teams.
W hen You Should Do Reporting
ere are countless KP Is and data points you can report on, but not all of
them carry the same weight, and you won’t need to track each one all the time.
For instance, strategic KP Is don’t need daily reporting. D o you think you’ll
get meaningful insight by looking at the number of bookings every day? ese
types of metrics are more valuable when viewed in context, such as compared
to the previous month, quarter, or year. In many cases, reviewing KP Is on a bi-
weekly or monthly basis is enough.
On the other hand, when you look at operational KP Is, it might be helpful
to review them daily. For example, how many calls did a rep complete? It’s
helpful to see that number every day. W e talked earlier about gamiﬁcation. For
that to work, you need live data on your dials.
You can take the KP I categories we discussed earlier and pair them with
appropriate reporting frequencies to create a more structured and useful
reporting approach.

---

## Page 275

Important note: Even if the frequency is bi-weekly or monthly, this doesn’t
mean that you completely lose sight of the KP Is. is approach works well
when things are running smoothly. But should there be a warning signal, for
example, a higher amount of churn than usual, then you don’t want to wait a
month to know. at’s why it’s crucial to have monitoring systems in place to
catch these signals early. ese systems can include qualitative indicators (such
as a team member ﬂagging an issue) or quantitative ones (like automated
triggers that detect irregular behavior).
You might have noticed that not all reporting needs fall neatly into KP I
categories. ere are activities that don’t require frequent reporting, while
others do. For instance, evaluating your growth formula can be a monthly
exercise, much like linking product and revenue data.
Your Reporting Technology Stack
Reporting can be facilitated by technology. ere is a whole category of
dedicated reporting tools available, and some of them are built-in with your
existing tools. e price points of those range from being free to easily
spending hundreds of thousands on it.

---

## Page 276

Spreadsheets: ere is a saying that almost every software company started
as a spreadsheet. And most businesses still compete against a good spreadsheet.
D ue to their versatility, spreadsheets are popular for any type of reporting and
analytics. All you have to do is input your data, combine it with formulas, and
within seconds, you learn what you need. e downsides are that you can
easily make errors, manipulate your data, and reach limits depending on the
size of the data set.
Recommended use case: monthly reporting in early-stage companies,
infrequent reporting, or unique reporting cases.
Platform Built-In Reporting: M any platforms, like your CRM , have
built-in reporting. e beauty of this is that you can do it without any speciﬁc
reporting skills, which empowers most of your employees to use them. ey
use live data, and chances are low that data is wrongly reported, as constraints
are built in.
Recommended use case: Operational K PIs like dials or dow nloads and tactical
K PIs like conversion rates. (T actical K PIs can reach a limit, and you might require
Excel or a BI setup that we’ll look at next.)
BI setup: Eventually, your analytical use cases will advance. You’ll want to
connect and combine data from diﬀerent sources and types to gain deeper
insights. At this point, you will require a data warehouse, ET L, reverse ET L,
data building tool, and a data analytics tool like Tableau, Looker, or Power BI.
Eventually, you will add Py-Analytics (Py-PI), an analytics package in Python,
the programming language that gives you a high degree of freedom and allows
you to assess big data sets.
Recommended use cases include strategic and tactical K PIs, special projects
(product data, ﬁnance data, etc.), and operational K PIs.
All of these tools might sound like a foreign language to you, and that’s
okay! is kind of setup typically includes a dedicated analytics team and oﬀers
a lot of versatility. Beyond just reporting, it can support a wide range of use
cases across the business.

---

## Page 277

How AI W ill Change The Gam e For Reporting
W ith an AI like ChatG PT, all you have to do is ask it questions to get an
answer. e same interaction can happen with your internal data. Ask your
internal RevenueG PT:
•How many M QLs did we create last month?
•How many of those M QLs are from Germany?
•W hat is the conversion rate of the German M QLs vs the ones from
France?
AI platforms like this make analytics accessible to more people. e more
people who have access to analytics, the more data-driven decisions you’re able
to make.
To make this work, it’s important that the underlying data is reliable. at’s
why I’ve stressed the importance of data hygiene. W hen it comes to reporting,
most of the time is spent ensuring the accuracy of the data.
To help you along, here are some practical best practices:
•Link KPIs to your Strategic OKRs: For example, a KPI can be NRR.
at supports the OKR  in increasing customer retention.
•Focus on 3 to 5 strategic KPIs: You must be focused with strategic
KP Is. ey are guiding metrics. You can not have too many, or else your
focus will be spread thin. I recommend 3 to 5. For smaller companies,
three should suﬃce.
•Review your OKRs and KPIs regularly: Reviewing OKR s and KPIs is
useful to do on occasion to make sure they are still relevant. Smaller
companies will have to do this more frequently as they go through so
many changes. But there might be big events that require any business to
perform a review, such as new regulations, geopolitical events, or other
black swan events.

---

## Page 278

•e Right Set of Eyes: W ho looks at the data will have an impact on
how the data is interpreted. RevOps is in a good position to analyze the
data as they understand the GT M  motion and have the analytical skills.
Once your data is clean and accurate, you’re ready to forecast.
The Crucial Role of Forecasting
e most important activity for a business is its ability to sell. Selling is the
raison d’etre for a business.
e second most important activity is forecasting, which is the ability to
predict future sales. Forecasting is what distinguishes the bad, the good, and
the great companies from each other. G reat companies have accurate sales
forecasting, which is a competitive advantage.
e ability to forecast accurately unlocks many beneﬁts. Capacity planning
and planning, in general, is more reliable as revenue streams are predictable.
Investors have more conﬁdence in a business that has predictable revenue
streams. is makes it easier to secure future investments.
Better forecasting will also create a better culture and workplace. e
inability to reach sales forecasts is a main driver for restructurings, which is
always devastating for employees who are ﬁred and reduces the morale of
employees who stay. Accurate forecasting is a powerful tool for driving business
success.
But what should you forecast?
Every business does a sales forecast, and some businesses do a revenue
forecast. Some also call the revenue forecast a GT M  forecast.
Let’s look at the diﬀerence.
Sales Forecasting
Sales forecasting is the process of estimating future sales by predicting how
much a product or service will sell in the next week, month, quarter, or year.
is is an educated estimate based on past sales data, current market trends,

---

## Page 279

and a touch of industry intuition. Sales forecasting primarily focuses on new
business within the Sales department.
In practice, the forecast can vary depending on the industry you are in. A
forecast for a manufacturing company is very diﬀerent from a forecast for an
insurance company or a SaaS company. In SaaS especially, you have the special
distinction between new business, expansion, and renewal. at’s where a
revenue forecast comes in.
Revenue Forecasting
Revenue forecasting is about predicting the company’s revenue generation over
a speciﬁc period of time. is includes revenue generated from sales and
recurring revenue such as expansions and renewals. In that regard, revenue
forecasting always includes sales forecasting but adds the recurring part. is is
especially important for SaaS companies, as the majority of the revenue comes
from the recurring business.
Figure 8.3 Sales Forecasting, a subset of Revenue Forecasting
Revenue forecasting unites every element of RevOps. It requires
integration, alignment, and collaboration from all G T M  teams. For example, a
robust revenue forecast considers how marketing campaigns, conversion rates,

---

## Page 280

onboarding success, and churn impact the revenue for the quarters ahead. e
model is very robust, as it considers data points from all G T M  teams. at’s
why it’s worth investing in a strong RevOps team that can handle everything
needed to produce a forecast that’s accurate.
Forecast Methods
ere are various methods to approach forecasting, whether it’s for sales or
revenue.
Top-Down Forecast
Top-down forecasting is a strategic method for estimating business outcomes
by starting with the broader market and narrowing it down to speciﬁc
segments. In Chapter 3, we looked at capacity planning, which can be a top-
down forecast.
An example of a top-down forecast is when calculating the Total
Addressable M arket (TAM ). To calculate the TAM , you look at macro factors
such as technology or regulations, competitors, historical results, brand, or
product stickiness. is all helps give an indication of a top-down forecast.
e main disadvantage of top-down forecasting is that it’s too granular and
relies too much on assumptions. Humans are terrible at predicting the future.
ere are so many variables that impact it, and most of them are unknown.
Clearly, it’s a starting point, and we have to start somewhere, so don’t disregard
the top-down forecast.
Bottom -U p Forecasting
In bottom-up forecasting, you look at detailed sales data to create an overall
forecast. is includes deals closed, conversion rates, lead sources, deals by sales
rep, or your current pipeline.
You start at an individual level and aggregate it from the bottom to the top.
But don’t stop there. You should also consider new hires and layoﬀs, legislative
changes, territory shifts, product changes, marketing campaigns, and literally

---

## Page 281

anything that can impact the individual’s sales performance. All of this creates
a more statistically robust model of your future forecast.
Bottom-up forecasting can also involve individual sales reps or managers
creating their own forecasts by making judgment calls on the likelihood of
deals closing. W hile this approach provides insights directly from those closest
to the deals, it introduces potential problems. e main one is that it puts a lot
of responsibility on the forecasters. Poor processes, governance, and pipeline
hygiene will result in unreliable and even totally wrong forecasts. It has a lot of
dependency on the team. Having said that, in a disciplined work environment,
it can be quite accurate.
Weighted Forecast
A weighted forecast is a simple method to evaluate the probability that a
sale will close. In its simplest form, each sales stage is connected to a percentage
that determines the likelihood of closing.
Stage Probability Example for a $50,000
deal
Closed-Lost 0% $0
Qualiﬁcation 10% $5,000
Inﬂuence 20% $10,000
Proposal 40% $20,000
Commercial Negotiation 60% $30,000
Legal Review 80% $40,000
Closed-W on 100% $50,000
W hen you have a contract value of $50,000 that’s in the proposal stage,
then you would count $20,000 of it towards the weighted forecast.
e question becomes: how accurate is a weighted forecast?
ere are a few options for improving the accuracy of the forecast—for
example, analyzing historical data to determine the probability of each stage.
Based on past data, you can reach the conclusion that 20% of deals that reach
the Inﬂuence stage eventually close. is can improve the forecast accuracy.

---

## Page 282

(I’ll discuss how to measure forecast accuracy later on.) If you’re a company
just starting out, then you can take industry averages, if available. It’s best to
ask ChatG PT  for these averages. In the table above, the probability is based on
averages for B2B SaaS companies selling products in the range between
$30,000 to $70,000.
One issue with weighted forecasts is their reliance on deal stages. For the
forecast to be accurate, sales reps must correctly identify the deal stage, which
can easily go wrong if the entry and exit criteria for each stage aren’t clearly
deﬁned. If a deal is placed in the wrong stage, the associated probability is also
incorrect, as these probabilities are often hardcoded into the system.
In my experience, many companies set these probabilities when
implementing their CRM  and never revisit or update them, even as new data
becomes available. Over time, this leads to outdated assumptions, and the
same businesses are left wondering why their forecasts consistently miss the
mark.
e world is always changing. So should your forecast.
AI Forecast
AI forecasting is a new but very promising ﬁeld. In an AI forecast, you can use
available data to make a prediction. is includes the current deal velocity,
historical information about similar deals, current data like engagement
history, market environment, and any other internal and external factors you
can think of. e main beneﬁt is that the AI can run these calculations in the
background. e result is that it can determine a forecast amount and close
date based on all of these data points.
In practice, I see this playing out as a “human” forecast alongside an AI-
based forecast. Comparing both forecasts gives some fascinating insights. e
beauty of AI forecasting is that the AI will continuously learn, and its forecast
accuracy will become better over time.
W hich model you choose will depend on your business. Sometimes, you
don’t have a choice. An early-stage company can only rely on top-down
forecasting, as there is no historical data. M ature companies will mainly rely on
bottom-up forecasting because they have more data available. M y guess is that

---

## Page 283

AI forecasts will soon dominate, as forecasting is one of the most important
processes for a business. e ability to predict cash ﬂow determines
investment, hiring plans, and stock prices.
G etting forecasting right is important.
For that, we need good data.
Data Requirem ents for Forecasting
Sales forecasting is all about data and consumer behavior. By deﬁnition,
human behavior is unpredictable, or else we would always reach our targets.
But we can increase our odds with data. W ith forecasting, the more data you
have, the higher the likelihood your forecast will be accurate. at makes data
availability the key input for your forecasting.
It’s not just the availability of data but also the quality of your data (which
I stressed earlier). Data quality determines the success or failure of a business.
Measuring Forecast Accuracy
e best way to measure forecast accuracy is to calculate the M ean Absolute
Percentage Error (M APE). is is a statistical measure expressed as a
percentage. e beneﬁt of this method is that it allows easier comparison of
various data sets, like diﬀerent currencies.
It shows the percentage by which your predictions diﬀered from the actual
outcomes. A lower M APE value indicates more accurate forecasts.
U se the following formula to calculate the M APE:
Here’s how to break it down:

---

## Page 284

1.Look at each forecast and the actual sales per month. For each month,
calculate the error by subtracting the forecast from the actual value. is
is how much the forecast missed the actual value.
2.Second, you want to convert it to a percentage. Find out the percentage
error by dividing the error (the diﬀerence we just calculated) by the
actual value. Take the absolute value of this percentage (ignore whether
it’s positive or negative).
3.Finally, average it out. at means adding all the percentage errors
together and dividing them by the number of forecasts you received.
For example:
M onth Forecast Actual Sales
January $120 $100
February $85 $100
M arch $90 $100
Calculate the following to ﬁnd the M APE:
M onth Error Percentage Error
January $120 - $100 = $20 ($20/$100)×100%= 20%
February $85 - $100 = -$15 ($15/$100)×100%= 15%
M arch $90 - $100 = -$10 ($10/$100)×100%= 10%
M APE (20%+15%+10%)/3 = 15%
A number closer to 0 is better, as it indicates no error, meaning the forecast
matched the actual sales exactly (though this is rare in practice). e real value
comes from comparing this number to benchmarks, such as results from a
diﬀerent time period, product line, or business unit.
e table below can also be used as a benchmark for a statistical
interpretation of the results.34 M y recommendation is to aim for a M APE of
under 20%.

---

## Page 285

M APE Interpretation
<10% Highly accurate forecasting
10-20% G ood forecasting
20-50% Reasonable forecasting
>50% Inaccurate forecasting
How Often You Should Forecast
In terms of forecast frequency, it depends on your sales cycle. e table below
shows an indication of recommended forecast frequencies. However, they’re
just an indication because every business is diﬀerent.
Sales cycle Forecast frequency
7 to 30 days (B2C or SM B) D aily to W eekly
30 to 90 days (M id-market) W eekly to M onthly
90 to days (Enterprise) M onthly to Quarterly
The Psychology of Forecasting
e most beautiful aspect of forecasting is the psychology of it. It can work in
the favor of forecasting, but also against it.
In forecasting, it’s important to be aware of cognitive bias, a systematic
pattern of deviation rationality in judgment. ere are four main ones.
Cognitive Bias D escription
Conﬁrmation Bias e tendency to favor information that conﬁrms pre-existing
beliefs or hypotheses.
Overconﬁdence Bias Forecasters might be overly conﬁdent in their predictions,
underestimating the uncertainty or the range of possible
outcomes.
Anchoring Bias e reliance on an initial piece of information (the “anchor”)
to make subsequent judgments, which can lead to the under-
adjustment of forecasts.

---

## Page 286

Availability Heuristic M aking decisions based on information that is more readily
available or memorable rather than all relevant data.
All of them are very common, and you might be more receptive to one bias
over another based on your personality. I am more receptive to conﬁrmation
bias and availability heuristics. e good news is the more aware you are about
cognitive bias, the more likely you won’t fall for them.
A way to avoid cognitive biases is to have training around them to create
awareness. W orking with diverse teams also helps reduce the risk of falling for
one bias because it’s spread across multiple individuals. It also helps to work
with various scenarios and models so you’re not reliant on only one forecast.
Pro T ip: Complement a human-based forecast w ith an AI forecast to further
avoid cognitive biases.

CHAPTER 9
THE COU NTERW EIGHT –
REVOPS AND FINANCE
“You have to understand accounting and you have to understand the nuances of
accounting. It’s the language of business.”
W arren Buﬀett
e ﬁrst line of your income statement is revenue.
is is a book about RevOps. Even without an in-depth knowledge of
ﬁnance, you can see the two are connected. But what does revenue actually
mean?
In its simplest terms, revenue is what a company earns by selling goods and
services. In RevOps, we have revenue in our name: “Revenue Operations.”
W e are the operators of revenue.
e purpose of RevOps is to drive business impact, primarily on revenue.
Take the growth formula from the previous chapter. An improvement in the
ICP deﬁnition leads to more targeted marketing campaigns, which, in turn,
generates more quality inbound leads. At this point, you can already see the
impact on revenue. More leads ultimately bring in more revenue.
A new Service Level Agreement (SLA) between SD Rs and Account
Executives can improve lead qualiﬁcation by 8%. is improvement cascades
down and further increases revenue.

---

## Page 290

Consider a new customer health dashboard that combines product usage
data and email engagement to ﬂag at-risk clients 26% more accurately,
resulting in a 17% reduction in churn. at’s another strong revenue win
driven by RevOps programs. W ell done, team!
RevOps has substantial leverage over ﬁnancial KP Is like revenue, operating
expenses, and EBIT D A. Almost any activity we engage in has a real business
impact. If you’re working on something that lacks business impact, it’s better to
stop. Like if you’re:
•Over-focusing on perfecting a technical process that already functions
well.
•Prioritizing interesting, low-value projects (the “shiny new tool”) over
essential, high-value projects (such as data foundation).
•Spending more time on formatting than on creating deliverables
(presentations, reports).
In RevOps, it’s crucial to focus on key programs that drive ﬁnancial
impact. Stakeholders such as the CFO, CRO, CEO, board members, and
investors are interested in genuine business results.
You’ll notice a more serious tone in this chapter, reﬂecting the strategic
weight that RevOps holds as a business partner. W e manage revenue, and if we
fail, the worst-case scenario is business bankruptcy.
It doesn’t have to reach that extreme, though, to demand seriousness in
RevOps.
RevOps holds a lot of power.
In Chapter 6, we discussed capacity planning and the need for more people
to serve more customers. W ith the rise of AI technologies that improve
eﬃciency, capacity planning may reveal a need to downsize. You might achieve
your desired capacity with new AI tools by empowering your top reps.
Or perhaps you uncover that a signiﬁcant portion of the marketing budget
was wasted on ineﬀective projects during our holistic reporting. D epending on
the budget size, such ﬁndings could lead to structural changes.

---

## Page 291

Finance values these insights. It’s a numbers-driven function. W hen
ﬁnancial KP Is are in the green, investments are made, money ﬂows freely, and
there’s harmony within the company. But once some ﬁnancial KP Is turn red,
investments shrink, budgets get cut, and an air of uncertainty sets in. e
worst scenario is when KP Is shift from green to red unexpectedly. is often
leads to drastic cost-cutting measures and frequently results in layoﬀs.
e reason Finance values RevOps is predictability. Our work in RevOps
involves creating alignment, collaboration, and integration across all revenue
functions. is, in return, produces more predictable revenue streams. In a
well-structured RevOps setup, you can anticipate that a signiﬁcant drop in
website visitors in one quarter might lead to lower new business revenue nine
months down the line while impacting revenue from existing customers 18
months later. is level of predictability is rare for many companies, but it’s
exactly what RevOps can deliver to Finance and the rest of the business.
Finance can leverage this predictability to make smarter decisions about
budgeting, investments, and timing for fundraising. is chapter begins with
an overview of Finance’s role, highlights its areas of overlap with RevOps, and
explores how diﬀerent types of investors engage with these insights.
U nderstanding W hat Finance Really Does
e list below shows typical Finance functions. I’ve highlighted in bold where
RevOps has strong areas of collaboration.
•Accounting (accounts payable, accounts receivable, G AAP/IFRS,
general ledger)
•Corporate Finance (budgets, capital structure, debt/equity)
•Financial Planning & Analysis (FP&A) (budgets, planning,
forecasting)
•Treasury (cash management, FX, risk management)
•Tax (planning, compliance, tax authority management)
•Investor Relations (reports & disclosures, investor management)
•Payroll

RevOps’ Impact on Financial Accounts
Alexander, a business owner and good friend of mine, gave me the following
business advice: if you want to succeed in G ermany, you need a good lawyer
and a good accountant. I imagine this saying holds true in any country with a
strong rule of law that enforces regulations and standards. At the same time,
any good accountant will tell you that accounting is more art than science.
Let me explain.
In previous chapters, I mentioned G AAP and IFRS as the primary
standards guiding how companies should manage their ledger (which
represents the record-keeping system for a company’s ﬁnancial data) in a
standardized way. is allows for easier comparison of ﬁnancial reports, which
is essential; without these standards, comparing the ﬁnancial performance of
diﬀerent companies and making informed investment decisions would be
challenging. G AAP principles are crucial, as they dictate how we report
business KPIs.
e interesting aspect for SaaS companies is that metrics like Annual
Recurring Revenue (ARR), Lifetime Customer Value (LT V), Total Contract
Value (TCV), and Customer Acquisition Costs (CAC) aren’t covered by G AAP.
As long as you disclose your KP Is transparently, you can report them. But they
aren’t mandatory. M any SaaS companies choose to report these metrics because
they can portray their business in a favorable light.
is is what I mean when I say accounting is more art than science.
Company K PI Calculation

However, many companies adhere to similar deﬁnitions, as is also expected
of their investors—especially since many share the same investors.
Revenue, though, is diﬀerent. Revenue is standardized by G AAP and IFRS,
with speciﬁc rules on how companies must report revenue based on their
industry and line of business. In the simplest terms, revenue is recognized upon
the delivery of a product or service. For SaaS companies, this means that
recurring revenue is recognized.
Now is a good time to understand the diﬀerence between recurring and
non-recurring revenue.

---

## Page 294

Recurring vs. Non-Recurring Revenue
To better understand recurring and non-recurring revenue, let’s look at
examples of each. Non-recurring revenue includes purchases like grocery sales
in a supermarket (an apple), purchasing a car (a Volvo), or buying a newspaper
(the New York Times) at a shop. Recurring revenue, on the other hand, can
come from software licenses that grant access to a service as long as you pay,
such as an insurance policy or the rent a landlord receives.
I can make things even more interesting by oﬀering goods and services “as-
a-Service,” turning them into recurring revenue. is could mean a monthly
food delivery (like Huel), leasing a car (like a Volvo), or subscribing to a
newspaper (the New York Times). Notice that the same item can be non-
recurring or recurring revenue depending on the method of purchase. Cool,
right?
Figure 9.1 Recurring vs. Non-Recurring Subscription Revenue SaaS example
Let’s consider a SaaS company to see how its products and services might
be categorized as either recurring or non-recurring. Anything a company sells,
whether recurring or non-recurring, is what we call a booking. Bookings can be
either type: non-recurring might be a one-time professional service, like a fee
for software implementation. In the recurring category, we have our SaaS
products: the main product and any add-ons. Services can also fall under
recurring revenue if they occur on an ongoing basis, such as access to on-

---

## Page 295

demand online training courses or quarterly strategy calls over a three-year
license period.
ere’s also room for creativity. Instead of bundling strategy calls in a
retainer, you could sell them as one-time training sessions. Here’s where
accounting becomes an art.
Revenue Recognition: The Straight Line
Method
It’s important to understand that bookings are not the same as revenue.
Remember, revenue is recognized upon delivery. If you were only selling
apples, your bookings and revenue would be the same. But for SaaS
companies, it’s diﬀerent. In SaaS, we often sell licenses over a speciﬁed period.
M ost commonly, it’s a 12-month term.
Regulations mandate that revenue must be recognized as the service is
delivered. Let’s assume we sold $120,000 on January 1. ese are the bookings.
To recognize the revenue, Finance typically uses the straight-line method,
spreading the revenue recognition over the contract period; it recognizes
revenue in equal increments across the term. e most common and
straightforward approach is by even periods. You divide the total booking
amount by 12 and recognize an equal amount each month over the 12-month
period. is means that each month, $10,000 is recognized as revenue.
Technically, you can only recognize the revenue for completed months. For
example, in February, $10,000 would be recognized, while the remaining
$110,000 would be deferred revenue. In other words, deferred revenue is a
liability representing advance payments for services that will be delivered in the
future. e illustration below shows this example.

---

## Page 296

Figure 9.2 Straight Line Revenue Recognition for a two-year deal
Revenue recognition is essential in RevOps because it ensures accurate
reporting of earned income, aligns ﬁnancial data with customer contracts, and
provides a clear view of actual business performance.
Decoding Bookings, Billings &  Revenue
One element we must add at this point is billings.
Bookings represent the contractual value a customer agrees to pay, while
revenue is what you can recognize on the ﬁnancial statement using the straight-
line method. In between, you must invoice clients. ese invoices are the
billings.
Let’s take a two-year contract signed on July 1, 2024. e bookings for this
deal are $240,000, which corresponds to the Total Contract Value (TCV). e
Annual Contract Value (ACV) for this deal is $120,000. e billings, however,
will follow an invoicing schedule, determining how many invoices are issued to
collect payment. For this example, let’s assume a quarterly invoicing schedule.
is means that to cover the total amount of $240,000, the customer will
make eight payments of $30,000 each. erefore, the billing is $30,000 per
quarter.

---

## Page 297

Figure 9.3 Straight Line Revenue Recognition for a two-year deal with quarterly billing
Bookings, billings, and revenue each have distinct impacts on RevOps and
Finance. For RevOps, bookings are an indication of sales performance and can
reveal detectable trends, such as increased sales following certain events. For
Finance, bookings represent potential future revenue, assisting with accurate
budgeting and resource planning, such as determining how many people can
be hired over the next six months.
In RevOps, billings can help predict churn; clients who are consistently late
on payments or avoid paying altogether are more likely to churn. For Finance,
billings are essential for managing short-term cash ﬂow, answering the question
of how much cash is available in the bank.
Revenue enables RevOps to gauge actual business growth, which can also
be used to benchmark against competitors. For Finance, revenue informs the
Proﬁt & Loss (P&L) statement and provides critical information for investors.

---

## Page 298

TCV, ACV, ARR, MRR, and Revenue – W hat to
U se and W hen?
So far, we’ve discussed several KP Is that indicate how much money a business
makes, each with a slightly diﬀerent focus: TCV, ACV, ARR, M RR, and
Revenue. Let’s look at when to use each.
Total Contract Value (TCV) bookings are best used for annual or multi-
year deals. For example, the amount ﬁeld on an opportunity in your CRM
would display this number. TCV includes both recurring and non-recurring
revenue, making it easy to understand the impact of M arketing, Sales, and
Customer Success on future revenue. It provides a good indication of how
much money the business is projected to make. However, TCV bookings
exclude any licenses from previous years, focusing only on current closed deals.
Annual Contract Value (ACV) is identical to TCV when the contract
duration is exactly one year, and it also includes recurring and non-recurring
revenue. ACV is relevant because licenses are typically priced annually, and
sales conversations usually occur in annual terms. ACV is also frequently used
as the basis for sales compensation. Like TCV, ACV excludes revenue from
multi-year deals closed in previous years.

---

## Page 299

Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR) includes revenue from all recurring
sources, such as subscription revenue from software licenses or service retainers
for a speciﬁc period. ARR also accounts for revenue from multi-year deals,
upsells, as well as downgrades or cancellations. In simple terms, this KP I
encompasses all recurring revenue sources, making it an excellent forward-
looking indicator of revenue from these sources. One limitation of ARR is
potential inconsistency; for instance, churn or renewal may occur on the same
day, such as December 31.
M onthly Recurring Revenue (M RR) is similar to ARR but focuses on
monthly intervals. M RR is especially useful when services are oﬀered on a
monthly basis. As a general rule, companies with larger B2B accounts tend to
rely more on ARR, while B2C companies often use M RR.
Finally, there’s revenue, which is only recognized upon the delivery of
goods or services sold. Revenue includes both recurring and non-recurring
sources and is the only KP I that follows G AAP standards, making it a
requirement in audited ﬁnancial statements. D ue to this standardization, it’s
ideal for year-over-year and cross-company comparisons. However, revenue is
not eﬀective for predicting future performance, as it only reﬂects historical
data.
GTM and RevOps in Financial Statem ents

---

## Page 300

e overlap of bookings, billings, and revenue between RevOps and Finance
underscores the critical integration between these functions. It also highlights
how the underlying data from teams like M arketing, Sales, and Customer
Success impacts these metrics. For example, fewer website visitors lead to fewer
deals and fewer won opportunities, ultimately resulting in less revenue. If Sales
has a suboptimal handover process to Customer Success, it can increase
onboarding churn. And if Customer Success is not equipped to serve
customers eﬀectively, this can lead to churn, which also means less revenue.
ese factors have a direct impact on:
•New business bookings
•Expansion revenue
•Renewal revenue
Each of these can be either recurring or non-recurring.
It’s not just G T M  and RevOps activities that impact revenue. ey also
inﬂuence another major category on the income statement: Operating
Expenses (OpEx). OpEx represents the costs a business incurs to keep
operations running smoothly. Typically, this includes salaries and wages
(excluding direct labor costs for production), rent and utilities, oﬃce supplies,
marketing and advertising, depreciation of equipment, and technology
expenses.
M ost of these OpEx expenditures are directly inﬂuenced by RevOps and
G T M  teams. Here’s how our work in RevOps can impact various OpEx
subcategories:

roughout this book, I’ve advocated for RevOps as the enabler of AI. is
will impact the bottom line of your business, too. Every CFO will tell you that
the largest expenditure is headcount, especially in software companies.
Reducing headcount aﬀects not only people in G T M  functions but also those
in support functions like RevOps, HR, and Business Operations.
e reality is that the companies of the future will be leaner.

Net D ollar Retention (ND R) is crucial for both RevOps and Finance, as it
reﬂects customer satisfaction and revenue growth from existing clients, making
it a primary driver of predictable revenue growth. For Finance, a high ND R
signals strong recurring revenue potential, while RevOps views it as an
indicator of successful customer engagement strategies that drive expansion
revenue and reduce churn.
Net M agic Number measures sales eﬃciency, showing how eﬀectively the
company converts sales and marketing spend into recurring revenue. Finance
values this metric to assess ROI on growth investments, while RevOps uses it
to ﬁne-tune and optimize sales processes to ensure each dollar spent accelerates
growth eﬀectively.
Burn M ultiple reﬂects the company’s spending eﬃciency relative to
revenue generation. Finance prioritizes this metric for cash ﬂow management,
while RevOps uses it to assess whether operational investments are yielding
suﬃcient returns to justify further growth expenditure. Note that burn is
irrelevant if there is no investor funding.
CAC highlights the eﬃciency of acquiring new customers. Finance closely
monitors CAC to ensure spending aligns with revenue growth targets, while
RevOps leverages CAC insights to reﬁne targeting and outreach strategies,
improving cost eﬃciency in customer acquisition.

---

## Page 303

CLT V combines ﬁnancial and operational perspectives to assess the
proﬁtability of customer relationships. It encapsulates the entire revenue funnel
from website visits to leads, opportunities, won deals, renewals, and,
ultimately, whether a client stays or leaves. Every RevOps initiative impacts this
journey, and all of it is reﬂected in CLT V, making it the ultimate RevOps KPI.
All this reporting ultimately serves to build trust with investors by
demonstrating how you’ll drive sustainable growth. Clear metrics like CLT V
showcase the long-term value you’re creating. is gives investors conﬁdence in
your strategy.
“A common mistake companies make is to think that they need to track
everything, especially ﬁrst-time founders and new er businesses. You don’t. W hat
you need to do, though, is select a set number of KPIs and not keep changing
them all the time when they don’t tell you the narrative you w ant to see. T ry
selecting three main KPIs to track from each of the main areas of the bow  tie,
from M arketing to CS, give them leading and lagging indicators, and keep the
pace for at least six months. is will give you consistency to even know  if those
are the correct ones to track or not, and then you can iterate.”
- Nicholas Gollop, Senior RevOps Advisor at RevOps On-D emand
Investor Relations and RevOps
Investor Relations (IR) acts as a bridge between a company and its investors.
Both public and private companies engage in investor relations activities. Let’s
look at two examples of how mismanaged investor relations can go wrong.
IR in Public Companies Gone Wrong
e “G olden Age of SaaS,” from 2008 to 2022, marked an exciting era when
growth seemed limitless.
Fueled by investor enthusiasm, SaaS companies could pursue “growth at all
costs,” spending aggressively on expansion with minimal scrutiny of their paths

---

## Page 304

to proﬁtability. Companies focused on capturing market share, scaling quickly,
and building loyal customer bases, with investors conﬁdent that proﬁts would
eventually follow. However, as market conditions shifted after COVID -19,
tolerance for high cash burn dwindled. Rising interest rates, economic
uncertainty, and shifting investor priorities ushered in a new era that required
ﬁnancial discipline alongside growth.
For Asana, a project management software company, this shift came at a
pivotal moment. In 2022, Asana found itself at the center of this changing
tide. Known for its intuitive platform and rapid adoption, Asana had been an
investor favorite since its IPO in 2020. e company was investing heavily in
marketing, product innovation, and global reach to capture as much market
share as possible. Yet, as investors became more cautious, the strategy of
aggressive spending to drive growth began to face criticism.35
Although Asana’s revenue kept growing each quarter, the company wasn’t
turning a proﬁt. It was burning through cash without showing signs of moving
toward a sustainable model. Even with strong adoption and revenue growth,
investors wanted a clear plan to reduce losses, something Asana was slow to
provide. W hen the company signaled continued high spending, investor
conﬁdence dropped, leading to a sharp decline in its stock. Eventually,
operational corrections, including layoﬀs, followed.
Figure 9.5 D rop in Asana stock price after failing to meet growth trajectory

---

## Page 305

Asana’s story highlights the challenge of balancing growth with the need for
transparency and ﬁnancial discipline. Investors still value growth, but now they
expect a clearer path to proﬁtability. For Asana, ﬁnding this balance is essential
to regaining investor conﬁdence in an era where “growth at all costs” is no
longer suﬃcient.
is shift in expectations is relevant not only for public companies but also
for private ones.
IR in Private Companies Gone Wrong
W eW ork once promised to reshape the world of workspaces.
It captivated investors with its sleek, ﬂexible oﬃces and ambitious
expansion plans. Backed by major players like SoftBank, the company pursued
growth at all costs, with locations sprouting up in cities worldwide. Investors
were enthralled by the vision, driving W eW ork’s valuation to an astonishing
$47 billion, convinced they were supporting the future of work.36
In 2019, however, as W eW ork prepared for its IPO, the facade began to
crack. e public ﬁling exposed the reality behind the rapid growth: a massive
cash burn, with losses exceeding $1.6 billion in 2018 alone. Even more
troubling were governance issues. CEO Adam Neumann wielded outsized
control and had engaged in questionable deals, such as leasing buildings he
owned to W eW ork, raising serious concerns over transparency and conﬂicts of
interest. W hat had seemed like a promising business started to resemble a
house built on a shaky foundation.
e fallout was swift. Investor conﬁdence plummeted, and W eW ork’s
initial $47 billion valuation was slashed. e IPO was called oﬀ, Neumann was
ousted, and SoftBank had to step in with a rescue package. By the time the
dust settled, W eW ork’s valuation had dropped to around $9 billion, and more
than 2,400 employees were laid oﬀ.37
W eW ork’s story serves as a stark reminder of the risks of unchecked growth
without a solid ﬁnancial plan and transparent governance. In the end, a
visionary idea was not enough; without a foundation of trust and
accountability, even the boldest ventures can collapse.

---

## Page 306

Growth at All Costs Only Makes Sense for
Category Leaders
Asana and W eW ork both pursued a growth-at-all-costs model when capital was
cheap. At the time, this approach was widely accepted because nearly everyone
else was doing it. But if everyone else jumps oﬀ a cliﬀ, would you follow?
For me, the growth-at-all-costs model never made much sense. e
primary argument for it is the goal of becoming the category leader with your
product. ink of Slack for internal communication, G oogle W orkspace for
email, or Uber for ride-sharing.
To become a category leader, three factors need to work in your favor:
network eﬀects, strong lock-in, and a brilliant product.
U ber is an excellent example of network eﬀects. e more drivers there are
on the app, the more rides users can book, and in turn, more users attract more
drivers.
G oogle W orkspace, on the other hand, has a strong lock-in eﬀect. Once
your emails, calendars, and ﬁle storage are integrated into one app, the cost of
switching becomes high. Migrating to another client could take weeks.
Finally, having a product that users perceive as brilliant helps secure loyalty.
People love using Slack because it simply works well. Slack also beneﬁts from
network eﬀects, as colleagues are already using it. Plus, Slack Connect allows
connections with external partners, which strengthens its network eﬀect.
How many category leaders can there be in an industry? By deﬁnition, not
many. ere is usually one or a handful of leaders in any given space. is
strategy only works when multiple strategic factors align. However, even with
these three factors in place, success is not guaranteed; operational ineﬃciencies
can still cause failure.
Inefﬁciencies at Scale and RevOps as a
Solution

---

## Page 307

Operational ineﬃciencies at scale are often driven by silos. M isalignment in
data can lead to up to a 30% loss in revenue.38 ese silos are so common that
you don’t need to know anything about RevOps to recognize them. W hen I
speak with people working in D esign, D evelopment, Legal, or on the retail
ﬂoor, they are all more than familiar with silos.
A common response I hear is, “Finally, someone that wants to reduce silos! I
see all the time that M arketing isn’t talking to Sales, and of course, nobody asked
us.” You probably know where this is going if you’ve been reading the book!
RevOps is all about creating alignment, collaboration, and integration
across the organization. Our focus is on eliminating silos and the ineﬃciencies
they create. Growth at all costs without a RevOps mindset can lead to disaster.
Imagine these worst-case scenarios:
•Each department buys its own software, creating massive duplicate
spending.
•Hiring is the answer to every problem, leading to over-hiring.
•D isjointed messaging across the customer journey, resulting in unhappy
clients.
•Lack of accountability, leading to ﬁnger-pointing and blame.
•No focus on conversion points, resulting in ineﬃcient processes.
In building a world-class RevOps organization, you need to address all of
these areas. Now, consider these best-case scenarios:
•Centralized software purchasing decisions focused on value-added
solutions powered by AI.
•Strategic hiring decisions that complement team and AI resources.
•U niﬁed messaging across the customer journey, resulting in satisﬁed
clients.
•Clear accountability that fosters collaboration and productivity.
•An intense focus on conversion points, reducing ineﬃcient processes.

---

## Page 308

W hich approach do you think Finance is going to prefer? I’ve been
fortunate to work with CFOs who prioritize proﬁtability over growth at all
costs. ese best-case scenarios not only help you reach proﬁtability but also
boost it. at’s a strong signal to any investor.
Board Reporting
Board reporting is essential for driving strategic decisions at the highest level.
ese reports typically provide a comprehensive view of a company’s
performance, highlighting key metrics, forecasts, and challenges. Reports to the
board are usually sent periodically, most commonly as quarterly board reports
and a summary of the ﬁscal year.
e RevOps function plays a signiﬁcant role in shaping what gets reported
to the board by aligning cross-functional teams and oﬀering a holistic view of
the G T M  strategy’s eﬀectiveness. RevOps ensures that data shared with the
board is accurate, meaningful, and action-oriented and they focus on growth
levers that are most relevant to the company’s strategic objectives.
In board reports, Finance and RevOps ensure that key metrics like M RR,
CLT V, and CAC are represented to give the board a clear view of revenue
health, growth potential, and the eﬃciency of sales and marketing eﬀorts.
M ore importantly, RevOps can extend beyond current performance
metrics. RevOps provides forecasts on revenue growth, customer retention, and
sales pipeline progression, helping the board understand potential future
revenue streams, identify risks, and evaluate the scalability of current processes.
is strategic foresight enables the board to make informed decisions about
resource allocation and growth strategies.
A further key aspect is aligning ﬁnancial data with operational data. For
instance, how does customer satisfaction impact churn? How does the sales
cycle impact revenue? How do website visits inﬂuence pipeline generation?
RevOps helps paint an aligned picture of operational and ﬁnancial data.
Finally, both a brilliant RevOps and a great ﬁnance person will tell you the
same thing: while we cannot change the facts, we can control the narrative

---

## Page 309

around them. It’s about framing a compelling story within the business
context. For example, which statement would an investor prefer?
“W e had a 15% churn because the product features did not meet the
expectations of users.”
Or,
“Last year, w e pivoted our product to attract a new  audience, w hich helped
grow revenue by 55%. As a result of this pivot, w e saw  a 15% churn among clients
who w ere not aligned with the new  direction. H ow ever, the 15% churn only
represented 9% of revenue. W e are conﬁdent the new  direction w ill attract higher-
paying clients.”
Both statements are factually accurate, yet the second provides the context
to make it a compelling story.
How Venture Capital Drives the Swing
Venture Capital (VC) ﬁrms embrace RevOps and appreciate its main premise
of fostering more predictable revenue growth. VC ﬁrms are focused on
achieving a successful exit with substantial returns. Knowing when to expect
those returns is crucial. is timing inﬂuences their decisions on further
investments.
Predictable revenue growth is a major advantage, but VCs also value the
operational eﬃciencies that RevOps brings. In RevOps, our goal is to invest
wisely and make resourceful decisions to drive revenue without tolerating
waste. is resourcefulness is highly valued by VC-backed companies.
Being resourceful does not slow us down. In fact, RevOps is about building
revenue engines that can accelerate at any speed that’s required. ink of
RevOps as the support crew of a Formula 1 team. e person in the driver’s
seat (G T M  stakeholders) can only win the race if the support crew (RevOps)
has created an environment optimized for success.
VCs are known for their aggressive growth targets and typically aim to
multiply their investment by a factor of 10 or even up to 100 when entering an
early seed round. To achieve this, they are willing to take risks and set
ambitious goals for the company. RevOps plays a key role in not only tracking

---

## Page 310

essential KP Is like M RR, CAC, and CLT V but also ﬁnding ways to improve
each. As we’ve discussed throughout this book, this is where RevOps excels.
The Eight Roads Perspective: RevOps as a
Competitive Advantage
By Kevin Kimber and M ichael Treskow, Partners at Eight Roads
Eight Roads Ventures is a global venture ﬁrm that was founded over 50
years ago in Boston and today manages more than $11 billion in assets
across a global portfolio of over 400 companies. As a scale-up stage investor
(Series B, give or take a round), Eight Roads provides founders with access
to a value-add team of experienced executives who advise on key areas to
accelerate growth and operational excellence.
K evin K imber, a key member of the Eight Roads expert network, brings
extensive experience in B2B sales and revenue strategy. Prior to Eight Roads,
K evin was part of the founding team at ServiceNow, helping scale the
company from less than $1 million in ARR to $140 million ARR and a $4
billion IPO on the NYSE. He also led SAP’s cloud business and served as
CEO of Rimilia, where he scaled the company post-founder CEO and
successfully exited the business. K evin works closely with the Eight Roads
portfolio companies to optimize their sales and RevOps functions, helping
them scale eﬀectively.
Speaking with K evin, he said, “rough our extensive experience working
with B2B scale-ups, we have observed that RevOps plays a crucial role in
helping companies navigate the complexities of scaling eﬃciently. RevOps is
instrumental in aligning Sales, M arketing, and Customer Success functions
to drive predictable growth and long-term success. However, when we
invest, RevOps
is often underdeveloped or entirely absent. M any scale-ups operate with
siloed revenue functions, leading to fragmented processes, ineﬃciencies, and

---

## Page 311

limited data visibility. is lack of integration often results in suboptimal
decision-making, inconsistent forecasting, and missed revenue
opportunities. W ithout a structured RevOps function, companies may
struggle to build the operational foundation necessary for sustained, scalable
growth.”
Prior to Series B, companies are often focused on establishing the fair value
of their software and reﬁning their pricing strategy. Post-Series B, as G T M
motions become more structured, RevOps can then play a crucial role in
ensuring scalable and repeatable revenue operations. Our experience has
shown that the strategic introduction of RevOps is most eﬀective around
the Series B stage. At this point, companies begin transitioning from
founder-led sales to a more structured, repeatable revenue engine. As
revenue growth becomes more complex, a dedicated RevOps function can
provide the necessary framework to streamline operations, enhance data-
driven decision-making, and improve G T M  execution. Implementing
RevOps early allows companies to proactively address operational
ineﬃciencies before they become barriers to scale.
RevOps serves as the backbone of revenue-generating functions, ensuring
operational excellence and cross-functional alignment. It plays a crucial role
in standardizing and automating lead-to-revenue workﬂows, creating a
single source of truth for revenue data, and managing CRM  systems,
automation platforms, and analytics tools to enhance productivity. RevOps
also bridges the gap between Sales, M arketing, and Customer Success,
driving a more cohesive G T M  strategy. G iven its impact on revenue
performance, RevOps typically falls under the CRO or, in some cases, the
COO, reﬂecting its strategic signiﬁcance within the organization.
At Eight Roads, we view RevOps as a critical enabler of scale. Companies
that prioritize RevOps early in their growth journey gain a competitive edge
by improving sales eﬃciency, enhancing forecasting accuracy, and increasing
customer lifetime value. By embedding RevOps as a core pillar of their
growth strategy, portfolio companies have been able to optimize revenue
operations, maximize sales productivity, and create a scalable foundation for

---

## Page 312

long-term success. As the venture ecosystem continues to evolve, Eight
Roads is conﬁdent that RevOps will remain a cornerstone of scalable
growth. By embracing operational excellence alongside product innovation,
founders can position their businesses for accelerated success and long-term
market leadership.
RevOps in Private Equity Firm s
e characteristics we see in VC-backed companies also apply to Private
Equity(PE)-backed companies, with one key diﬀerence: the investment
horizon. W hile VCs invest with an expected exit in 7 to 10 years, PE ﬁrms
often aim to exit within 3 to 5 years. is compressed timeline requires PE-
backed companies to achieve both growth and proﬁtability quickly, which
shifts RevOps priorities.
For PE-backed companies, the focus is on proﬁtability and operational
excellence. Aggressive cost-cutting, restructuring, mergers, and acquisitions are
common strategies. e mandate is to maximize EBIT D A in preparation for
an exit.
Another key focus is cash ﬂow, which is essential for servicing debt or
ﬁnancing additional investments. In a PE context, RevOps focuses on
maximizing cash ﬂow and reducing waste, aligning with the PE ﬁrm’s emphasis
on liquidity and ﬁnancial stability. is approach contrasts with the growth-at-
all-costs mentality, as PE ﬁrms are generally more risk-averse than VC funds.
e exit strategies for each also diﬀer. VCs typically look for an IPO or a
sale to a larger company, while PE ﬁrms often target strategic buyers or
secondary buyouts by another PE ﬁrm.

RevOps plays a valuable role in both VC-backed or PE-backed company
and while the focus diﬀers, the end of the growth-at-all-costs era has led VCs
to adopt a slightly more PE-like approach, particularly with an increased
emphasis on eﬃciency.
However, the higher risk appetite of VC ﬁrms remains.
RevOps in Publically Listed Com panies
In publicly listed companies, the focus of RevOps shifts signiﬁcantly toward
ensuring predictable growth. Every public action, whether voluntary or
involuntary, can impact the stock price.
e good news is that companies with RevOps are well-positioned to
achieve predictable revenue growth due to their holistic approach, emphasis on
alignment, and tools like the growth formula. Our Revenue W izards research
shows that S&P 500 companies outperform their peers without RevOp with
an average 14.14% higher stock price, 225.52% greater market capitalization,
and 176.69% higher EBIT D A.39

---

## Page 314

Public companies have to follow strict rules around ﬁnancial reporting and
compliance, so it’s important for RevOps to keep sales, marketing, and
customer data clean, accurate, and easy to audit, especially with regulations like
Sarbanes-Oxley (SOX) in play. On top of that, there’s extra pressure when it
comes to data security and privacy. RevOps plays a key role in making sure
customer data is handled safely and stays in line with standards like G D PR and
CCPA, which really highlights just how critical good data quality is.
All these factors contribute to more mature reporting requirements, as
quarterly and annual reports can signiﬁcantly inﬂuence stock prices.
Balancing Finance and RevOps
W hat is your risk appetite?
Chances are, if you work in RevOps, it’s at the higher end of the spectrum
compared to your colleagues in Finance. Its focus is on risk management, with
the primary goal of minimizing a company’s ﬁnancial risks. is cautious
approach often contrasts with G T M  strategies, where taking calculated chances
is essential.
In business, you sometimes need to take a leap and hope for the best
outcome. You can’t always wait for data to make a decision. Sometimes, there
isn’t any data to rely on. All it takes is one competitor to make that leap of faith
and seize an opportunity, potentially resulting in lost revenue or even damage
to market positioning if we hesitate.
In this section, we’ll explore techniques for ﬁnding a balance in risk-taking
between Finance, RevOps, and the rest of the GT M  teams.
Balancing Data with Intuition
Finance and RevOps both share a love for data, yet data can often be diﬃcult
to obtain. ere will be times when you need to make decisions based on
limited information, whether because of time constraints or incomplete data.
In some situations, you may have data pointing in one direction while your
intuition suggests the opposite.

---

## Page 315

For example, a thorough vendor selection process might indicate that we
should choose Vendor A, but our intuition favors Vendor B, even though
Vendor B has a higher purchase price and lower ROI. Finance would likely
choose Vendor A, while an experienced business leader might opt for Vendor
B.
W ho should we listen to?
In my experience, I would favor Vendor B. Intuition can be diﬃcult to
rationalize, but from personal experience, I have found that ignoring it often
leads to disappointment. You might be surprised to read about intuition in a
Finance chapter, but this is precisely the balance you need when working with
the Finance team.
Executive Sponsor to Balance KPI Overload
In Finance, it can be all too easy to focus heavily on numbers and lose sight of
the growth strategy. If Finance were the only stakeholder, in this extreme case,
only conservative decisions would be made based on the right KP Is. is
approach might work in a world with perfect information, but in reality,
business decisions require a balance.
Executives like the CRO, CM O, or CEO play a crucial role in balancing
KP I-driven insights with healthy risk-taking. is doesn’t mean that executives
take risks without considering facts; rather, they understand they may not have
complete data and recognize that speed is often more important. is drives
them to take calculated risks.
Such risk-taking is essential in business, and an executive sponsor is needed
to balance the Finance perspective.
Short-Term vs. Long-Term  Focus
Finance tends to have a short-term focus, often looking only a few quarters
ahead and rarely considering years into the future. is focus can be limiting,
as certain strategic projects require years to fully realize their beneﬁts, such as
building a community for sales, establishing a brand, or developing a channel

---

## Page 316

strategy. Finance may ﬁnd it challenging to see the long-term value of these
initiatives.
is is where the power of RevOps comes into play. You can provide
improved models to forecast revenue ﬂows over multiple quarters—or even
years—to support Finance in long-term planning.
Socratic Reﬂections
Finance and RevOps drive impact across the whole organization.
•How can you improve the predictability of your work so that it beneﬁts
Finance?
•Are there initiatives you could implement in the coming months that
would positively impact your OpEx?
•Are you tracking the right KPIs?
•W hat operational data is impacting your ﬁnance KPIs?
•Is your board aware of the operational data point from the previous
questions? If not, would it beneﬁt the board to understand the
operational data?
•List ﬁve risks you see when working with your Finance department. How
can you mitigate those?

CHAPTER 10
THE NEXT ARC – AI’S ROLE
IN REVOPS
“ere is nothing like a dream to create the future.”
Victor H ugo
Imagine it’s the year 2030. You’re looking inside the operations of U mbrella AI
Corp, a SaaS company that has fully embraced AI.
e website of U mbrella AI Corp. was built by AI and is AI-enhanced. It
automatically understands if it’s talking to a VP of Sales based in G ermany or a
Head of M arketing in the U S. Based on the persona, the AI will remodel its
content to suit the persona.
An AI bot will start a chat with the prospect in their preferred language. By
now, the chatbot knows the identity of the prospects. It’s talking to Anna, the
VP of Sales from an enterprise in Germany.
e AI adjusts its style to ﬁt the personality type of Anna. It’s not hard for
the AI, as it considers all of her public social posts and enriches her customer
record with third-party data. It notices that she prefers calling over chatting.
So, they ask for a quick call to provide her with all the information she needs.
She has the choice to book an AI-assisted demo or with a human colleague.
Anna decides to book a demo with a human. e AI expected that already, as
Anna is labeled as “old fashioned,” but the AI still tries to oﬀer an AI-assisted
demo to lower costs.

---

## Page 318

Once the call is over, the AI creates a new record in the CRM  with the call
summary. It scores Anna and compares her with similar prospects that
U mbrella AI Corp. interacted with in the past. Anna gets assigned to
Samantha from Sales, who’s been the most successful in closing similar
companies and personality types.
Samantha is an Account M anager at U mbrella AI Corp. and supports
clients from beginning to end. In the past, Account M anagers had a fake
“manager” title, as they never managed anyone. But G T M  Account M anagers
at Umbrella AI Corp. are true to their title—they manage diﬀerent AIs.
Samantha is assisted by a team of AI assistants. Ava is her BD R who does
prospecting. Ava is trained in Samantha’s writing style and has adopted her
tonality for cold calling. For every booked meeting, Ava updates Samantha
about the key steps so she can have the “real meeting” with the client.
Samantha does a practice run with Ava in the metaverse to make the real
client call more eﬀective. Ava takes on the persona of the prospect, Anna. As
the saying goes, practice makes perfect. W ith all of this preparation, Anna is
87% certain to close the deal. At least that is what Ava says, and she’s never
wrong.
In the meantime, Albus, another AI, supports her across the sales journey,
making sure everything is correctly logged into the CRM . He does continuous
account research, schedules meetings and responds to RFPs. Eventually, Albus
will give Samantha a sign to ring the bell—a nostalgic relic from the pre-AI
time to celebrate a win (some traditions never die!).
e deal is won! Samantha checks with Alpha about onboarding Anna into
the software; he’s in charge of ensuring the platform is integrated into the
client’s systems. Alpha educates Samantha continuously on product
development. In the past, U mbrella AI Corp. had solution consultants do that,
but they are obsolete with Alpha.
Alpha is also client-facing. He provides clients with continuous live support
and is a bridge to the Product team and their AIs. He provides them with
continuous feedback on how to improve the product. In the past, SaaS
companies avoided custom development or priced them extremely high. But

---

## Page 319

now, custom development is part of the product and the product never stops
improving.
Once the day is over (yes, this all happened in one workday!), Samantha
reﬂects on how 2025 was only ﬁve years ago. U mbrella AI Corp. would have
needed 2,000 people to service the same number of customers it does now,
with just 200 people and 800 AI avatars.
U mbrella AI Corp. might sound like science ﬁction, but I bet you noticed
some familiar technology, such as call recording, automated logging of calls, or
AI mock calls. e story is infused with technologies that are currently
available and those technologies that will soon mature:
•AI website builders.
•Intent data to uncover invisible website visits.
•Live-tailored website that adjusts to website visitors.
•AI scoring models.
•AI chatbots.
•AI phone calls.
•AI transcripts.
•Automated record update.
•AI enablement and trading.
None of the capabilities in the U mbrella AI Corp. are impossible; you can
bet a startup on this planet is already working on making it a reality. e main
limitations are computer and model accuracy. But it’s almost certain this
technology will come.
e question is: when will these technologies arrive and be adopted by the
market?
M y belief is that the coming ﬁve years will be a critical time for the G T M
tech space (it’s 2025 at the time of this writing). I alluded that a company of
200 people that embraces AI could do as much as a company with 2,000
employees. I do believe the future will play out in this direction. W hat impacts

---

## Page 320

this will have on the labor market and society as a whole is unclear and goes
beyond the scope of the book.
Time will tell.
I’ve stressed the importance of AI throughout this book because it is such a
transformative technology. For anyone in RevOps, being able to understand
and deploy AI will be a key job requirement.
It’s now everywhere and changing how G T M  and RevOps teams work.
Ninety percent of marketers said that AI helps them to work faster and
automate mundane tasks, giving them time to work on activities they enjoy
more. At the same time, the same marketers had more work than ever before.40
AI is raising the bar.
It allows us to do more with less. It’s an interesting appeal for G T M  teams and
stakeholders. M issing the AI train is not an option. Companies that adopt AI
are outperforming companies that don’t. e speed of innovation is
unprecedented; so is the speed of adoption.
e deployment of AI tools will diﬀer per stage of companies. Startups will
be more agile and adopt AI tools faster. e risk of experimentation isn’t big
since enterprises will be more cautious as their existing processes are more
complex. ere is less room for experimentation to roll out an AI tool
organization-wide.
W hat follows next is an overview of the diﬀerent branches of AI, reasons
for AI’s rapid spread, and how best to prepare your company for AI
deployment.
Exploring the AI Spectrum : Branches of
Artiﬁcial Intelligence
ere are diﬀerent branches of AI, and not all of them are relevant to G T M
and RevOps. Here’s an overview of the most important ones for these groups.
Machine Learning

---

## Page 321

M achine learning is the foundation of AI. It’s all about creating computer
programs that can improve, and it does so by learning from data. For example,
a CRM  might recommend you a lead that is “hotter” than others using
machine learning techniques.
W ithin machine learning, there is a subset called deep learning. It tries to
work like the human brain. It uses artiﬁcial neural networks to learn from a lot
of data with little human intervention.
In relation, there is also reinforcement learning. is involves an award for
the computer if it gets the answer right. In this method, the computer program
can improve on its own in a given task.
M achine learning, deep learning, and reinforcement learning are the
foundation on which most AI technologies are built.
Natural Language Processing
Natural Language Processing (NLP) lets computers understand and use human
language. AI models can read textual data, understand its meaning, write text,
and converse with people. Tools like ChatG PT  are NLP in action.
NLP unlocks many use cases:
•Sentiment analysis (understand the sentiment of a text.)
•Language translation
•Text summarization
•Named entity recognition (such as names, businesses, and locations)
•Question answering
•Information extraction
•Text classiﬁcation
•Text generation
•Speech recognition and voice assistants
•Language modeling (predict language, spell checking)
•D ocument analysis and search

---

## Page 322

•Text-to-speech and speech-to-text conversion
From this list, you can already imagine that NLP is the most used for
G T M  and RevOps. M ost SaaS businesses build AI tools using NLP, and most
deploy these tools. at’s no surprise, as language is how humans
communicate.
Computer Vision
Computer vision aims to enable machines to understand and interpret visual
information from images or videos. For example, an AI can analyze the
recording of a sales video call and understand the sentiment of the buyer based
on facial expressions. But not only the sentiment, it can also be used to check
the truthfulness of statements. is makes every sales rep an expert in lie
detection.
Generative AI
G enerative AI combines most of the above models and can produce various
types of content, including text, imagery, audio and synthetic data. is is your
ChatG PT, Bard, or Claud. Anyone can interact with G enerative AI, and it
feels “human.”
No wonder it is the most widespread AI format. In 2023, M cKinsey
published a report called “G enerative AI Breakthrough.” It shows that, on
average, already 80% of the workforce uses generative AI tools.41 is number
will easily reach 100%, and the use cases will spread more across businesses.
W hy AI Shifts the Swing
ere are many reasons for AI adoption, ranging from individual performance
to company-wide beneﬁts such as revenue growth or cost reduction.

---

## Page 323

Productivity Gains
AI is boosting the productivity of G T M  teams and skyrocketing individuals’
performance. Here are a few use cases per function:
M arketing
•Content research can be semi-to-fully automated.
•Content writing can be close to fully automated.
•Campaign planning is semi-automated.
•Predictive prospect scoring can be powered by AI.
•Social media listening can be fully automated.
Sales
•Account research can be fully automated.
•Email writing is fully automated.
•M ore personalization in outreach at scale.
•CRM  can be updated automatically.
•Accurate forecasting with AI.
•Personalized coaching and guidance.
•Automated RFP responses.
Custom er Success
•AI Chatbots.
•Automate knowledge base creation.
•AI rooting of support requests.
•M ulti-language support (text, video & call).
•Virtual assistants.
•Identify at-risk customers.
•Identify upsell opportunities.
